Causality and Created Things

The Agent Must Be a Free Chooser
Concludes the second proof against infinite regress and establishes the agent's free choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's agent cannot be a necessary cause and must be a free chooser, based on the argument from particularization.

Core Idea
A non-physical necessary cause would necessitate uniformity; diversity proves choice.

This passage completes the second proof against infinite regress by showing that the cause of the world's diverse attributes cannot be a mindless necessary cause. Since a non-physical cause has equal relation to all bodies, if it necessitated, all bodies would receive the same attributes. But we observe specific attributes in specific bodies, so the cause must be a free agent that chooses.

Guided Path
1
Recall the four divisions of infinite regress
The author refers back to earlier divisions. Understand that infinite regress all at once or sequentially without first has been rejected. Now the fourth division is examined.
2
Follow the argument against a necessary cause
Read the paragraph where the author argues that if the determinant is non-physical, it must be either necessary or free. Then see why necessary is eliminated: equal relation to all bodies would produce uniform attributes.
Focus on the key terms 'qawābil' (recipients) and 'muʾaththir' (efficient cause).
3
Conclude that the agent is a free chooser
When the necessary cause is shown impossible, the remaining possibility is a free agent. The author concludes that the world's agent is fāʿil mukhtār. Also note the remark about the pivot of the whole argument.
This conclusion is a cornerstone of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك التسلسل إن وقع دفعة واحدة فهو باطل. كما بيناه ، وإن وقع بحيث يكون كل حادث مسبوقاً بحادث آخر ، لا إلى أول . فقد أبطلناه أيضاً . والقسم الرابع أيضاً باطل . لأن ذلك المقتضى إن كان جسماً أو جسمانياً، عاد التقسيم فيه، وهو أنه كيف اختص ذلك الجسم بالصفة التي لأجلها صار مقتضياً لهذه الأحكام ، وإن لم يكن جسماً ولا جسمانياً ( فهو إما أن يكون موجباً أو مختاراً ، ولا جائز أن يكون موجباً ، لأنه لما لم يكن جسماً ولا جسمانياً ) (١) لم يكن مختصاً بشيء من الأجسام بالقرب منه أو بالبعد منه ، وإذا كانت نسبته إلى الكل واحدة ، وكانت الأجسام بأسرها متساوية في قبول هذه الصفات ، فحينئذ لا يكون حصول الصفة المعينة في بعض الأجسام أولى من حصولها في سائر الأجسام (۲) ، لأن القوابل بأسرها متساوية في القبول والمؤثر ، والنسبة إلى الكل على السوية . فوجب التشابه المذكور ، وحيث لم يحصل علمنا أن القول (٣). بالموجب باطل . وإذا بطل هذا يثبت أن فاعل العالم فاعل مختار ، لا علة موجبة بالذات ، واعلم أن مدار هذه الحجة ) ومدار الحجة ) (٤) التي قبلها على القول ببطلان حوادث لا أول لها
That infinite regress, if it occurs all at once, is invalid, as we have shown, and if it occurs such that every event is preceded by another event, without a first, we have also refuted it. And the fourth division is also invalid, because that determinant (al-muqtaḍā) if it is a body or corporeal, the division recurs in it, namely how that body is particularized by the attribute due to which it becomes a determinant for these judgments. And if it is not a body nor corporeal (it is either a necessitating cause (mūjib) or a free agent (mukhtār), and it cannot be a necessitating cause, because since it is not a body nor corporeal) (١) it is not particularized by anything of the bodies by nearness or distance from it. And when its relation to the whole is one, and the bodies in their entirety are equal in accepting these attributes, then the occurrence of a specific attribute in some bodies is not more likely than its occurrence in the rest of the bodies (۲), because the recipients in their entirety are equal in reception, and the efficient cause (al-muʾaththir) and the relation to the whole are equal. So the mentioned similarity is necessary, and since it does not occur, we know that the statement of the necessitating cause is false. And when this is false, it is established that the agent of the world is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), not a self-necessitating cause (ʿilla mūjiba bi-dhātihā). And know that the pivot of this argument (and the pivot of the argument) (٤) preceding it is upon the statement of the invalidity of events without a beginning.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage completes the second proof against infinite regress and establishes that the world's agent must be a free chooser. The author first summarizes the rejection of infinite regress (tasalsul) whether it occurs all at once or sequentially without a first. Then he addresses the fourth division: if the determinant (al-muqtaḍā) of the attributes in bodies is a body or corporeal, the same problem of particularization recurs. If it is neither body nor corporeal, then it is either a necessary cause (mūjib) or a free agent (mukhtār). It cannot be a necessary cause because a non-physical entity has no special relation of nearness or distance to any body; its relation to all bodies is uniform. Since all bodies are equally receptive, if the cause were necessitating, every body would equally receive the same attributes. But we observe specificity (e.g., fire burns but water does not), so the cause must be a free agent that chooses which body receives which attribute. The author concludes that the world's agent is a free agent (fāʿil mukhtār), not a self-necessitating cause. Finally, he notes that this proof and the preceding one rest on the rejection of an infinite series of events without a beginning (an early argument for the necessity of a first cause).

Key Terms
تسلسل (tasalsul)
مقتضى (muqtaḍā)
موجب (mūjib)
مختار (mukhtār)
علة موجبة بالذات (ʿilla mūjiba bi-dhātihā)
فاعل مختار (fāʿil mukhtār)
قوابل (qawābil)
حوادث لا أول لها (ḥawādith lā awwala lahā)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies the possibility of a non-physical necessary cause altogether.
Correction: It denies that a non-physical necessary cause can necessitate specific attributes in some bodies over others without a choice; it does not deny the existence of non-physical necessary causes in principle.

Checkpoint
Why does the passage argue that the world's agent cannot be a necessary cause (mūjib)?
A) Because it would lead to an infinite regress of causes.
B) Because a non-physical agent has equal relation to all bodies, so if it necessitated, all bodies would be equal in receiving attributes, which they are not.
C) Because the agent must be a body to be a necessary cause.
D) Because the attributes of bodies are impossible for a necessary cause.

Key Takeaway
The diversity in the world necessitates a free agent who chooses which bodies receive which attributes, rather than a blind necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the unique qualities of different people, animals, and things around you, do you truly see them as the deliberate choice of a free Creator, or have you become so accustomed to diversity that you treat it as a mere accident? How can you train your heart to see each difference as a mark of His choice?
?
The argument proves that God must be a free agent who chooses. But do you live as if your own choices matter? Or do you fall into thinking that everything is already determined by nature or fate? What would change in your daily decisions if you truly believed that your Creator deeply cares about which attributes He gives to each thing?
?
If you truly believed that every good quality you have is a specific gift chosen for you by a free and loving Agent, how would your gratitude and reliance on Him be different? Start right now: name one attribute you have and say to yourself, 'This is a deliberate gift from the One who chose it for me.'

The Third Proof: Against the Agent Being a Necessary Cause
Presents the third proof that the world's agent cannot be a necessary cause and must be a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the world's agent cannot be a necessary cause and must be a free agent, based on the impossibility of both one and multiple effects from a simple cause.

Core Idea
A necessary cause cannot produce the world's diversity without contradiction; only a free agent can.

This lesson presents the third proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah, which argues that the world's efficient cause cannot be a self-necessitating cause (mūjib bi-dhātih). Instead, it must be an agent by choice (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār). The argument works by dividing the possibilities and showing each leads to absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once and its translation. Notice the logical structure: division into two cases, then an objection and answer (though incomplete).
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two divisions
The proof begins by dividing the possibilities: if the agent were a necessary cause, its effect would be either one or more than one. Both are shown impossible.
3
Analyze the first case: effect is one
Read why one effect leads to an infinite chain of causation among particles, which is false by necessity. Understand the argument that the effect of a simple cause must be one, then that effect would cause another, and so on.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Analyze the second case: effect is more than one
Read the argument that a simple cause cannot produce two distinct effects. The author gives three scenarios: the concepts of causing each effect could be constitutive (making the cause composite) or external (leading to regress), etc.
This part uses technical terms like māhiyya and tasalsul.
5
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection that the effect might be one, and the beginning of the answer. Note that the answer is incomplete in this excerpt, but it seems to deny that a single effect would lead to the infinite chain because the effect itself has aspects that prevent it.
The answer is cut off; we rely on the commentary for completion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : لو كان المؤثر في العالم موجباً) بالذات ، لكان إما أن يكون معلوله واحداً ، وإما أن يكون أكثر من واحد . والقسمان باطلان ، فالقول بالموجب باطل . أما الخصر فظاهر . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله شيئاً واحداً . ذلك لأنه لما وجب أن يكون معلول الواحد واحداً فقط . فمعلول ذلك الواحد أيضاً يجب أن يكون واحداً . وهلم جرا إلى آخر المراتب . فيلزم أن لا يوجد في هذا العالم شيئاً من قطرات الماء ، وذرات الهباءات ، إلا . ويكون (٦) أحدهما علة للآخر ، والآخر معلولا له ، ومعلوم أن ذلك باطل . لأنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن هذه الذرة ليست علة لتلك الذرة الأخرى ولا بالعكس . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله أكثر من واحد ، لأن هذا متفق عليه بين الفلاسفة المفهوم وأيضاً : لو فرضنا ذلك لكان مفهوم أنه صدر عنه هذا المعلول ، مغاير أنه صدر عنه المعلول الثاني، وهذان المفهومان إن كانا مقومين لتلك العلة ، لزم كونها مركبة . وقد فرضناها بسيطة هذا خلف . وإن كانا خارجين عنها ، فكل صفة خارجة عن الماهية لاحقة بها فهي ممكنة لذاتها ، واجبة بتلك الماهية. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول في كيفية كون تلك الماهية علة لذينك المفهومين الخارجين . ويفضي ذلك إلى التسلسل، وهو محال . وإن كان أحدهما داخلا في الماهية ، والآخر خارجاً عنها فحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الماهية مركبة ، لأن الداخل في الماهية جزء لتلك الماهية ، وكل ماهية لها جزء فهي مركبة . وأيضاً : معلول الماهية ليس إلا ذلك الواحد ، فثبت (۱) أن البسيط الحق يمتنع أن يكون علة المعلولين معاً ، فيثبت أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات لكان إما أن يكون معلوله واحداً أو أكثر من واحد ، وثبت فساد القسمين . فيمتنع كونه موجباً بالذات ، ويبقى أن يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار. فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : معلوله شيء واحد فقط ؟ قلنا قوله : لو كان كذلك لكان معلول ذلك الواحد (٢) أيضاً واحداً، وحينئذ يلزم المحال . قلنا : لا نسلم أنه لو كان معلول ذات الله واحداً ، لزم أن يكون معلول ذلك المعلول أيضاً واحداً. وبيانه : هو أن ذلك المعلول لا بد وأن يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته . أو لا بد أن يكون وجوده صادراً تلك العلة . فحصل هناك أمور ثلاثة : إمكانه ، ووجوده ، ووجوبه عن
The third proof: If the efficient cause in the world were a self-necessitating cause (mūjib bi-dhātih), then its effect would either be one or more than one. Both divisions are false, so the statement of the necessitating cause is false. As for the division [of being one], it is apparent. And we say: It is impossible that its effect be one thing. That is because since it is necessary that the effect of the one is only one, then the effect of that one must also be one, and so on to the end of the ranks. Thus it would follow that there would not exist in this world any of the drops of water or atoms of dust except that one would be the cause of the other and the other its effect, and it is known that this is false. For we know necessarily that this atom is not the cause of that other atom nor vice versa. And we say: It is impossible that its effect be more than one, because this is agreed upon among the philosophers. Also: If we suppose that, then the concept that this effect emanated from it is different from the concept that the second effect emanated from it. If these two concepts are constitutive of that cause, then it would be composite, and we have supposed it simple; this is contradictory. If they are external to it, then every attribute external to the essence (māhiyya) and attached to it is possible in itself and necessary by that essence. Then the first division returns concerning how that essence is a cause for those two external concepts. And that leads to infinite regress, which is impossible. And if one of them is internal to the essence and the other external, then the essence would be composite, because the internal is a part of that essence, and every essence having a part is composite. Also, the effect of the essence is nothing but that one [which is internal]. So it is established that the truly simple (al-basīṭ al-ḥaqq) cannot be a cause for two effects together. Thus it is established that if the Exalted were a self-necessitating cause, its effect would be either one or more than one, and both divisions are proven false. So it is impossible that He is a self-necessitating cause, and it remains that He is an agent by choice (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār). For if it is said: Why is it not permissible to say that His effect is only one thing? We say: His saying: If it were so, then the effect of that one would also be one, and then an impossibility follows. We say: We do not concede that if the effect of God's essence were one, then the effect of that effect would also be one. Its explanation: That effect must be possible in itself, or its existence must emanate from that cause. So three things arise: its possibility, its existence, and its necessity from...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof that the world's agent cannot be a necessary cause (mūjib) but must be a free agent (mukhtār). The proof proceeds by dividing the possibilities: if the agent were a necessary cause, its effect would be either one or more than one. Both options are shown to be impossible. First, if the effect is one, then since the effect of a simple cause must be one, that effect itself would cause another one, and so on, leading to an infinite chain where every particle is causally linked to another. But we know by necessity that not every particle is the cause of another (e.g., two separate atoms are not causally related). Thus the effect cannot be one. Second, if the effect is more than one, the author invokes a philosophical consensus that a simple cause cannot produce two distinct effects directly. He then provides a logical argument: the concept of causing the first effect is different from the concept of causing the second. If these concepts are constitutive of the cause, the cause would be composite (contradicting its simplicity). If they are external to its essence, they are contingent attributes needing a cause, leading to an infinite regress. If one concept is internal and the other external, then the essence is composite (having parts). Therefore, a simple cause cannot have multiple effects. Since both possibilities fail, the agent cannot be a necessary cause; it must be a free agent. The passage then addresses an objection: why cannot we say the effect is one? The objection argues that if it is one, its effect would also be one, leading to an infinite chain. But the objection's answer (cut off) seems to deny that the effect of a single effect must be one, explaining that the effect itself has three aspects: possibility, existence, and necessity from the cause, which may prevent the chain. However, the text is incomplete.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات (mūjib bi-dhātih)
معلول (maʿlūl)
واحد (wāḥid)
بسيط (basīṭ)
مركب (murakkab)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
فاعل بالاختيار (fāʿil bi-l-ikhtiyār)
ماهية (māhiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof denies that God can create multiple effects.
Correction: The proof does not deny that God can create multiple effects; it denies that a necessary cause (by essence) can produce more than one effect directly without composition or external attributes. God as a free agent can create multiple effects by will.

Checkpoint
According to the third proof, why cannot the effect of a necessary cause be one?
A) Because that would make the cause composite.
B) Because that would require the effect to cause another effect, leading to an infinite chain of cause-effect among particles.
C) Because the philosophers unanimously denied it.
D) Because the effect must be multiple.

Key Takeaway
The world's agent cannot be a self-necessitating cause; it must be a free agent because a simple necessary cause cannot produce the observed multiplicity without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessary cause, your prayers would be pointless. In what area of your life do you still act as if God's actions are automatic or predictable, rather than the free choice of the All-Wise? What would it look like to truly live in light of His freedom today?
?
The proof shows that the diversity in the world—every drop of water and atom of dust—is the result of God's free choice. Does your heart respond with gratitude when you see variety in your circumstances, or do you resent that God chose differently than you would have? How can you train yourself to thank Him for the unique situations He freely gives you?
?
The distinction between a necessary cause and a free agent is not just academic. In your moment of deepest trial, do you cry out to a personal, free Chooser or to an impersonal force? What one practical change can you make this week to remind yourself that God chooses to hear you?

Causality and Created Things

8 min
Proof That a Simple Cause Cannot Produce Two Effects
The author proves that a simple essence cannot cause two distinct effects, concluding God acts by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the proof that a simple essence cannot cause two distinct effects and why this leads to affirming that God acts by choice.

Core Idea
A simple cause can only have one effect; otherwise it becomes composite.

This passage presents a rational argument from the philosophers that a truly simple being cannot be the cause of two different effects. The author uses this to prove that God, being absolutely simple, cannot be a necessary cause (which would necessitate a single effect) but must be a free agent. Let's walk through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the initial premise
The author begins with an obvious fact: one atom is not the cause of another atom. This sets the stage for the argument about causality and simplicity.
2
Grasp the philosophical consensus
The author notes that philosophers agree a simple cause cannot have more than one effect. This is a key premise for the proof.
3
Follow the three-case analysis
The proof examines three possibilities if a simple cause had two effects: (1) the concepts of the effects are constitutive of the cause, (2) they are external, (3) one internal and one external. Each leads to contradiction or infinite regress.
Focus on how each case undermines simplicity.
4
Conclude the impossibility
Since all cases are impossible, a simple cause cannot have two effects. Therefore, if God were a necessary cause, His effect would be either one or many, but both are impossible, so He cannot be a necessary cause.
5
Affirm free agency
The only remaining possibility is that God acts by choice (فاعل بالاختيار), not by necessity.
This conclusion aligns with Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لأنا نعلم بالضرورة : أن هذه الذرة ليست علة لتلك الذرة الأخرى ولا بالعكس . وإنما قلنا : إنه يمتنع أن يكون معلوله أكثر من واحد ، لأن هذا متفق عليه بين الفلاسفة . وأيضاً : لو فرضنا ذلك لكان مفهوم أنه صدر عنه هذا المعلول ، مغاير لمفهوم أنه صدر عنه المعلول الثاني، وهذان المفهومان إن كانا مقومين لتلك العلة ، لزم كونها مركبة . وقد فرضناها بسيطة هذا خلف . وإن كانا خارجين عنها ، فكل صفة خارجة عن الماهية لاحقة بها فهي ممكنة لذاتها ، واجبة بتلك الماهية. وحينئذ يعود التقسيم الأول في كيفية كون تلك الماهية علة لذينك المفهومين الخارجين . ويفضي ذلك إلى التسلسل، وهو محال . وإن كان أحدهما داخلا في الماهية ، والآخر خارجاً عنها فحينئذ يلزم كون تلك الماهية مركبة ، لأن الداخل في الماهية جزء لتلك الماهية ، وكل ماهية لها جزء فهي مركبة . وأيضاً : معلول الماهية ليس إلا ذلك الواحد ، فثبت أن البسيط الحق يمتنع أن يكون علة المعلولين معاً ، فيثبت أنه تعالى لو كان موجباً بالذات لكان إما أن يكون معلوله واحداً أو أكثر من واحد ، وثبت فساد القسمين . فيمتنع كونه موجباً بالذات ، ويبقى أن يكون فاعلاً بالاختيار.
Because we know necessarily: that this atom is not the cause of that other atom, nor vice versa. And we said: it is impossible for its effect to be more than one, because this is agreed upon among the philosophers. Also: if we supposed that, then the concept that this effect proceeded from it would be different from the concept that the second effect proceeded from it. If these two concepts are constitutive of that cause, then it would be composite, and we have supposed it simple—this is a contradiction. And if they are external to it, then every attribute external to the essence and attaching to it is possible in itself and necessary by that essence. Then the first division returns regarding how that essence is a cause for those two external concepts, and that leads to regress, which is impossible. And if one of them is internal to the essence and the other external, then that essence would be composite, because what is internal to the essence is a part of that essence, and every essence having a part is composite. Also, the effect of the essence is only that one. So it is established that the truly simple cannot be a cause for two effects together. Hence it is established that if He, exalted, were a necessary cause by essence, then His effect would be either one or more than one, and both possibilities have been proven false. Therefore, He cannot be a necessary cause by essence, and it remains that He is an agent by choice.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a proof by contradiction demonstrating that God cannot be a necessary cause (موجب بالذات), but must be a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار). The argument begins with a known premise: no single atom is the cause of another atom. Then the author states the philosophical consensus that a cause cannot have more than one effect. He then argues: if a simple essence were the cause of two effects, the concepts of those effects would either be part of the essence or external to it. If they are constitutive, the essence becomes composite, contradicting its simplicity. If they are external, they would be attributes that are possible in themselves and necessary by the essence, leading to a regress when analyzing how the essence relates to them. If one concept is internal and the other external, the essence becomes composite. Thus, a simple essence cannot be the cause of two effects. Consequently, if God were a necessary cause, His effect would be either one or more; both are impossible, so He cannot be a necessary cause. Hence, He must act by free will.

Key Terms
بسيط
علة
معلول
موجب بالذات
فاعل بالاختيار
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A simple cause can produce multiple effects without being composite.
Correction: The proof shows that any attempt for a simple cause to have two effects leads to composition or regress, making it impossible. Simplicity entails a single effect.

Checkpoint
According to the proof, what happens if the concepts of two effects are constitutive of the cause?
A) The cause remains simple.
B) The cause becomes composite.
C) The concepts are external.
D) The cause is not affected.

Key Takeaway
A simple essence cannot be the cause of two distinct effects; therefore God, being absolutely simple, acts by free will, not by necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God were a necessary cause, your existence would be automatic – no love, no purpose. But the proof shows He acts by choice. Does your worship reflect a relationship with a free and personal God, or do you treat Him as an impersonal force? How will you change your prayer today to acknowledge His free will?
?
The argument proves that God's simplicity means He cannot be forced to act. Yet you often live as if God's actions are mechanical – expecting things to happen by cause and effect. Where in your life have you assumed God's responses are automatic? Repent of that presumption and ask for a heart that recognizes His sovereignty.
?
You are reading a proof that God acts by choice. Do you truly believe He chose to create you? Does that truth fill you with humility? If He had no choice, you would have no value. But He chose you. What difference should that make in how you live today?

The Three Aspects of a Single Effect
Objection and answer: a single effect has three causal aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the reply to the objection that God's effect is one uses the three aspects of the first effect to allow multiplicity without requiring a composite cause.

Core Idea
A single effect can have multiple aspects that generate multiple effects, avoiding regress.

In this passage, the author presents an objection: why not simply say that God's effect is one? The reply shows that even a single effect has multiple facets—possibility, existence, and necessity by another—that can be causes of multiple things. Thus, the objection fails to prove that only one thing exists.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Let the full structure of the objection and reply sink in before analyzing details.
Notice that the reply has two parts: an initial dismissal and a detailed explanation.
2
Identify the objection and the initial reply
Find the objection (فإن قيل) and the first reply (قلنا). The initial reply is a simple counter: if the effect is one, its effect would also be one, leading to an infinite regress. Then the author refines this reply.
The refined reply uses a different approach.
3
Analyze the detailed reply
Focus on the three aspects of the first effect: possibility (إمكان), existence (وجود), and necessity by another (وجوب بالغير). See how each aspect becomes a cause for a different thing: matter of the outermost sphere, its form, and the governing intellect.
This shows that multiplicity can arise from a single effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : معلوله شيء واحد فقط ؟ قلنا : قوله : لو كان كذلك لكان معلول ذلك الواحد أيضاً واحداً، وحينئذ يلزم المحال . قلنا : لا نسلم أنه لو كان معلول ذات الله واحداً ، لزم أن يكون معلول ذلك المعلول أيضاً واحداً. وبيانه : هو أن ذلك المعلول لا بد وأن يكون ممكن الوجود لذاته . أو لا بد أن يكون وجوده صادراً تلك العلة . فحصل هناك أمور ثلاثة : إمكانه ، ووجوده ، ووجوبه بالغير، فنجعل هذه الأمور الثلاثة علة لمعلولات ثلاثة فنجعل الإمكان علة المادة الفلك الأقصى، والوجود علة لصورته ، ووجوبه بالغير علة للعقل المدبر لذلك الفلك . وإذا كان هذا المعنى محتملاً ، لم يلزم من القول بأن معلول ذات الله واحد . القول بأن معلول معلوله يجب أن يكون واحداً .
If it is said: why may it not be said that His effect is only one thing? We say: his statement: if it were so, then the effect of that one would also be one, and then the impossible would follow. We say: we do not accept that if the effect of God's essence is one, then the effect of that effect must also be one. Its explanation: that effect must be possible in itself and its existence must proceed from that cause. So three things arise: its possibility, its existence, and its necessity by another. We make these three things causes of three effects: we make possibility the cause of the matter of the outermost sphere, existence the cause of its form, and its necessity by another the cause of the intellect that governs that sphere. And if this meaning is probable, then from the statement that the effect of God's essence is one, it does not follow that the effect of His effect must be one.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection and its answer. The objector suggests: why not simply say God's effect is one? The initial reply states that if so, then that one effect would also have one effect, leading to an infinite chain, which is impossible. However, the author then provides a more detailed answer: he does not accept that the effect's effect must be one. He explains that the effect (the first created being) has three aspects: its possibility (إمكان), its existence (وجود), and its necessity by another (وجوب بالغير). These three aspects can be causes of three separate things: possibility causes the matter of the outermost sphere, existence causes its form, and necessity by another causes the intellect governing that sphere. Thus, multiplicity can arise from a single effect without requiring the cause to be composite. Therefore, the objector's simple scenario does not force the conclusion that only one thing exists.

Correction: The passage shows that a single effect has multiple aspects (possibility, existence, necessity by another) that can produce multiple effects, thus multiplicity does not require composition in the cause.

The Second Objection: One Effect, Diverse Receivers
Objection that the one effect is existence, and diversity comes from receivers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the First Cause produces only existence, and all multiplicity arises from the different receptive capacities of essences.

Core Idea
The cause gives existence; receivers create multiplicity.

In this passage, the second objection challenges the idea that a simple cause cannot produce multiple effects. Instead, it argues that the First Cause produces only one thing—existence—and all diversity comes from the different essences that receive that existence. An analogy of sunlight helps illustrate how a single effect can appear diverse due to receivers.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection's claim
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the objector begins by granting that the earlier division is invalid, then proposes an alternative: the effect is one (existence).
The objection is not rejecting the previous argument entirely, but proposing a different model.
2
Follow the proof against essence-influence
The passage argues that the agent does not influence the essence (mahiyya) because if it did, removing the agent would change the essence, which is impossible. Identify this step and see how it leads to the conclusion that the agent only influences existence.
The example of blackness is used to show that essence is intrinsic, not dependent on an agent.
3
Study the sunlight analogy
The objection likens the First Cause to the sun, whose effect is one illumination. The diversity of light on stone vs. tree is due to the receivers, not the light itself. Apply this to the metaphysical case: existence is one, essences diversify it.
This analogy is central to the objection; it makes the abstract idea concrete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثاني : سلمنا أن هذا التقسيم فاسد . فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : أن معلوله واحد . ومع ذلك فإنه يكون علة لوجود كل الممكنات ؟ وبيانه : هو أن تأثير الفاعل ليس في الماهية ، فإنه لو كان كون السواد سواداً واقعاً بالفاعل ، وكل ما بالغير يلزم ارتفاعه عند ارتفاع ذلك الغير. فعند فرض عدم ذلك المؤثر ، يجب أن يخرج السواد عن كونه سواداً . وذلك محال . فيثبت أن تأثير الفاعل ليس في الماهية ، بل في الوجود . لكن الوجود من حيث إنه وجود أمر واحد ، فالصادر عن العلة الأولى هو الوجود فقط ، والماهيات من حيث هي قابلة للوجود ، فيصل ذلك الوجود إلى كل ممكن بمقدار استعداده واستحقاقه . فالصادر عن العلة الأولى واحد ، والتعدد إنما حصل بحسب تعدد القوابل. وهذا كما نقول : إن الشمس علة للإضاءة ، والإضاءة أثر واحد وحقيقة واحدة . ثم إن الضوء إذا وقع على العالم فبعضه وقع على الحجر ، وبعضه وقع على الشجر . وذلك التعدد لم يحصل في التأثير، وإنما حصل تعدد القوابل . فكذا هاهنا
The second question: we grant that this division is invalid. So why may it not be said that His effect is one, and yet He is the cause of the existence of all possible things? Its explanation: the agent's influence is not upon the essence, because if blackness being black were from an agent, then whatever is by another would cease when that other ceases. So when that agent is supposed non-existent, blackness would have to cease being black, which is impossible. So it is established that the agent's influence is not upon the essence, but upon existence. But existence, insofar as it is existence, is one thing. So what proceeds from the First Cause is existence only, and essences, insofar as they are receptive of existence, receive that existence according to their preparedness and merit. So what proceeds from the First Cause is one, and multiplicity only occurs according to the multiplicity of receivers. This is like our saying: the sun is the cause of illumination, and illumination is one effect and one reality. Then when light falls upon the world, some of it falls on stone and some on tree. That multiplicity did not occur in the effect, but rather the multiplicity of receivers occurred. So it is here.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, which argues that the First Cause's effect is one (existence itself), and multiplicity arises from the different receptive essences (الماهيات). The objection begins by granting that the earlier division (that a simple cause cannot produce two effects) is invalid, and then proposes an alternative: the agent does not act upon the essence (ماهية) but upon existence. If the agent acted upon the essence, then removing the agent would change the essence—e.g., blackness would cease to be blackness—which is impossible. Therefore, the agent only gives existence. Existence is one, so the First Cause produces only existence. Different essences receive this existence according to their capacity, causing multiplicity. The analogy of sunlight is given: the sun causes one illumination, but it takes different forms on different objects. Thus, the unity of the cause is preserved while accounting for multiplicity in the world.

Key Terms
ماهية
وجود
فاعل
قابل
استعداد
إضاءة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A single cause must produce a single effect; if effects are many, there must be many causes.
Correction: The objection shows that a single cause (First Cause) produces a single effect (existence), but multiplicity arises from the multiplicity of receivers (essences), not from the cause itself.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, what does the agent's influence affect?
A) The essence
B) The existence
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The First Cause's effect is one (existence), but multiplicity arises from the diversity of receptive essences, as illustrated by the sunlight analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Which view drives you to take more responsibility for your own spiritual growth?
?
If the diversity of creation comes from the receivers and not from the Giver, then your own limitations and strengths are, in a real sense, your own. How does that make you feel about your role in shaping your soul? What will you do today to increase your 'preparedness' to receive God's guidance?
?
The sunlight is one, but stone and tree respond differently. In your life, where are you hard like stone – unreceptive to reminders, unwilling to change? And where are you like a tree – alive, growing, soaking in the light? Write down one area where you will deliberately soften your heart this week to receive God's light more fully.

The Third Objection: Can One Cause Produce Multiple Effects?
Objector challenges the principle 'from one only one proceeds' by citing an earlier criticism and drawing a distinction between different and multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection to the principle 'from one only one proceeds' and how it challenges the proof based on distinct concepts.

Core Idea
The 'one cause, one effect' principle may only forbid effects of different kinds, not multiple effects.

So far, the discussion has assumed that a single cause can only produce a single effect. The third objection challenges this assumption directly. The objector points out that the proof previously given—based on the distinctness of concepts—only shows that a cause cannot produce two different effects, not that it cannot produce multiple effects. This objection cuts to the heart of the causal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic text and translation carefully. Notice how the objector frames the challenge: 'why may it not be said that more than one can proceed from the one?'
2
Identify the key distinction
The objector argues that the proof only prevents two different effects, not multiple effects of the same type. Locate where this distinction is made.
3
Recognize the meta-critique
The objector references an earlier objection against the proof. Understand that this is a layered critique—it challenges the foundation of the entire causal argument.
The text is cut off, so the full conclusion is missing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السؤال الثالث : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه يجوز أن يصدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد ؟ والدليل الذي ذكرتموه قد سبق الاعتراض عليه في باب أحكام العلل والمعلولات . والذي يقوي هذا الكلام هو أن الدليل الذي ذكرتموه في أن الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد، إنما قام على المؤثر ، فإنا قلنا : إن مفهوم صدر عنه هذا الأثر مغاير لمفهوم أنه صدر عنه ذلك الأثر . فهذا الدليل إن صح وجب أن يدل على أن المؤثر الواحد لا يصدر عنه أثران مختلفان ، وحينئذ يلزم
The third question: why may it not be said that it is permissible for more than one to proceed from the one? The proof you have mentioned has already been objected to in the chapter on the rules of causes and effects. What strengthens this statement is that the proof you mentioned, that from the one only one proceeds, is based on the agent, for we said: the concept that this effect proceeded from it is different from the concept that that effect proceeded from it. So if this proof is valid, it must indicate that one agent does not produce two different effects, and then it follows...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection, which directly challenges the principle "from one only one proceeds" (الواحد لا يصدر عنه إلا الواحد). The objector references an earlier discussion in the chapter on causes and effects where this proof was already criticized. He argues that the proof given earlier (that the concepts of two different effects are distinct) only shows that one agent cannot produce two effects that are different in kind, not that it cannot produce multiple effects. The text cuts off at the end of the page with "وحينئذ يلزم" (and then it follows...), leaving the conclusion incomplete. This objection is a meta-critique of the logical foundation of the entire argument about divine causality.

Key Takeaway
The third objection challenges the principle by arguing that the proof only restricts causing two different effects, not multiple effects of the same kind.

Answer to the First Objection: Possibility Cannot Be a Cause
Refutation of the first objection using three proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility (imkan) cannot be a cause of any existing thing according to the three proofs presented, and why it is considered a non-existent mental consideration.

Core Idea
Possibility is non-existent, and non-existence cannot cause existence.

The objection claims that three aspects of the first caused being—possibility, existence, and necessity-by-another—cause the celestial matter, form, and intellect. In response, the author demonstrates that possibility, being non-existent, cannot be a cause. This lesson explores the two complete proofs and the beginning of a third that refute this claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and the first proof
Carefully read the objection and the first proof, which argues that possibility is not an existing thing and therefore cannot be a cause.
Notice how the proof eliminates both necessity and possibility for possibility itself.
2
Analyze the second proof
The second proof presents a dilemma: possibility is either non-existent (cannot be a cause) or existent (implies infinite regress or contradiction). Follow the logical steps.
This is a classic kalam dilemma argument.
3
Consider the incomplete third proof
The third proof begins by noting that possibility is one concept with individuals, but the argument is cut off. Reflect on what a possible completion might be.
The missing part may discuss the ruling (hukm) of possibility on all individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما السؤال الأول: وهو قوله: إمكان المعلول الأول علة لمادة الفلك الأقصى، ووجوده علة لصورته، ووجوبه بالمبدأ الأول علة للعقل المدبر لذلك الفلك. فنقول: هذا باطل لوجوه: الحجة الأولى: الإمكان، ليس من الأمور الموجودة، وما لا يكون موجوداً امتنع كونه علة لشيء موجود. وإنما قلنا: إن الإمكان ليس من الأمور الموجودة، لأنه لو كان موجوداً، لكان إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته أو ممكناً لذاته. لا جائزاً أن يكون واجباً لذاته، لأنه يثبت بالدليل أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا واحداً، ولأن الإمكان صفة للممكن، وصفة الشيء مفتقرة إليه، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان. ولا جائز أن يكون ممكناً لذاته، وإلا لكان إمكانه زائداً عليه، ولزم التسلسل، فثبت أن الإمكان ليس من الموجودات. فنقول: وما كان معدوماً يمتنع أن يكون علة لشيء موجود، لأن العدم نفي محض، والنفي المحض يمتنع كونه علة للموجود، فيثبت بهذا البرهان: إن الإمكان يمتنع أن يكون علة لشيء من الموجودات. الحجة الثانية: أن نقول: الإمكان إما أن يكون معدوماً، وإما أن يكون موجوداً. فإن كان معدوماً امتنع كونه علة للموجود، وإن كان موجوداً فهو من قبل العلة، وليست تلك العلة إلا العلة الأولى. فالعلة الأولى علة للإمكان والوجود، فهي علة بمعلولين. وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك. هذا خلف. الحجة الثالثة: الإمكان مفهوم واحد، فهو نوع تحته أشخاص، وحكم
As for the first question: his statement: 'The possibility (imkan) of the first caused thing is a cause ('illa) for the matter (madda) of the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aqsa), its existence (wujud) is a cause for its form (sura), and its necessity (wujub) by the First Principle (al-mabda' al-awwal) is a cause for the intellect ('aql) that governs (mudabbir) that sphere.' Then we say: This is false for reasons. The first proof: Possibility is not among existing things (al-umur al-mawjuda), and that which is not existent is impossible to be a cause for something existent. We say that possibility is not among existing things because, if it were existent, it would either be necessary by itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) or possible by itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi). It cannot be necessary by itself because it is established by proof that the necessarily existent by itself is only one, and because possibility is a property (sifa) of the possible thing, and a property of a thing is dependent on it, and that which is dependent on the possible is more deserving of possibility. And it cannot be possible by itself, otherwise its possibility would be additional to it, and infinite regress (tasalsul) would follow. Thus it is established that possibility is not among existing things. So we say: And that which is non-existent is impossible to be a cause for something existent, because non-existence ('adam) is pure negation (nafi mahd), and pure negation is impossible to be a cause for the existent. Thus this proof establishes that possibility is impossible to be a cause for any of the existents. The second proof: We say: Possibility is either non-existent or existent. If it is non-existent, it is impossible to be a cause for the existent; and if it is existent, then it is from the cause, and that cause is nothing but the First Cause (al-'illa al-ula). So the First Cause is the cause of both possibility and existence; thus it is a cause of two caused things (ma'lulayn). But we have assumed that it is not so. This is a contradiction (khalaf). The third proof: Possibility is one concept (mafhum wahid); it is a type (naw') under which there are individuals (ashkhas), and the ruling (hukm) ...
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is an answer to the first objection. The objection claimed that the first caused being (al-ma'lul al-awwal) has three aspects: its possibility (imkan), its existence (wujud), and its necessity by the First Principle (wujub bi-al-mabda' al-awwal). It then asserted that these three aspects are causes for three different things: possibility causes the matter (madda) of the outermost sphere (al-falak al-aqsa), existence causes its form (sura), and necessity-by-another causes the intellect that governs that sphere. The author responds that this is false and provides three proofs. The first proof argues that possibility is not an existing thing; it is a mere mental consideration. If it were existent, it would have to be either necessary by itself or possible by itself. It cannot be necessary because only one being is necessary by itself (God), and possibility is a property dependent on the possible, so it cannot be necessary. It cannot be possible because that would require a second possibility for it, leading to infinite regress. Therefore, possibility is non-existent, and non-existence cannot be a cause of something existent. The second proof presents a dilemma: possibility is either non-existent or existent. If non-existent, it cannot be a cause. If existent, it must be from a cause, which is none other than the First Cause. Then the First Cause would be the cause of both possibility and existence, i.e., two caused things. But the original claim assumed that the First Cause only produces one effect (the first caused thing). This leads to a contradiction. The third proof begins but is cut off; it seems to argue that possibility is a single concept that can be divided into individuals, and the ruling (hukm) applies to all of them, but the text ends here. The overall refutation shows that the proposed causal relationship is impossible because possibility cannot be an existent cause and would lead to contradictory consequences.

Key Terms
إمكان (imkan)
وجود (wujud)
وجوب بالغير (wujub bi-al-ghayr)
علة ('illa)
معلول (ma'lul)
فلك أقصى (falak aqsa)
عقل مدبر ('aql mudabbir)
عدم ('adam)
نفي محض (nafi mahd)
تسلسل (tasalsul)
خلف (khalaf)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that possibility is an existing attribute that can act as a cause for physical things.
Correction: The passage shows that possibility is non-existent (a mere mental consideration) and therefore cannot be a cause; non-existence is pure negation and has no causal power.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why cannot possibility (imkan) be a cause of any existent thing?
A) Because possibility is an attribute of God.
B) Because possibility is a non-existent pure negation, and non-existence cannot be a cause of existence.
C) Because possibility exists only in the mind and has no effect on the physical world.
D) Because possibility requires a cause itself, leading to infinite regress.

Key Takeaway
Possibility (imkan) is non-existent and therefore cannot be a cause of anything; any claim that it causes something is false.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a fear or worry you have about the future that is based on a mere possibility, not something actual. Are you letting that non-existent possibility become a cause of your anxiety? How can you instead rely on Allah, the only true Cause, to bring about what is good for you?
?
The author argues that only the First Cause truly causes, and secondary causes are not independent. Examine your daily life: do you act as though people, circumstances, or your own efforts are independent causes of outcomes? What would it mean to truly believe that nothing happens except by Allah's will?
?
The objection tried to analyze the first caused being into possibility, existence, and necessity to explain creation. Have you ever tried to reduce Allah's creative act to human logic, forgetting that He is beyond our categories? Repent from intellectual arrogance and humble yourself before His absolute sovereignty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the First Cause can only produce one effect and the author's immediate refutation labeling it a fallacy.

Core Idea
A false claim about divine causality can be refuted by consensus and recognized as fallacy.

Philosophers sometimes argue that even if God is powerful and free, He can only directly cause one thing. This seems to limit divine power. The author does not elaborate further, but the refutation sets up the need for a more detailed response later.

First Proof: Possibility is Not Existent
The author refutes the philosophers' claim by proving possibility is non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author argues that possibility (al-imkan) is not an existent thing and therefore cannot serve as a cause for anything.

Core Idea
Possibility is not a real entity; it cannot cause anything.

In this passage, the author responds to the philosophers' claim that the possibility of the first effect causes the matter of the outermost sphere. He presents his first proof: possibility is not among existent things. If it were, it would have to be either necessary or possible in itself, but both options lead to contradictions. Therefore, possibility is non-existent and cannot be a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Notice the two key parts: the philosophers' claim and the author's refutation.
The author uses a dichotomy: if possibility were existent, it must be either necessary or possible in itself.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim
The philosophers assert that possibility of the first effect causes the matter of the outermost sphere, its existence causes its form, and its necessity from the First Principle causes the intellect governing that sphere. Understand that they treat possibility as a real causal entity.
3
Follow the dichotomy: necessary or possible
The author assumes possibility were existent. It would then be either necessary in itself or possible in itself. Why can it not be necessary? Because only one necessary existent exists (God), and possibility is an attribute dependent on something possible. Why can it not be possible? Because that would lead to an infinite regress of possibilities.
The argument relies on the principle that an attribute is more deserving of possibility than its subject.
4
Understand the conclusion
Since both options are impossible, possibility is not an existent thing. Therefore, it cannot be a cause for something existent, as non-existence is pure negation.
The author concludes that possibility cannot cause any existent thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما السؤال الأول : وهو قوله : إمكان المعلول الأول علة لمادة الفلك الأقصى ، ووجوده علة لصورته ، ووجوبه بالمبدأ الأول علة للعقل المدبر لذلك الفلك . فنقول : هذا باطل لوجوه : الحجة الأولى : الإمكان ، ليس من الأمور الموجودة ، وما لا يكون موجوداً امتنع كونه علة لشيء موجود . وإنما قلنا : إن الإمكان ليس من الأمور الموجودة ، لأنه لو كان موجوداً ، لكان إما أن يكون واجباً لذاته أو ممكناً لذاته . لا جائزاً أن يكون واجباً لذاته، لأنه يثبت بالدليل أن واجب الوجود لذاته ليس إلا واحداً ، ولأن الإمكان صفة للممكن ، وصفة الشيء مفتقرة إليه ، والمفتقر إلى الممكن أولى بالإمكان . ولا جائز أن يكون ممكناً لذاته ، وإلا لكان إمكانه زائداً عليه ، ولزم التسلسل ، فثبت أن الإمكان ليس من الموجودات . فنقول : وما كان معدوماً يمتنع أن يكون علة لشيء موجود ، لأن العدم نفي محض ، والنفي المحض يمتنع كونه علة للموجود ، فيثبت بهذا البرهان : إن الإمكان يمتنع أن يكون علة لشيء من الموجودات.
As for the first question: it is his statement: 'The possibility of the first effect is a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, and its existence is a cause for its form, and its necessity from the First Principle is a cause for the intellect that governs that sphere.' And we say: 'This is false for reasons.' The first proof: possibility is not among existent things, and whatever is not existent cannot be a cause for something existent. We say that possibility is not among existent things because if it were existent, it would either be necessary in itself or possible in itself. It cannot be necessary in itself because it is established by proof that the Necessary of Existence in itself is only One, and because possibility is an attribute of the possible, and an attribute of a thing is dependent on it, and what is dependent on the possible is more deserving of possibility. Nor can it be possible in itself, because then its possibility would be additional to it, leading to infinite regress. So it is established that possibility is not among existent things. We then say: what is non-existent cannot be a cause for something existent, because non-existence is pure negation, and pure negation cannot be a cause for the existent. By this proof it is established that possibility cannot be a cause for any existent thing.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a specific philosophical claim. The opponent asserts that the possibility of the first effect causes the matter of the outermost sphere, its existence causes its form, and its necessity from the First Principle causes the intellect governing that sphere. The author responds that this is false, and gives the first of several proofs. The proof argues that possibility (al-imkan) is not an existent thing, and therefore cannot cause anything. To demonstrate that possibility is not existent, the author uses a dichotomy: if possibility were existent, it would be either necessary in itself or possible in itself. It cannot be necessary in itself because only one necessary existent (God) exists, and possibility is an attribute dependent on something possible. It also cannot be possible in itself because that would lead to an infinite regress of possibilities. Since it is neither necessary nor possible, it is not existent. Therefore, being non-existent, it cannot cause anything.

فلك الأقصى
علة
واجب الوجود
ممكن لذاته
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a real entity that can act as a cause.
Correction: The author proves possibility is not an existent thing; it is merely an attribute dependent on the possible, so it cannot be a cause.

Checkpoint

D) Because the philosophers deny it

Key Takeaway
Possibility is not an existent thing; it is a mere attribute dependent on the possible, and thus cannot be a cause.

Second Proof: Dichotomy on Possibility
Proof by dichotomy against possibility as a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the second proof against possibility as a cause, which forces the First Cause to have two effects, contradicting the principle of one cause one effect.

Core Idea
If possibility were existent, the First Cause would produce two effects — an impossibility.

In this passage, the author presents the second proof against the claim that possibility can be a cause. Using a logical dichotomy, the argument shows that whether possibility is non-existent or existent, it cannot serve as a genuine cause. This proof relies on the principle that from one cause only one effect can proceed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Pay attention to the dichotomy presented.
2
Understand the dichotomy
The argument splits possibility into two cases: non-existent or existent. Follow each branch and its implication.
3
Examine the first branch
If possibility is non-existent, it cannot be a cause for anything that exists. This is straightforward.
4
Examine the second branch
If possibility is existent, it must have a cause, which is the First Cause. This would make the First Cause the cause of both possibility and the existent thing, i.e., two effects.
The assumption is that the First Cause produces only one effect.
5
Identify the contradiction
The proof concludes that this leads to a contradiction (خلف), thus possibility cannot be a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : أن نقول : الإمكان إما أن يكون معدوماً ، وإما أن يكون موجوداً . فإن كان معدوماً امتنع كونه علة للموجود ، وإن كان موجوداً فهو من قبل العلة وليست تلك العلة إلا العلة الأولى . فالعلة الأولى علة للإمكان والوجود، فهي علة بمعلولين . وقد فرضنا أنه ليس كذلك . هذا خلف .
The second proof: we say: possibility is either non-existent or existent. If it is non-existent, it cannot be a cause for the existent. If it is existent, then it is from a cause, and that cause is none other than the First Cause. So the First Cause is a cause for both possibility and existence, thus it is a cause for two effects. And we have assumed that it is not like that. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the second proof against the claim that possibility can be a cause. The argument is a dichotomy: possibility is either non-existent or existent. If it is non-existent, it cannot cause anything. If it is existent, it must have a cause, and that cause can only be the First Cause. This would mean the First Cause produces two effects: the possibility itself and the existence of the possible thing. But the opponent's assumption (or the general philosophical principle) is that from one cause only one effect proceeds. Thus, this leads to a contradiction (خلف). Therefore, possibility cannot be a cause.

Key Terms
الإمكان
العلة الأولى
معلولين
خلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility is a property that does not need a separate cause.
Correction: The argument treats possibility as something that, if existent, would require a cause, leading to two effects, which contradicts the principle of one cause one effect.

Checkpoint

A) It is existent and caused by the First Cause
B) It is non-existent and cannot be a cause
C) Either way, it leads to a contradiction
D) It is a necessary attribute of matter

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that possibility as a cause leads to a contradiction, whether it is non-existent or existent.

Third Proof: Possibility as a Single Concept
The author presents the third proof via logical analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the third proof uses the logical principle that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like to refute the claim that possibility is a cause.

Core Idea
If possibility causes one effect, it must cause similar effects everywhere—but it doesn't.

We now encounter the third proof, a concise but powerful argument by analogy. The author reasons that possibility (al-imkan) is a single concept, a genus with many individuals. According to a fundamental logical rule, if a property like possibility causes a specific effect in one case, it must cause a similar effect in every instance. This universal implication leads to an absurd conclusion, making the original claim impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the compact style: the argument is just two sentences.
2
Identify the logical principle
Find the phrase 'حكم الشيء حكم مثله' (the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like). This is the key rule used in the proof.
This principle is common in kalam reasoning.
3
Apply the principle to possibility
Understand how the author applies the principle: if possibility is a single concept (genus), then its effect must be similar in all instances. So if the possibility of the outermost sphere caused its matter, then every possible thing should cause a similar sphere.
The argument relies on the idea that possibility is one concept.
4
See the absurd conclusion
Recognize that this conclusion is false (most possible things do not produce spheres). Therefore, the original assumption that possibility is a cause is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : الإمكان مفهوم واحد ، فهو نوع تحته أشخاص ، وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ، فلو كان الإمكان علة لمادة الفلك الأقصى ، لوجب أن يكون إمكان كل ممكن علة لمثل هذا المعلول . وهو محال .
The third proof: possibility is a single concept, so it is a genus under which are individuals, and the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. So if possibility were a cause for the matter of the outermost sphere, then it would be necessary that the possibility of every possible be a cause for a similar effect. This is absurd.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the third proof, an argument by analogy. The author states that possibility (al-imkan) is one concept, a genus with individual instances. According to logical reasoning, if a quality is a cause in one instance, the same quality in other instances should produce similar effects. If the possibility of the first effect caused the matter of the outermost sphere, then the possibility of any other possible thing should also cause a similar effect, such as a sphere. Since this is clearly not the case (most possible things do not produce spheres), the original claim is absurd.

Key Terms
مفهوم واحد
نوع
أشخاص
حكم الشيء حكم مثله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Possibility only causes effects when God chooses to actualize it.
Correction: The argument is about the inherent causal power of possibility itself, not about divine choice. If possibility were a cause by its nature, it would necessitate similar effects in all cases regardless of divine will.

Checkpoint
What is the logical principle used in the third proof?
A) The law of non-contradiction
B) The judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like
C) Possibility is a genus
D) One cause only produces one effect

Key Takeaway
The third proof shows that if possibility were a cause, it would entail absurd consequences, disproving the claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possible thing you could do were to actually happen because of its mere possibility, what would that mean for your choices? Are you assuming that possibility alone justifies action?
?
Do you treat some possibilities as if they must lead to certain outcomes, ignoring the absurdity of that assumption? How does this proof expose a hidden arrogance in assuming that because something is possible, it should happen?
?
When you consider the order in the universe, do you ever take that order for granted? This proof shows that matter is not spontaneously arranging itself by mere possibility. Where in your life have you assumed that something happens 'just because it's possible' rather than by the will of Allah?

The Fourth Proof: Leading the Possible to Necessity
Author presents fourth proof against possibility as cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth proof against the philosophers' claim that the possibility of the first effect causes the existence of the matter, and how it leads to a logical contradiction.

Core Idea
If possibility can cause existence, then the possible becomes necessary—an absurdity.

The author now presents a fourth argument, building on the principle from the third proof that the judgment of like things is the same. If the possibility of the first effect is taken as a cause for the existence of the matter, then by analogy, the possibility of that matter itself would have to cause its own existence. But since possibility is a necessary concomitant of the essence, this would force the essence to entail its own existence, turning a possible being into a necessary being. This contradiction shows the original assumption is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and English
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument come through.
Notice the logical connectors: 'لو كان', 'فيلزم', 'وهو محال'.
2
Trace the logical chain
Identify the conditional assumption: if possibility of first effect causes matter's existence, then possibility of that matter would cause its own existence. Follow the entailment: essence -> possibility -> existence.
The chain uses the principle 'حكم الشيء حكم مثله' (judgment of a thing is judgment of its like).
3
Understand the entailment relations
Note that possibility is a necessary concomitant (لازم) of the essence. So the essence entails possibility, and possibility entails existence. Therefore essence entails existence, making it necessary in itself.
The key term 'مستلزم' means 'entailer'.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that a possible in itself would be necessary in itself—a direct contradiction. This shows the original assumption must be false.
Ask yourself: why is it impossible for a possible being to be necessary in itself?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان إمكان المعلول الأول علة لوجود المادة ، لكان إمكان تلك المادة علة لوجودها لما بينا أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ، ولو كان إمكان المادة علة لوجودها ، وإمكان المادة من لوازم تلك الماهية ، فعلى هذا تلك الماهية مستلزمة لذلك الإمكان ، وهو مستلزم لذلك الوجود ، ومستلزم المستلزم ، مستلزم . فيجب أن تكون تلك الماهية مستلزمة لوجود نفسها . وما كان كذلك كان واجباً لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجباً لذاته . وهو محال .
The fourth proof: if the possibility of the first effect were a cause for the existence of the matter, then the possibility of that matter would be a cause for its own existence, because we have shown that the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like. And if the possibility of the matter is a cause for its existence, and the possibility of the matter is among the necessary concomitants of that essence, then that essence entails that possibility, and that possibility entails that existence, and the entailer of the entailer is an entailer. So it must be that that essence entails its own existence. And whatever is like that is necessary in itself. Thus it follows that a possible in itself would be necessary in itself. That is absurd.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the fourth proof, building on the principle from the third proof (judgment of the like). If the possibility of the first effect caused the matter's existence, then analogously, the possibility of that matter would cause its own existence. But the possibility of matter is a necessary concomitant of the essence of matter, meaning the essence entails possibility, which then entails existence. Therefore, the essence would entail its own existence, making it necessary in itself. Since matter is supposed to be possible, this is a contradiction.

Key Terms
إمكان المادة
لوازم الماهية
مستلزم
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that entailment of existence by essence is not the same as being self-caused, but the argument shows logical necessity suffices for the absurdity.
Correction: The argument does not require self-causation; it uses logical entailment: if essence entails existence, then the being is necessary in itself, which contradicts its possible nature.

Checkpoint
What does the fourth proof conclude about the claim that possibility causes matter's existence?
A) It is possible
B) It leads to matter being necessary in itself
C) It is consistent with philosophy
D) It proves the existence of God

Key Takeaway
If possibility can cause existence, then the possible becomes necessary—a contradiction that refutes the original assumption.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the possibility of something cannot cause its own existence, what does that imply about your own tendency to rely on possibilities and probabilities rather than the Necessary Being? In what area of your life are you placing your hope in 'maybe' rather than in Allah?
?
The proof shows that a possible being cannot be self-sufficient in existence. Where in your spiritual life do you act as if you are self-sufficient, as if your own efforts and possibilities guarantee success? How will you consciously depend on Allah alone today?
?
The argument uses logical necessity to lead to an absurdity if one assumes possibility is a cause. Are there assumptions in your own worldview that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would lead to absurdity? What are they, and are you willing to abandon them for the truth revealed in this lesson?

Possibility is Receptivity, Not Activity
The author argues that possibility is passive receptivity, so cannot be an active cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why possibility, being pure receptivity, cannot serve as an active cause in the emanation framework.

Core Idea
Possibility is receptivity, not activity.

The philosophers argue that the sphere can be both cause and effect, but the author points out a logical flaw: one thing cannot be both receptive and active simultaneously. Since possibility is defined as the receptivity of an essence to existence and non-existence, it cannot be an active cause. This proof reinforces that only the Necessary Being can be the true active cause.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the key principle
Read the first line: 'one thing cannot be both receptive and active at the same time.' This is a self-evident principle that the philosophers themselves accept.
2
Apply the principle to the sphere
If the sphere were a cause of the first intellect, it would be both an effect (of the intellect) and a cause — violating the principle.
The principle applies to the same respect; in different respects it might be possible, but here it is the same relation.
3
Define possibility
The author states: 'Possibility is nothing other than receptivity.' Understand that 'imkan' means the essence is receptive to existence or non-existence.
This definition is crucial: possibility is passive, not active.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since possibility is purely receptive, it cannot be an active cause. Therefore, the possibility of the first intellect cannot be a cause for the matter of the sphere.
This refutes the emanationist claim that possibility plays a causal role.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن مذهبهم : أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلاً معاً وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فإنه لا يكون نفس الفلك علة لنفس العقل الأول . لكن الإمكان ، ليس إلا القبول . وهو أن تكون الماهية قابلة للوجود والعدم، فوجب أن لا يكون إمكان العقل الأول ، علة لمادة الفلك .
The fifth proof: their doctrine is that one thing cannot be both receptive and active at the same time. And if this is the case, then the sphere itself cannot be a cause of the first intellect itself. But possibility is nothing other than receptivity. That is, that the essence is receptive to existence and non-existence. So it must be that the possibility of the first intellect is not a cause for the matter of the sphere.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the fifth proof. The author cites a principle held by the philosophers themselves: one and the same thing cannot be both receptive (qabil) and active (fa'il) simultaneously. The author then applies this to the object under discussion: the sphere cannot be both a cause and an effect in the same respect. More importantly, possibility (al-imkan) is defined as receptivity (qubul) — it is the fact that an essence can receive existence or non-existence. Since possibility is purely receptive, it cannot be an active cause. Therefore, the possibility of the first intellect cannot be a cause for the matter of the sphere.

Misconception: Possibility is an active power that can cause things to exist.
Correction: The author clarifies that possibility (al-imkan) is nothing but receptivity (qubul) — the capacity to receive existence or non-existence, not an active faculty.

The Sixth Proof: The Sphere's Divisibility Proves It Cannot Be Caused by a Single Possibility
The author presents the sixth proof using imaginary division to demonstrate that the sphere is composite, refuting the idea that a single possibility can cause it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the concept of imaginary division leads to a proof that the sphere's matter cannot be caused by a single possibility, upholding the principle that from one only one proceeds.

Core Idea
The sphere's imaginary divisibility proves it is composite, so one cause cannot produce it.

In this sixth proof, the author uses a key physical concept—imaginary division—to expose a logical contradiction in the philosophers' causal account of the sphere. By showing that the sphere is composed of many parts, he forces the question: how could a single simple cause produce a composite effect? The passage presents a crisp argument that leads to an absurdity rejected by both sides.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the key premise
The author states 'the sphere is divisible in imagination.' Ask yourself: what does this imply about the sphere's nature?
3
Follow the logical steps
Trace the argument: from divisibility to composition, then to the problem of one cause causing many parts.
Notice the reference to a later discussion on the indivisible substance.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The proof shows that the cause of the sphere's matter cannot be the single possibility, otherwise one would cause many.
This reaffirms the principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : وهي الفلك قابل للقسمة الوهمية . وسيأتي في مسئلة إثبات الجوهر الفرد : أن ما يكون قابلاً للقسمة الوهمية ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مؤلفاً من الأجزاء . وذلك ينتج أن جسم الفلك مؤلف من الأجزاء الكثيرة ، والإمكان القائم بالعقل الأول شيء واحد ، فلو جعلناه علة لمادة الفلك وتلك المادة عبارة عن مجموع تلك الأجزاء فحينئذ يلزم كون الشيء الواحد ، علة لمجموع تلك الأجزاء ، فيعود إلى أنه صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد .
The sixth proof: the sphere is divisible in imagination. And it will come in the issue of proving the indivisible substance that what is divisible in imagination must be composed of parts. That yields that the body of the sphere is composed of many parts. And the possibility subsisting in the first intellect is one thing. So if we make it a cause for the matter of the sphere, and that matter is the sum of those parts, then it would be necessary for one thing to be a cause for a sum of parts. That reverts to that from the One more than one proceeds.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is the sixth proof. The author uses the concept of imaginary division (al-qisma al-wahmiyya). The sphere of the outermost heaven can be divided in imagination, which proves it is composed of parts (as will be shown in the discussion on the indivisible substance). Therefore, the body of the sphere is a composite of many parts. Now, the possibility (al-imkan) that subsists in the first intellect is a single, simple thing. If we claim that this single possibility is the cause of the matter of the sphere, which is a composite of many parts, then one simple cause produces a composite effect with many parts. This violates the principle that from one cause only one effect proceeds (since the sphere's matter has many parts). Thus, the claim leads to the absurdity that from the One (the First Cause) more than one proceeds, which the philosophers themselves reject.

Key Terms

جوهر فرد
أجزاء
شيء واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The sphere is a simple, indivisible entity.
Correction: The author argues that because the sphere can be divided in imagination, it must be composed of parts, making it composite.

Checkpoint

A) Because the sphere is indivisible
B) Because the sphere is composed of many parts and possibility is one
C) Because possibility does not exist
D) Because the sphere is eternal

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof uses the imaginary divisibility of the sphere to show it is composite, thereby refuting the claim that a single possibility causes its matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the sphere's imaginary division reveals its dependence on a cause beyond itself, what does your own ability to imagine your limits reveal about your need for a Creator? Will you pause today and honestly examine one area where you act as if you are self-sufficient?
