The Fourth Argument: Possibility as Cause Leads to Self-Necessity
Al-Isfahani's fourth argument refuting possibility as a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why claiming that possibility causes existence leads to the absurd conclusion that a contingent thing becomes necessary in itself.

Core Idea
If possibility were a cause, contingent things would become self-necessary.

Al-Isfahani now delivers the fourth and most penetrating argument against the view that the First Intellect's possibility causes matter. Using the logical principle that 'the judgment of a thing applies to its like', he shows that if possibility were a cause, then any essence's own possibility would force it to necessitate its own existence, turning a contingent into a necessary being — a clear contradiction. This argument drives home why possibility cannot be a genuine cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once through
Read the Arabic and translation without pausing. Let the logical flow sink in before analyzing.
Focus on the chain: possibility → cause → existence.
2
Identify the parallel reasoning
Notice the clause 'حكم الشيء حكم مثله' — it extends the original assumption to a parallel case: the possibility of matter itself.
This is the engine of the argument.
3
Follow the chain of necessitation
Trace how essence necessitates possibility, which necessitates existence, leading to essence necessitating its own existence.
Each 'must' is a logical necessity.
4
Understand the impossible conclusion
The conclusion is that a contingent thing would be necessary in itself (واجب لذاته). Why is that impossible?
Contingent and necessary are mutually exclusive categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان إمكان المعلول الأول علة لوجود المادة ، لكان إمكان تلك المادة علة لوجودها لما بينا أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله ، ولو كان إمكان المادة علة لوجودها ، وإمكان المادة من لوازم تلك الماهية (١) ، فعلى هذا تلك الماهية مستلزمة لذلك الإمكان ، وهو مستلزم لذلك الوجود ، ومستلزم المستلزم ، مستلزم . فيجب أن تكون تلك الماهية مستلزمة لوجود نفسها . وما كان كذلك كان واجباً لذاته ، فيلزم أن يكون الممكن لذاته واجباً لذاته . وهو محال
The fourth argument: If the possibility of the first effect were a cause for the existence of matter, then the possibility of that matter would be a cause for its existence because we have shown that the judgment of a thing is like the judgment of its like. And if the possibility of matter were a cause for its existence, and the possibility of matter is among the necessary concomitants of that essence (1), then on this basis that essence necessitates that possibility, and it necessitates that existence, and the necessitator of the necessitator is a necessitator. Therefore, that essence must necessitate its own existence. And whatever is like that is necessary in itself. Hence it would follow that the contingent in itself is necessary in itself, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the claim that the possibility of the First Intellect (the first effect) is the cause of the celestial sphere's matter. The argument proceeds by drawing a parallel: if the possibility of the first effect were the cause of matter's existence, then by the rule that 'the judgment of a thing applies to its like', the possibility of that matter itself must also be the cause of its own existence. But the possibility of matter is a necessary concomitant of the essence of matter. Therefore, the essence would necessitate its own possibility, which necessitates existence. Thus the essence would necessitate its own existence, making it necessary in itself. But that is impossible because contingent things are not necessary in themselves. The conclusion is that the original claim leads to contradiction.

Key Terms
إمكان
علة
معلول أول
مادة
حكم الشيء حكم مثله
لازم
مستلزم
واجب لذاته
ممكن لذاته

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument only applies to matter because the opponent claims the First Intellect's possibility causes matter, but the argument shows the principle 'the judgment of a thing is like its like' makes it universal.
Correction: The argument demonstrates that if possibility were a cause for any existent, then any essence would become self-necessitating, which is impossible for contingents.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate absurd consequence if possibility were a cause for matter?
A) The First Intellect would be self-caused
B) The essence of matter would necessitate its own existence
C) Matter would not exist
D) The celestial sphere would be eternal

Key Takeaway
If possibility were a cause, then any contingent essence would necessitate its own existence, turning it into a necessary being — a contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what ways do you subconsciously treat possibilities as if they have causal power, expecting things to happen just because they are possible? How does this argument strip away that illusion?
?
If every contingent essence would be self-necessary if possibility were a cause, then you are not self-necessary. What does that reveal about your absolute dependence on the Necessary Being? Write down one area of your life where you act as if you are self-sufficient.
?
The argument uses logic to destroy a false assumption. What false assumptions about causality do you hold that need to be shattered by the same rigorous reasoning? Commit to questioning one such assumption today.

The Fifth Argument: Possibility as Receptivity Cannot Be a Cause
Al-Isfahani argues that possibility is mere receptivity, so it cannot be an active cause for matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the opponent's own principle prevents possibility from being a cause for matter.

Core Idea
A cause must be active; possibility is merely passive.

In this passage, Al-Isfahani presents the fifth argument, which turns the opponent's own principle against them. The argument hinges on the distinction between agency and receptivity. Let's follow the logical steps that lead to the conclusion that possibility cannot be a cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before analyzing.
Notice the footnote markers in Arabic; they indicate textual variants or references.
2
Identify the key premise
The argument starts by stating the opponent's own doctrine: one thing cannot be both receptive and agent. This is a crucial starting point.
3
Note the definition of possibility
The passage defines possibility as nothing but receptivity—the essence's capacity to receive existence or non-existence. This definition is central.
Receptivity is passive, not active.
4
Follow the logical deduction
From the principle and the definition, deduce that the possibility of the First Intellect cannot be a cause for the matter of the sphere, because that would require agency.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum of the opponent's position.
5
Reflect on the refutation
Consider how this argument uses the opponent's own premises to refute them, without introducing external assumptions.
This is a common strategy in kalam debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الخامسة : إن مذهبهم : أن الشيء الواحد لا يكون قابلا وفاعلاً معاً (۲) وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فإنه لا يكون نفس الفلك (۳) علة لنفس العقل الأول (1) . لكن الإمكان ، ليس إلا القبول . وهو أن تكون الماهية قابلة للوجود والعدم، فوجب أن لا يكون إمكان العقل الأول ، علة () لمادة الفلك .
The fifth argument: Their doctrine is that one thing cannot be receptive and agent together (2). If this is the case, then the sphere itself (3) is not a cause for the First Intellect itself (1). But possibility is nothing but receptivity—that is, the essence being receptive to existence and non-existence. Therefore, it is necessary that the possibility of the First Intellect is not a cause () for the matter of the sphere.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which relies on the opponent's own principle that a single thing cannot be both recipient and agent simultaneously. The argument states: since the sphere itself is not a cause for the First Intellect (as per the opponent's own view), and possibility is merely receptivity (the essence's capacity to receive existence or non-existence), then the possibility of the First Intellect cannot be a cause for the matter of the sphere. Because if it were a cause, it would be an agent, but it is defined as pure receptivity, violating the principle.

Key Terms
قابل
فاعل
إمكان
قبول
مادة الفلك
عقل أول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the argument is circular because it assumes the opponent's position.
Correction: The argument uses the opponent's own premise against them, so it is a valid reductio ad absurdum, not circular.

Checkpoint

A) Because possibility is an accident
B) Because possibility is mere receptivity, and a cause must be active
C) Because matter does not exist
D) Because the First Intellect is uncreated

Key Takeaway
Possibility is pure receptivity, so it cannot act as a cause; a cause must be active.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Sixth Argument: Divisibility of the Sphere and Unity of Cause
Al-Isfahani presents the sixth argument linking sphere divisibility to composition and causality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that the divisibility of the celestial sphere implies its composition, and that a single possibility cannot be the cause for its many parts without violating the principle that from the One only one proceeds.

Core Idea
If the sphere is divisible, it must be composite, so a single cause cannot produce its many parts.

In this passage, Al-Isfahani presents the sixth argument against the claim that the possibility subsisting in the First Intellect is the cause for the matter of the sphere. He begins with an accepted premise: the sphere is divisible in imagination. From this, he argues that the sphere must be composed of many parts, and a single cause cannot be responsible for a multiplicity of effects. This argument is crucial for understanding the kalam position on the uniqueness of the Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Al-Isfahani builds the argument step by step.
2
Identify the main premise and conclusion
The premise is that the sphere is divisible in imagination. The conclusion is that a single cause cannot produce the sphere's many parts. Find the intermediate steps.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace how divisibility leads to composition, and then how composition conflicts with a single cause. Use the commentary and breakdown to clarify.
The reference to the indivisible atom proof is crucial.
4
Understand the principle at stake
The argument relies on the principle 'from the One only one proceeds.' Reflect on why this principle is important and how it is used here.
This principle is accepted by both sides, so it strengthens the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السادسة : وهي الفلك قابل للقسمة الوهمية . وسيأتي في مسئلة إثبات الجوهر الفرد : أن ما يكون قابلاً للقسمة الوهمية ، فإنه لا بد وأن يكون مؤلفاً من الأجزاء . وذلك ينتج أن جسم الفلك مؤلف من الأجزاء الكثيرة ، والإمكان القائم بالعقل الأول شيء واحد ، فلو جعلناه علة لمادة الفلك وتلك المادة عبارة عن مجموع تلك الأجزاء فحينئذ يلزم كون الشيء الواحد ، علة لمجموع تلك الأجزاء ، فيعود إلى أنه صدر عن الواحد أكثر من الواحد .
The sixth argument: The sphere is divisible in imagination. And it will come in the topic of proving the indivisible atom that whatever is divisible in imagination must necessarily be composed of parts. That yields that the body of the sphere is composed of many parts. The possibility subsisting in the First Intellect is one thing. So if we make it a cause for the matter of the sphere, and that matter is the sum of those parts, then it would follow that one thing is a cause for a sum of those parts, which returns to the principle that more than one issues from the One.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the sixth argument, which focuses on the divisibility of the celestial sphere. The author states that the sphere is divisible in imagination, and from the later discussion on the indivisible atom, it follows that anything divisible in imagination is composed of parts. Therefore, the sphere's body is composed of many parts. The possibility that subsists in the First Intellect is a single entity. If we claim that this single possibility is the cause for the matter of the sphere, and that matter is the aggregate of those many parts, then one single cause would be the cause for a multitude of parts. This would contradict the principle that from the One only one can proceed, a principle the opponents themselves accept. Thus the claim is invalid.

?

The Seventh Argument: Existence as a Universal Type
Al-Isfahani refutes a causal claim using type-sameness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that the existence of the First Intellect causes the sphere's form fails, by grasping the principle that all existences belong to a single type.

Core Idea
If one existence causes the sphere's form, all existences would.

Al-Isfahani now presents a crisp argument against a specific causal claim. He shows that if the existence of the First Intellect were the cause of the sphere's form, then every existence would have to be such a cause, which is obviously false. This argument relies on the logical principle that the judgment of a thing applies to its like.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the argument starts by stating the opponent's claim, then rejects it, then gives a reason.
The reason uses the key phrase 'حكم الشيء حكم مثله'.
2
Identify the logical principle
The argument hinges on the principle that 'the judgment of a thing is the judgment of its like'. Understand that this means if two things are of the same type, they share the same rulings.
3
Follow the reductio ad absurdum
The argument uses the principle to show that if the First Intellect's existence causes the sphere's form, then every existence would cause it (or a similar form). Since that is impossible, the original claim is false.
This is a classic reductio argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة السابعة : إنكم جعلتم موجود العقل الأول علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى ، وذلك باطل . لأن الوجودات من حيث إنها وجودات أشخاص ، داخلة تحت نوع واحد . وحكم الشيء حكم مثله ، فيلزم أن يكون وجود كل شيء علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى (١) ، أو لمثل تلك الصورة . وهو محال .
The seventh argument: You have made the existence of the First Intellect a cause for the form of the outermost sphere, but that is false. Because existences, insofar as they are existences of individuals, fall under a single type. And the judgment of a thing is like the judgment of its like. So it would follow that the existence of everything would be a cause for the form of the outermost sphere (1) or for the like of that form. And that is impossible.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh argument, which attacks the claim that the existence of the First Intellect is the cause for the form of the outermost sphere. The argument reasons that all existences, as individual existences, belong to one and the same type (i.e., existence as such). By the established principle that 'the judgment of a thing applies to its like', if the existence of the First Intellect is a cause for the sphere's form, then every existence would equally be a cause for that sphere's form or a similar form. This is clearly absurd, because many things exist without causing the sphere's form. Therefore, the original claim is false.

Key Terms
موجود
عقل أول
صورة الفلك الأقصى
وجودات
نوع واحد
حكم الشيء حكم مثله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The First Intellect's existence is special and unique, so the principle of type-sameness does not apply.
Correction: The argument emphasizes that existence as a type is common to all existents; the principle applies to all existences in respect of their existence, not their specific differences.

Checkpoint
What does the seventh argument use to refute the claim that the existence of the First Intellect causes the sphere's form?
A) The First Intellect is unique
B) The principle that the judgment of a thing applies to its like
C) The sphere does not exist
D) The existence is an accident

Key Takeaway
If all existences share the type 'existence', then the causal power of one existence would have to be shared by all, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that if one existence caused something, all existences would cause it. Do you ever treat certain people or actions as 'special exceptions' to how you judge others? How can you apply the principle of consistency from this lesson to your own reasoning?
?
When you attribute outcomes to specific causes (e.g., 'my effort caused this success'), do you consider that similar causes might be present elsewhere? What would it mean for your humility if you realized that no single existence has exclusive causal power?
?
The principle 'the judgment of a thing applies to its like' is about fairness in reasoning. Where in your life have you been inconsistent, holding others to a standard you don't apply to yourself? Commit to one change based on this lesson.

The Eighth Argument: Independence of the Cause from the Essence
Al-Isfahani argues that the alleged cause must be independent of the possible essence, contradicting its being a property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the eighth argument uses the opponent's own principle of independence to show that the alleged cause of the sphere cannot be a property of the possible essence.

Core Idea
If a cause is independent in act, it must be independent in essence—contradicting it being a property.

Al-Isfahani now deploys a powerful reductio ad absurdum argument. He takes the opponent's own doctrine—that independence in act implies independence in essence—and turns it against them. By analyzing two possible ways the alleged existence could cause the sphere, he shows that each leads to an impossibility, ultimately forcing the conclusion that the existence cannot be a mere property of the essence.

Guided Path
1
Recap the opponent's principle
Notice that the argument begins by stating the opponent's own rule: 'whatever is independent in its act from another is independent in its essence from that other.' This is the key premise.
This principle is not challenged; it is used as a weapon.
2
Understand the two alternatives
The argument presents two possibilities for the causation: with or without participation of the possible essence. Read each case carefully and see why the first is impossible according to the opponent's own view.
In the first case, the recipient becomes part of the agent, which they deny.
3
Follow the consequence of the second case
If causation is without participation, the existence is independent in act. By the opponent's principle, it must then be independent in essence—making it self-subsistent.
But the opponent claimed it was a property (صفة) of the essence, which is a contradiction.
4
Identify the contradiction
The argument ends with 'this is a contradiction' (خلف). See how the self-subsistent existence cannot be a property, so the initial assumption that it is a property must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثامنة : مذهبهم أن ما كان غنياً في فعله عن الغير ، كان غنياً في ذاته عن ذلك الغير. فنقول : كون ذلك الوجود علة لصورة الفلك الأقصى إما أن يكون بمشاركة من تلك الماهية الممكنة أو لا يكون . فإن كان الأول فقد صار القابل جزءاً من الفاعل ، وهو عندكم محال . وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ ذلك الوجود غني في ذلك التأثير عن تلك الماهية ، فوجب كونه غنياً في نفسه عن تلك الماهية ، فذلك الوجود أمر قائم بنفسه ، غني عن تلك الماهية . وقد فرضناه صفة لتلك الماهية . هذا خلف
The eighth argument: Their doctrine is that whatever is independent in its act from another is independent in its essence from that other. We say: That existence being a cause for the form of the outermost sphere is either with the participation of that possible essence or not. If the first, then the recipient has become a part of the agent, and according to you that is impossible. If the second, then that existence is independent in that effect from that essence, so it must be independent in its essence from that essence. Thus that existence is a self-subsistent thing, independent of that essence, while we have supposed it to be a property of that essence. This is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the eighth argument, which uses the opponent's own principle about independence. The argument says: If the existence (which is supposed to be a property of the possible essence) is the cause for the sphere's form, then there are two possibilities. First, this causation occurs with the participation of the possible essence—meaning the essence shares in the act. But then the recipient (the essence) becomes part of the agent, which the opponents consider impossible. Second, the causation occurs without the essence's participation. In that case, the existence is independent of the essence in its act. By the opponent's own rule, whatever is independent in act is also independent in essence. Therefore, the existence would be self-subsistent and independent of the essence, contradicting the assumption that it is a property (صفة) of that essence. Thus the claim leads to a contradiction.

غني في ذاته
مشاركة
ماهية ممكنة
قابل
فاعل
قائم بنفسه

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the existence could be an accident (صفة) of the essence while still being a cause independent in act.
Correction: The argument shows that if it is independent in act, it must be independent in essence, which is incompatible with being a property.

Checkpoint

B) It cannot be a property of the essence
C) It is the same as the essence
D) It is non-existent

Key Takeaway
The claimed cause of the sphere cannot be a property of the possible essence, because the very act of causation forces it to be independent and self-subsistent, creating a logical contradiction.

The lesson shows that if something is independent in act, it must be independent in essence. Consider the things you rely on daily—your job, your health, your family. Do you unwittingly treat them as independent causes, forgetting their total dependence on Allah? What practical step will you take today to shift your trust to Allah alone, the only truly independent being?
?
The opponent's own principle was used to refute them. Are you willing to apply rigorous logical scrutiny to your own theological beliefs, even if it leads to uncomfortable conclusions? What belief have you held that you might need to re-examine in light of logical consistency, as demonstrated in this argument?
?

The Ninth Argument: Three Entities from the First Cause
Al-Isfahani dismantles the causal claim via a trilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Isfahani's ninth argument shows that making necessity-by-other a cause leads to three emanations, contradicting the principle of 'only one from the One'.

Core Idea
Three emanations from the One contradict divine simplicity.

Al-Isfahani presents his ninth argument against the opponents' causal claim. By dissecting the first effect into three distinct aspects—possibility, existence, and being necessary by another—he exposes a dilemma: if this third aspect is not a separate entity, it cannot act as a cause; if it is separate, then it itself requires a cause, resulting in three existences emanating from the First Principle. This would violate the fundamental principle that from the One only one can proceed.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Let the argument's structure sink in before analyzing.
Notice the concise and logical progression.
2
Identify the Three Aspects
The first effect is said to have three aspects: possibility, existence, and being necessary by another. Highlight them in the text.
3
Understand the Dilemma
Consider the third concept: if it is not a distinct existent, it cannot be a cause; if it is a distinct existent, it is possible and needs a cause. Trace this reasoning.
This is the core logical move.
4
See the Consequence
If the third concept is a distinct existent, then together with possibility and existence, three distinct existences issue from the First Principle.
5
Connect to the Main Principle
The conclusion undermines the opponents' causal chain because multiple emanations contradict the principle 'from the One only one proceeds'.
Reflect on why this is a powerful refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أنتم جعلتم وجوب ذلك الوجود بالغير، علة للعقل الثاني - فنقول : المعلول الأول له إمكان وله وجود ، وله كونه واجباً بالغير . وهذا المفهوم الثالث إن لم يكن موجوداً ثالثاً (٢) ، امتنع جعله علة للعقل الأول . وإن كان موجوداً ثالثاً فهو ممكن. وله علة ولا علة له إلا المبدأ الأول . فقد صدر عن المبدأ الأول موجودات ثلاثة : الإمكان ، والوجود ، وكونه واجباً بالغير
The ninth argument: You have made the necessity of that existence by another a cause for the second intellect. We say: The first effect has possibility, has existence, and has its being necessary by another. This third concept, if it is not a third existent (2), then it is impossible to make it a cause for the first intellect. And if it is a third existent, then it is possible. And it has a cause, and it has no cause except the First Principle. So from the First Principle three existents have issued: possibility, existence, and its being necessary by another.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth argument. The opponents claim that the necessity-by-other (الوجوب بالغير) of the first effect's existence is the cause for the second intellect. The argument analyzes the first effect, which has three aspects: its possibility (إمكان), its existence (وجود), and its being necessary-by-other (كونه واجباً بالغير). The third aspect is a distinct concept. If it is not a distinct existent, then it cannot serve as a cause. If it is a distinct existent, then it is itself possible and requires a cause, which can only be the First Principle. Consequently, from the First Principle three distinct existences would emanate: possibility, existence, and necessity-by-other. But this contradicts the principle that from the One only one can proceed. Thus the claim is invalid.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third concept (being necessary by another) is merely an attribute of the first effect, not a separate entity.
Correction: The argument treats it as a distinct concept that must have an extra existence to function as a cause. If it were just an attribute, it could not cause anything.

The Tenth Argument: The Dilemma of Necessity-by-Other
Al-Isfahani argues that necessity-by-other leads to infinite regress or cannot be a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dilemma of necessity-by-other and why it cannot serve as a cause for the second intellect.

Core Idea
Necessity-by-other cannot survive ontological scrutiny — either it regresses or it fades into a mere relation.

The tenth argument presents a tight logical dilemma. Al-Isfahani asks a simple but devastating question: is 'necessity-by-other' a real, distinct thing or not? Both answers seem to lead to trouble. This passage will show why the concept cannot play the causal role assigned to it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dilemma structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that it presents two mutually exclusive possibilities: either necessity-by-other is a distinct entity or it is not. Each leads to a problematic conclusion.
The argument is a classical dilemma — it forces a choice between two bad options.
2
Follow the regress branch
If necessity-by-other is a distinct existent, then it itself is possible-in-itself and necessary-by-other. This creates a new necessity-by-other, leading to an infinite regress. Trace this chain carefully.
The regress is vicious because it never terminates.
3
Consider the alternative branch
If necessity-by-other is not a distinct existent, then it is merely a relational concept. But a mere relation cannot be a real cause (e.g., for the second intellect). Understand why this second horn is also fatal.
The conclusion: either way, necessity-by-other cannot function as a cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : الوجوب بالغير لو كان موجوداً مغايراً للأثر وللمؤثر . فهذا أيضاً ممكن لذاته ، واجب بغيره، فوجوبه بغيره أيضاً موجود آخر ولزم التسلسل . وإذا لم يكن الوجوب بالغير موجوداً مغايراً ، امتنع جعله علة ( للعقل الثاني ( (٣) .
The tenth argument: Necessity by another—if it were an existent distinct from the effect and from the agent—then this also is possible in itself, necessary by another. So its necessity by another would also be another existent, and an infinite regress would follow. And if necessity by another is not an existent distinct [from them], then it is impossible to make it a cause for the second intellect (3).
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents the tenth argument, which is a dilemma. The concept of 'necessity-by-other' (الوجوب بالغير) is either a distinct existent or not. If it is a distinct existent that is not identical to the effect or the agent, then it is itself possible in itself and necessary by another. This would require another 'necessity-by-other' for it, leading to an infinite regress (تسلسل). If it is not a distinct existent, then it is merely a relational concept and cannot serve as a real cause for the second intellect. Either way, the claim that necessity-by-other is the cause for the second intellect fails.

Key Terms
وجوب بالغير
موجود مغاير
أثر
مؤثر
ممكن لذاته
واجب بغيره
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That necessity-by-other is simply a property and does not require ontological analysis.
Correction: The argument shows that its ontological status matters: if it is a real entity, it leads to regress; if not, it cannot be a cause.

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma presented in the tenth argument?
A) Necessity-by-other is either eternal or created
B) Necessity-by-other is either distinct, leading to regress, or not distinct, and thus cannot be a cause
C) Necessity-by-other is either necessary or contingent
D) Necessity-by-other is either a substance or an accident

Key Takeaway
The concept of necessity-by-other fails as a real cause because it either leads to infinite regress or is merely a relation.

?
You have seen that infinite regress is impossible. Yet do you ever live as if your desires or worries are endless chains? How can this truth that every chain must end in a First Cause change how you prioritize your time and attachments?

The Sphere's Threefold Composition: Matter, Corporeal Form, and Specific Form
Al-Isfahani enumerates the sphere's three components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the celestial sphere, according to al-Isfahani, is not merely matter and corporeal form but also possesses a specific form, and why this distinction matters for arguments about causality.

Core Idea
The sphere is more than body; it has a specific form that complicates causal explanations.

In this short but crucial passage, al-Isfahani presents the eleventh argument concerning the celestial sphere. He insists that the sphere is not simply a composite of matter and corporeal form; it also includes a specific form. This seemingly simple point has significant implications for the debate about the causes of the sphere's existence and properties. Let's break down what he means and why it matters.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own wording come through before you interpret.
Notice the negation first, then the affirmation.
2
Identify the three components
The passage explicitly lists three components: matter (مادة), corporeal form (صورة جسمية), and specific form (صورة نوعية). Find each term and note the order.
Corporeal form gives dimension; specific form makes it a sphere.
3
Consider the implication for causality
Think: if the sphere has three components, any causal account must address all three. The opponents' claim that only matter and form are caused may be insufficient.
This argument likely sets up a further critique.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh argument, which is incomplete in the available text. The implication is that if the opponents claim that the possibility or existence of the First Intellect is the cause for the sphere's matter and form, they must also account for the specific form, which is a distinct element. This complicates the causal claim and suggests it cannot be reduced to a single cause. The argument likely continues to show that this leads to further problems.

The Ninth Proof: Necessity by Another and Multiple Emanations
Refutes the third aspect as a cause for emanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' concept of 'necessity by another' in the first effect leads to a contradiction: either it is not a distinct existent (so cannot be a cause) or it is distinct (so multiple things proceed from the One).

Core Idea
If necessity by another is a distinct existent, multiple emanations follow.

The Ninth Proof targets the philosophers' claim that the first effect (the First Intellect) has three aspects: possibility, existence, and being necessary by another. The author shows that the third concept cannot serve as a cause for the next emanation unless it is a distinct existent, but if it is distinct, then three existents emanate from the First Principle, contradicting the principle that only one can proceed from the One.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the full Arabic text once, then the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if it is not... if it is...'.
The passage is a concise argument; take note of the three aspects of the first effect.
2
Identify the three aspects
The first effect has possibility (imkān), existence (wujūd), and being necessary by another (kawnuhu wājiban bil-ghayr). The third is the focus.
3
Follow the conditional argument
The argument presents two cases: if the third concept is not a distinct existent, it cannot be a cause; if it is distinct, then it is possible and needs a cause, leading to three emanations.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.
4
Understand the consequence
If three existents proceed from the First Principle, the philosophers' axiom that 'from the One only one proceeds' is violated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : أنتم جعلتم وجوب ذلك الوجود بالغير، علة للعقل الثاني - فنقول : المعلول الأول له إمكان وله وجود ، وله كونه واجباً بالغير . وهذا المفهوم الثالث إن لم يكن موجوداً ثالثاً ، امتنع جعله علة للعقل الأول . وإن كان موجوداً ثالثاً فهو ممكن. وله علة ولا علة له إلا المبدأ الأول . فقد صدر عن المبدأ الأول موجودات ثلاثة : الإمكان ، والوجود ، وكونه واجباً بالغير
The Ninth Proof: You have made the necessity of that existence by another a cause for the second intellect. So we say: The first effect has possibility, existence, and its being necessary by another. This third concept, if it is not a third existent, it is impossible to make it a cause for the first intellect. And if it is a third existent, then it is possible. It has a cause, and it has no cause except the First Principle. Then from the First Principle have emanated three existents: possibility, existence, and its being necessary by another.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This is an argument (hujja) that refutes the philosophers' claim that the first effect (the First Intellect) has three aspects: possibility, existence, and being necessary by another. The author argues that the third concept, if it is not a distinct existent, cannot be a cause. If it is a distinct existent, then it is possible and needs a cause—so from the First Principle three things would have emanated, contradicting the principle that only one can proceed from the One.

Correction: The argument does not deny that God is necessary by Himself; it critiques the philosophers' use of 'necessity by another' as a distinct cause for emanation.

The Tenth Proof: The Regress of Necessity by Another
Dilemmatic argument against necessity by another as a distinct existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reifying 'necessity by another' as a distinct entity leads to an infinite regress, and why the philosophers' concept of a self-necessitating cause is untenable.

Core Idea
Necessity by another cannot be a separate existent without causing an infinite chain.

In this proof, the author refutes the notion that 'necessity by another' is a distinct existent that could serve as a cause. The argument shows that if it were distinct, it would itself require necessity by another, leading to an infinite regress. If it is not distinct, it cannot be a cause. This dilemma undermines the philosophers' causal model.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation once without analysis. Notice the structure: hypothesis, consequence, conclusion.
The passage is short but dense.
2
Understand the dilemma
Identify the two cases: (1) necessity by another as a distinct existent leads to infinite regress; (2) if not distinct, it cannot be a cause.
The author uses a logical dilemma: either it is distinct or it is not; both lead to problems.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this argument refutes the philosophers' idea of a self-necessitating cause (the second intellect). The proof shows that 'necessity by another' cannot be a separate entity underlying causation.
This is a technical argument against reification of a concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة العاشرة : الوجوب بالغير لو كان موجوداً مغايراً للأثر وللمؤثر . فهذا أيضاً ممكن لذاته ، واجب بغيره، فوجوبه بغيره أيضاً موجود آخر ولزم التسلسل . وإذا لم يكن الوجوب بالغير موجوداً مغايراً ، امتنع جعله علة للعقل الثاني
The Tenth Proof: The necessity by another, if it were an existent distinct from the effect and the effecter. Then this also is possible in itself, necessary by another, so its necessity by another is another existent, and an infinite regress follows. And if the necessity by another is not a distinct existent, then it is impossible to make it a cause for the second intellect.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This argument refutes the concept of 'necessity by another' as a distinct entity. If it is a distinct existent, then it itself would be possible in itself and necessary by another, leading to another necessity by another, ad infinitum. If it is not distinct, then it cannot be a cause. Thus the philosophers cannot have it as a cause.

Key Terms
وجوب بالغير
تسلسل
مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'necessity by another' is an actual entity or attribute that exists independently.
Correction: This argument shows that if it were a distinct existent, it would lead to infinite regress; if not distinct, it cannot be a cause. So it cannot be a real separate entity.

Checkpoint
According to the Tenth Proof, what happens if necessity by another is a distinct existent?
A) It becomes self-necessary.
B) It leads to an infinite regress of necessities.
C) It is impossible for it to be a cause.
D) It makes the effect necessary by itself.

Key Takeaway
Reifying 'necessity by another' as a distinct entity creates an infinite regress, undermining the philosophers' causal scheme.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on something in your life (e.g., a job, a skill, a relationship) to give you stability, do you unconsciously treat it as if it were an independent source of necessity, separate from the Creator? How does the lesson's warning against reifying 'necessity by another' challenge you to realign your trust?
?
The proof shows that reifying a cause leads to an endless chain. Think of a fear or anxiety you have about the future—what created thing are you treating as a 'distinct necessity' that must exist for your well-being? Will you today consciously return that thing to its status as merely possible and contingent, and place your ultimate reliance only on the Self-Necessary?
?
If 'necessity by another' cannot be a separate cause, then nothing in creation carries independent causal power. How does this truth confront the way you explain your successes and failures? Will you from now on see every secondary cause as a mere veil, and the Real Agent as the only One who truly acts?

The Sphere's Many Categories Challenge Emanation
Argument that the sphere's many categories cannot proceed from three aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Eleventh Proof exposes an inconsistency in the philosophers' emanation scheme by showing that the sphere's multiple categories cannot proceed from the first intellect's three aspects.

Core Idea
The sphere's many categories cannot come from just three aspects—showing a flaw in the emanation theory.

This proof focuses on the celestial sphere, which possesses numerous distinct properties spanning all ten Aristotelian categories. The philosophers claim that only three aspects emanate from the first intellect. But if the sphere has far more aspects, then either many things come from one (contradicting their own principle) or their scheme collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the list of the sphere's components and the logical conclusion.
The proof is structured as a reductio ad absurdum.
2
Identify the sphere's multiple aspects
The text enumerates several constituents: matter, corporeal form, celestial form, quantity, shape, position. These come from all ten categories.
3
Understand the three aspects of the first intellect
The philosophers claim the first intellect only has three aspects: possibility, existence, and necessity by another. These are supposed to explain all multiplicity.
4
See the contradiction
The sphere has many more than three aspects. So if the three aspects produce many, then from one (First Principle) could many come—contradicting their denial. If not, the sphere cannot be explained.
This two‑pronged argument forces an inconsistency.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The proof shows the emanation scheme cannot account for the sphere's complexity without violating the principle 'from one only one proceeds.'
This strengthens the case for direct divine creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : الفلك ليس عبارة عن المادة والصورة الجسمية فقط . بل هو عبارة عن المادة وعن الصورة الجسمية ، وعن الصورة النوعية الفلكية ، وعن المقدار المعين ، وعن الشكل العين ، وعن الوضع المعين ، وبالجملة فله من كل مقولة من المقولات العشر نوعاً واحداً وأنواعاً كثيرة . فإذا وزعنا هذه الأشياء على الجهات الثلاثة الحاصلة في العقل الأول . وهي الإمكان والوجود ، والوجوب بالغير . لزم أن يصدر عن الشيء الواحد أكثر من الواحد . فإن جوزوا ذلك فلم لا يجوز ما مثله في المبدأ الأول ؟ وإن لم يجوزوا ذلك فكيف جوزوه ههنا ؟
The Eleventh Proof: The sphere is not merely matter and corporeal form. Rather it is matter, corporeal form, specific celestial form, determined quantity, specific shape, specific position. In sum, it has from each of the ten categories one kind and many kinds. So if we distribute these things over the three aspects present in the first intellect—namely possibility, existence, and necessity by another—then more than one would necessarily emanate from a single thing. If they allow that, then why do they not allow the like in the First Principle? And if they do not allow that, then how do they allow it here?
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This argument points out that the celestial sphere has numerous constituents spanning all ten categories. The philosophers claim that from the first intellect only three aspects proceed (possibility, existence, necessity by another). But the sphere requires many more aspects. If they say that from one thing many can proceed, then why not from the First Principle? If they deny it, then they cannot explain the sphere's multiplicity.

Key Terms
فلك
مقولة
جهات
أكثر من الواحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof argues that the sphere does not exist or has no reality.
Correction: The proof does not deny the sphere's existence; rather, it uses the sphere's undeniable multiplicity to expose an internal contradiction in the emanation scheme.

Checkpoint
What does the Eleventh Proof say about the sphere's relationship to the first intellect's three aspects?
A) The sphere can be explained by the three aspects alone.
B) The sphere has many aspects that cannot be reduced to three, leading to multiplicity from one.
C) The sphere is identical to the first intellect.
D) The sphere has no reality.

Key Takeaway
The sphere's irreducible multiplicity forces an inconsistency in the emanation scheme, as it cannot be explained by only three aspects without violating the 'one from one' principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the attributes of Allah, do you ever unconsciously limit His power by thinking He must follow the same cause-and-effect patterns we observe in creation? This lesson exposes the fallacy of imposing created constraints on the Creator. What would it look like for you to truly trust that He is unrestricted?
?
The philosophers assumed that the first intellect had only three aspects, and yet the sphere showed far more complexity. How often do you assume that you can fully comprehend the nature of divine action based on your limited reasoning? Are there areas where you have become comfortable with a 'miniature god'—a deity you can explain and predict? This lesson challenges that comfort. What would it mean for you to embrace the mystery of Allah's absolute sovereignty, even when it defies your logic?
?
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: an idea is shown to lead to contradiction. Is there a belief or habit in your own life that you hold onto even though it leads to inconsistency with your faith? For example, believing Allah provides yet worrying excessively? Praying for guidance yet refusing to let go of your own plans? Take one concrete inconsistency you identify, and commit to a specific action today to align your life with the truth you proclaim.

The First Intellect's Multiple Intelligibles
Argument establishing multiple intelligibles need a cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' view that the first intellect knows many things leads to the problem of multiple intelligibles requiring a cause.

Core Idea
Multiple acts of intellection in an immaterial substance demand a cause.

This lesson introduces the first part of the twelfth proof, where the author establishes that the first intellect, being immaterial, knows itself and all its effects, resulting in multiple forms of knowledge. The question then arises: what causes these multiple intelligibles?

Guided Path
1
Read the passage establishing the first intellect's knowledge
Read the Arabic passage from the start until 'فلا بد لها من علة' and its translation.
2
Understand the philosophers' principle
Note that for philosophers, intellect equals form equal to the intelligible. So different intelligibles mean different forms.
3
Recognize the need for a cause
Conclude that these multiple forms require an external cause because they cannot be self-explanatory.
This sets up the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : إن العقل الأول جوهر مجرد عن المادة ، وكل ما كان كذلك فإنه يعقل ذاته ، ويعقل جميع معلوماته، لما ثبت أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول وهذه العلوم صور كثيرة ، لأن عند الفلاسفة لما كان العقل عبارة عن صورة مساوية للمعقول في العاقل ، فإن كانت المعقولات مختلفة بالماهية كانت تعقلاتها صوراً متساوية ، لها في الماهية فتكون هذه التعقلات صوراً مختلفة ، فلا بد لها من علة. فإذا أسندناها إلى ماهية العقل الأول ، فتلك الماهية اقتضت الامكان . فلو اقتضت هذه التعقلات فقد صدر عن تلك الماهية معلولات . وإن أسندناها إلى المبدأ الأول فقد صدر عنه وجود العقل الأول ، فلو صدر عنه مع ذلك عقله لذاته ، لكان قد صدر عنه معلولين . فثبت : أن على كل التقديرات، الإشكال لازم على القوم . وإن العذر الذي ذكروه وعولوا عليه باطل
The Twelfth Proof: The first intellect is a substance abstracted from matter, and everything that is such knows its essence and knows all its known things, because it is established that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. And these sciences are many forms, because according to the philosophers, since the intellect is a form equal to the intelligible in the intelligent, if the intelligibles differ in essence, then their acts of intellection are equal forms, having in essence, so these acts of intellection are different forms. Thus they must have a cause. If we attribute them to the essence of the first intellect, that essence necessitates possibility. But if it necessitated these acts of intellection, then from that essence would have proceeded effects. And if we attribute them to the First Principle, then from Him has proceeded the existence of the first intellect. If from Him also proceeded its intellection of itself, then from Him would have proceeded two effects. Therefore it is established that in all estimations, the difficulty is necessary upon the people [the philosophers]. And the excuse they mentioned and relied upon is false.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This is the twelfth proof against the philosophers. The first intellect, being immaterial, knows itself and all its effects. But its acts of intellection (knowledges) are multiple forms. They need a cause. If we attribute them to the essence of the first intellect, that essence only necessitates possibility, not multiple forms. If we attribute them to the First Cause, then from Him would have proceeded two things (the existence of the intellect and its intellection), violating the principle of one-from-one. Thus the philosophers' explanation fails.

Key Terms
عقل أول
جوهر مجرد
تعقل
صورة
معلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first intellect's knowledge is simple and unitary, so no cause needed.
Correction: The passage argues that because it knows multiple distinct effects, its acts of intellection are multiple forms, demanding a cause.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what is the relationship between the intellect and the intelligible?
A) They are different substances.
B) The intellect is a form equal to the intelligible.
C) The intellect is the cause of the intelligible.
D) The intelligible is a form of the intellect.

Key Takeaway
The first intellect's multiple acts of intellection, due to its knowledge of many effects, are multiple forms that require a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your mind contains countless distinct thoughts and pieces of knowledge. Do you ever pause to consider that this multiplicity itself is an effect that needs a cause? What does that say about your own self-sufficiency?
?
The passage argues that knowledge of the cause leads to knowledge of the effect. In your daily life, do you actively trace the effects you experience back to their ultimate cause? How would your perspective change if you did?
?
You have many different 'intellections'—beliefs, ideas, and convictions. Are you aware that each of them is a created form in your mind? How does recognizing their dependency on a Creator shape your accountability for what you think and believe?

The Causal Impasse: Two Failed Explanations
Examination and refutation of two causal attributions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why both attributing the multiple intelligibles to the first intellect's essence and to the First Principle fail, and how this proves the philosophers' view inconsistent.

Core Idea
Neither the first intellect's essence nor the First Cause can account for multiple intellections without contradiction.

Having established that the multiple intelligibles of the first intellect require a cause, the author now examines two possible sources: the essence of the first intellect itself, and the First Principle. Both lead to problems, showing the philosophers' account is untenable.

Guided Path
1
Read the two attributions
Read the Arabic passage from 'فإذا أسندناها' to the end and its translation.
2
Attribution to first intellect's essence
Note that the essence only necessitates possibility, not actual multiple forms. If it did, it would produce multiple effects.
3
Attribution to First Principle
The First Principle produces the existence of the first intellect. If it also produced its intellection, that would be two effects, violating the one-from-one rule.
4
Conclusion
Both attributions fail; therefore the philosophers' excuse is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Twelfth Proof: The first intellect is a substance abstracted from matter, and everything that is such knows its essence and knows all its known things, because it is established that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. And these sciences are many forms, because according to the philosophers, since the intellect is a form equal to the intelligible in the intelligent, if the intelligibles differ in essence, then their acts of intellection are equal forms, having in essence, so these acts of intellection are different forms. Thus they must have a cause. If we attribute them to the essence of the first intellect, that essence necessitates possibility. Therefore it is established that in all estimations, the difficulty is necessary upon the people [the philosophers]. And the excuse they mentioned and relied upon is false.

Commentary
This is the twelfth proof against the philosophers. The first intellect, being immaterial, knows itself and all its effects. But its acts of intellection (knowledges) are multiple forms. They need a cause. If we attribute them to the essence of the first intellect, that essence only necessitates possibility, not multiple forms. If we attribute them to the First Cause, then from Him would have proceeded two things (the existence of the intellect and its intellection), violating the principle of one-from-one. Thus the philosophers' explanation fails.

The Objection of Only Existence Emanating
Objection about only existence emanating and initial refutation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosopher's objection that only existence emanates from the First Cause and the author's initial refutation by denying the exclusivity and turning the argument back.

Core Idea
The author denies that only existence emanates; the cause's relation to essence mirrors that to existence.

This passage presents a key objection in debates on divine causality: if God causes only existence, multiplicity must come from recipients. The author's response begins by rejecting the premise that only existence emanates, then turns a potential counterargument back on the opponent.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and Translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the objection and answer.
Notice that the text cuts off; the argument continues.
2
Identify the Objection
Find the philosopher's claim: only existence emanates from the First Cause, and multiplicity arises from recipients.
3
Analyze the Author's Response
The author says 'we do not concede' that only existence emanates. Then addresses a hypothetical: if the cause caused essence, essence would change; he turns this back on existence.
This is a dialectical move.
4
Reflect on the Argument Structure
Consider how the author uses the opponent's own logic against them, showing consistency in the causal relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : الصادر عنه هو الوجود ، وهو شيء واحد . والتعدد والتكثر إنما يحصل بسبب تعدد القوابل . فالجواب عنه : أنا لا نسلم أن الصادر عن العلة الأولى ليس إلا الوجود . قوله : لو كانت العلة علة للماهية ، لكان عند فرض عدم تلك العلة وجب أن تنقلب الماهية . قلنا : وهذا لازم عليكم في الوجود، فإنه لو كانت العلة علة للموجود ، لكان عند عدم
And as for the Second Question: which is his statement: 'What emanates from Him is existence, and it is one thing. Multiplicity and plurality only occur due to the multiplicity of recipients.' The answer is: We do not concede that what emanates from the First Cause is only existence. His statement: 'If the cause were a cause for the essence, then when the absence of that cause is supposed, the essence would have to change.' We say: This is incumbent upon you regarding existence, for if the cause were a cause for the existent, then when its absence...
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers and the author's response. The philosophers assert that from the First Cause only existence emanates, and multiplicity comes from the recipients. The author rejects this, saying he does not concede that only existence emanates. Then he addresses a follow-up point: if the cause caused the essence, then without the cause the essence would change (implying the essence is contingent). The author turns this back on the philosophers: the same applies to existence—if absence eliminates existence, then existence is contingent too. The text ends incomplete, but the argument likely continues.

Key Terms

الموجود

D) He says the recipients cause the emanation.

Key Takeaway
The author's initial refutation denies that only existence emanates from God, setting the stage for a fuller argument that the cause is related to both essence and existence.

?
The opponent argued that multiplicity comes from recipients, as if the diversity in the world is just a product of matter. ?
The author turns the argument: if the cause causes existence, then its absence would eliminate existence. The Twelfth Argument: First Intellect's Concepts
The author refutes emanation by showing the First Intellect would cause multiple effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the philosopher's theory of emanation is refuted by showing that the First Intellect's multiple concepts cannot be caused without violating the principle of 'from one only one proceeds.'

Core Idea
The First Intellect's multiple concepts force a dilemma that unravels emanation.

In this passage, the author presents the twelfth argument against the philosophers' emanation theory. He focuses on the First Intellect—a key emanation—and shows that its nature as a simple immaterial substance requires it to have multiple concepts, which cannot be explained without issuing multiple effects from a single cause. This directly challenges the core principle of emanation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the nature of the First Intellect
The passage begins by describing the First Intellect as an immaterial substance that knows itself and all objects of knowledge. Grasp that this knowledge is multiple.
According to philosophers, knowledge is an image equal to the known.
2
Identify the multiplicity of concepts
The author argues that because the objects of knowledge are different, the intellect's concepts are multiple and different forms. Recognize that these multiple concepts require a cause.
This multiplicity contradicts the simplicity of the First Intellect's essence.
3
Analyze the two attribution scenarios
The author tests two possibilities: attributing the concepts to the First Intellect's own quiddity, or attributing them to the First Principle (God). See why both fail.
The quiddity only entails possibility, not actual concepts; attributing to God would mean two effects from one cause.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that in all cases the philosophers face a dilemma, and their excuse is false.
This refutes the emanation theory's consistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية عشر : إن العقل الأول جوهر مجرد عن المادة ، وكل ما كان كذلك فإنه يعقل ذاته ، ويعقل جميع معلوماته، لما ثبت أن العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول وهذه العلوم صور كثيرة ، لأن عند الفلاسفة لما كان العقل عبارة عن صورة مساوية للمعقول في العاقل ، فإن كانت المعقولات مختلفة بالماهية كانت تعقلاتها صوراً متساوية ، لها في الماهية فتكون هذه التعقلات صوراً مختلفة ، فلا بد لها من علة. فإذا أسندناها إلى ماهية العقل الأول ، فتلك الماهية اقتضت الامكان . فلو اقتضت هذه التعقلات فقد صدر عن تلك الماهية معلولات . وإن أسندناها إلى المبدأ الأول فقد صدر عنه وجود العقل الأول ، فلو صدر عنه مع ذلك عقله لذاته ، لكان قد صدر عنه معلولين . فثبت : أن على كل التقديرات، الإشكال لازم على القوم . وإن العذر الذي ذكروه وعولوا عليه باطل
The twelfth argument: The First Intellect is a substance abstracted from matter, and everything that is like that knows its essence and knows all its objects of knowledge, because it has been established that knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. And these knowledges are many forms, because according to the philosophers, the intellect is an image equal to the intelligible in the knower. So if the intelligibles differ in quiddity, their conceptualizations are equal images having [difference] in quiddity. So these conceptualizations are different forms, which must have a cause. If we attribute them to the quiddity of the First Intellect, that quiddity entails possibility. So if it entailed these conceptualizations, then multiple caused things would have issued from that quiddity. And if we attribute them to the First Principle, then from Him issued the existence of the First Intellect, so if there also issued from Him [the First Intellect's] intellection of its essence, then two caused things would have issued from Him. So it is established that in all estimations, the difficulty is necessary upon the [philosophers]. And the excuse that they mentioned and relied upon is false.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the twelfth argument refuting the philosophers' theory of emanation. The author first describes the nature of the First Intellect as a simple immaterial substance that must know itself and all things. According to the philosophers, knowledge is an image identical to the object known. Since the objects of knowledge are multiple and different in essence, the intellect's concepts must be multiple and different. These multiple concepts require a cause. Two possibilities are examined: either they are caused by the quiddity of the First Intellect itself, but that quiddity only entails possibility (al-imkan), not actual concepts; or they are caused by the First Principle (God), but then from Him would issue both the existence of the First Intellect and its intellection—two effects from one cause. Both possibilities violate the principle 'from one only one proceeds'. The author concludes that the philosophers' excuse is false.

Key Terms
العقل الأول
الجوهر المجرد
المعقول
التعقل
الإمكان
الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the First Intellect's multiple concepts are not separate effects and thus do not pose a problem.
Correction: The author shows that the concepts are distinct forms requiring a cause, and both possible attributions lead to multiple effects from a single cause, violating the emanation principle.

Checkpoint
What does the author argue would happen if the First Intellect's conceptualizations are attributed to its own quiddity?

B) It would entail that only one effect issues from the First Principle
C) The quiddity only entails possibility, not multiple concepts
D) The quiddity would become multiple

Key Takeaway
The First Intellect's multiple conceptualizations refute the emanation theory because they cannot be caused without violating the 'from one only one' principle.

?
The philosophers' excuse was false because they refused to accept the implications of their own principles. What excuse do you make to avoid accepting a difficult truth from the Qur'an or Sunnah? Would you rather cling to your excuse than surrender to the truth?

Refuting the Objection: Only Existence Emanates
Author refutes the second objection by showing its internal inconsistency and the impossibility of multiple effects from one cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that only existence emanates from God leads to an impossibility and how the author defends that quiddities also have causal relation.

Core Idea
Each existence is distinct, so attributing all to the First Principle implies multiple effects from one cause.

In this passage, the author tackles a serious objection: if only existence flows from the First Cause, then multiplicity comes only from receptacles. The author dismantles this by showing that the same reasoning applies to quiddities, and even if we grant the premise, each possible thing has its own distinct existence. This leads to the conclusion that requiring multiple effects from a single simple cause is impossible, thus the objection fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: objection, then author's response.
2
Examine the first rebuttal
The author first refuses the premise, then uses an analogy with quiddity to show inconsistency.
3
Understand the concession
Even if we grant that the efficient cause causes only existence, each existence is unique. Otherwise one accident would subsist in many loci.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Distinct existences from one cause would mean many effects, which is impossible.
This refutes the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثاني : وهو قوله : الصادر عنه هو الوجود ، وهو شيء واحد . والتعدد والتكثر إنما يحصل بسبب تعدد القوابل . فالجواب عنه : أنا لا نسلم أن الصادر عن العلة الأولى ليس إلا الوجود . قوله : لو كانت العلة علة للماهية ، لكان عند فرض عدم تلك العلة وجب أن تنقلب الماهية . قلنا : وهذا لازم عليكم في الوجود، فإنه لو كانت العلة علة للموجود ، لكان عند عدم تلك العلة ، لزم أن ينقلب الوجود، فكل ما يذكرونه في العذر عن الوجود ، فهو أيضاً عذر عن الماهية ، سلمنا أن العلة الفاعلية ليست علة ، أن لا وجود ، إلا أن وجود هذا الممكن غير وجود ذلك الممكن . وإلا لزم قيام العرض الواحد بالمحال الكثيرة . فثبت أن وجود كل موجود مغاير لوجود الموجود الآخر . فلو حكمنا بإسناد الموجودات بأسرها إلى المبدأ الأول ، لزم أن يصدر عن العلة الأولى معلولات كثيرة ، وهو محال
As for the second question: it is his statement: 'What emanates from Him is existence, and it is one thing. Multiplicity and plurality occur only due to the multiplicity of receptacles.' The answer to it: we do not concede that what emanates from the First Cause is only existence. His statement: 'If the cause were a cause of the quiddity, then upon the assumption of the absence of that cause, the quiddity would have to change.' We say: This is also necessary for you regarding existence, for if the cause were a cause of the existent, then upon the absence of that cause, the existence would have to change. So everything they mention as an excuse for existence is also an excuse for quiddity. We grant that the efficient cause is not a cause except existence; however, the existence of this possible thing is not the existence of that possible thing. Otherwise, it would necessitate the subsistence of one accident in multiple loci. So it is established that the existence of each existent is different from the existence of the other existent. Therefore, if we judge that all existents are attributed to the First Principle, then multiple caused things would proceed from the First Cause, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its answer. The objector claims that from God only existence emanates—one thing—and multiplicity arises from the different receptive capacities of things. The author rejects the premise that only existence emanates, arguing that if the cause were a cause of existence only, then the same reasoning would apply to quiddity: if the cause caused existence, then the absence of the cause would nullify existence, just as it would nullify quiddity. So the excuse for existence also works for quiddity. Even if we grant that the efficient cause (al-illa al-fa'iliyya) only causes existence, each possible thing has a distinct existence; otherwise one accident would subsist in many loci. Thus existences are multiple. Attributing all existents to the First Principle would require many effects from one cause, which is impossible.

Correction: Each possible thing has its own distinct existence; therefore multiple effects from one cause is impossible.

Key Takeaway

Using the Philosophers' Own Principle Against Them
The author rejects the objection by citing the philosophers' own agreement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection by appealing to the philosophers' own acceptance of the principle 'from one only one proceeds'.

Core Idea
The philosophers' own agreement proves the impossibility of multiple effects from one cause.

In this final objection, the opponent asks a seemingly reasonable question: why can't all possible things emanate from the single First Cause? The author's response is clever—he points out that the philosophers themselves have already agreed that this scenario is false. This is not a dodge but a demonstration of consistency within their own framework.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the concise structure: objection then answer.
The passage ends with 'And God knows best'.
2
Understand the objection
The objector asks why it is not possible for all possible things to proceed from the one cause. This challenges the argument that only one effect can come from a simple cause.
3
Analyze the author's response
The author states that the philosophers themselves agree this division is false. He then says he only mentioned the proof because it is exactly the proof they rely on, so they cannot dispute it.
This is a dialectical move: using the opponent's own accepted principle.
4
Reflect on the strategy
Consider why this refutation is effective. The philosophers cannot reject the argument without contradicting their own commitment to 'from one only one proceeds'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السؤال الثالث : وهو قوله : لم لا يجوز أن يصدر عن العلة الواحدة جميع الممكنات ؟ قلنا : أنتم وافقتمونا على أن هذا القسم باطل . ونحن إنما ذكرنا الدليل ، لأجل أنه هو الدليل الذي عليه عولتم في إثبات هذا المطلوب فليس لكم أن تنازعونا فيه . والله أعلم
As for the third question: it is his statement: 'Why is it not permissible that all possible things proceed from the one cause?' We said: You have agreed with us that this division is false. And we only mentioned the proof because it is the proof on which you relied in establishing this demand, so you cannot dispute us regarding it. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage presents a brief third objection and its answer. The objector asks why it is not possible that all possible things (all possible existents) emanate from the single first cause. The author responds that the objector (the philosopher) has already agreed with the author that this division (the possibility of multiple effects from one cause) is false. In other words, the philosophers themselves accept the principle 'from one only one proceeds', so they cannot now argue against it. The author states that he only mentioned the argument (the preceding proofs) because it is the very proof the philosophers rely on to establish their claim (probably the emanation of the First Intellect). Therefore, the philosophers have no ground to dispute it. The passage ends with 'And God knows best'.

Correction: The author is not avoiding; he is using the philosophers' own accepted principle (from one only one proceeds) to show consistency. The objection would undermine their own system.

The Fourth Argument: Necessary Agency Leads to Absurdity
The author presents a reductio argument against necessary agency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author uses a causal chain argument to refute the doctrine that God is a necessary agent, by showing it would imply that the cessation of events entails God's non-existence.

Core Idea
If God were a necessary agent, the absence of effects would imply His absence.

This lesson unpacks the fourth argument against the idea that God acts without choice as a necessary agent. The author shows that if God's effects were necessary concomitants of His essence, then the disappearance of those effects would force us to conclude that God Himself has ceased to exist — an obvious absurdity. The argument relies on tracing the causal chain back to the First Cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a conditional, then an explanation of the implication.
The argument is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key logical move
The author assumes the philosophers' view that God is a necessary agent, then shows what would follow if events ceased. Pinpoint the implication: cessation of events → cessation of causes → cessation of First Cause.
The key term is 'mulazama' (implication).
3
Understand the causal chain
Follow the chain: events need a cause; if events cease, their cause must have ceased; that cause's cause must have ceased, and so on up to the First Cause. This chain is used to connect events to God.
This is an argument by reductio ad absurdum.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the premise (necessary agency) is false because it leads to an impossible consequence: the non-existence of God. Ask yourself: why is this a strong refutation?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الرابعة : لو كان تعالى موجباً بالذات ، لزم من ارتفاع هذه الحوادث وعدمها ، ارتفاع ذات الله تعالى وعدمه . وهذا محال . فذاك محال . بيان الملازمة : أن هذه الحوادث مفتقرة إلى سبب لا محالة ، فارتفاعها يكون لارتفاع سببها ، وارتفاع ذلك السبب يكون لا محالة لارتفاع أسباب تلك الأسباب ، ولا تزال تلزم هذه الاعتبارات عند التصاعد ، حتى تنتهي إلى العلة الأولى . فإذا كان الأثر من لوازم المؤثر ، ومن المعلوم بالضرورة أن ارتفاع اللازم يدل على ارتفاع الملزوم ولزم من ارتفاع هذه الحوادث ارتفاع أسبابها ومن ارتفاع أسبابها ارتفاع أسباب أسبابها ، وهكذا حتى تنتهي إلى العلة الأولى ، فيلزم الجزم بأن ارتفاع هذه الحوادث يكشف عن ارتفاع ذات العلة
The fourth argument: If the Exalted were a necessary agent by essence, then from the cessation and non-existence of these events would follow the cessation of the essence of God, the Exalted, and its non-existence. But this is impossible, so that [premise] is impossible. The explanation of the implication: These events are necessarily in need of a cause; their cessation is due to the cessation of their cause, and the cessation of that cause is necessarily due to the cessation of the causes of those causes, and these considerations continue ascending until reaching the First Cause. For if the effect is among the necessary concomitants of the effecter, and it is known by necessity that the absence of the concomitant indicates the absence of the concomitated, then from the cessation of these events follows the cessation of their causes, and from the cessation of their causes follows the cessation of the causes of their causes, and so on until reaching the First Cause. Thus it necessarily follows that the cessation of these events reveals the cessation of the essence of the Cause.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the view that God is a necessary agent (mujib bi al-dhat). The author argues that if God were a necessary agent, meaning His acting is without choice and His effects are necessary concomitants of His essence, then if temporal events (al-hawadith) were to cease to exist, this would necessitate the non-existence of God. But the non-existence of God is impossible; therefore the premise that God is a necessary agent is impossible. The explanation (bayan al-mulazama) traces the causal chain: events need a cause; if they disappear, their cause must disappear, and so on up to the First Cause. Since the effect is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of its cause, the absence of the effect indicates the absence of the cause. Therefore the disappearance of events would imply the disappearance of God's essence. This is absurd, so the doctrine of necessary agency is false.

Key Terms
موجباً بالذات
الحوادث
الملازمة
السبب
الأثر
اللازم
الملزوم
العلة الأولى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that events have no connection to God's essence, so their cessation is irrelevant to God's existence.
Correction: The argument assumes the philosophers' view that God is a necessary agent, meaning His effects are necessary concomitants of His essence. The author does not endorse this view; he uses it to show its absurd consequence. The argument targets those who hold this view.

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument uses the necessary connection between cause and effect to show that if God were a necessary agent, the end of events would force the absurd conclusion that God ceases to exist.
