Objection and Answer on Causal Necessity and God's Choice
Sanusi refutes an objection about causal necessity and affirms that God acts by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi refutes the objection that the non-existence of an effect does not necessitate the non-existence of its cause, and why this supports the doctrine that God acts by choice.

Core Idea
The absence of an effect does not prove the absence of God if He acts by choice.

This passage presents a critical objection to the proof that God is a free agent. The objector argues that the non-existence of the world does not force the non-existence of God; Sanusi turns this objection into a proof. By carefully analyzing the implications of causality, he shows that only a freely acting God avoids logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the structure of the objection and response become clear.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'necessity' and 'choice'.
2
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says that the non-existence of an effect does not necessitate the non-existence of its cause, but only reveals that the cause ceased earlier. Find this claim in the passage.
3
Sanusi's concession and turn
Sanusi concedes the objector's point as true, but then shows how it actually supports his argument. Notice how he uses the objector's logic to derive an impossibility for the necessary cause model.
The key is that if the world's absence indicates the cause's cessation, then for a necessary cause, God would have to cease—impossible.
4
Contrast with the free agent model
Sanusi then presents the alternative: if God acts by choice, the world is not a necessary concomitant of His essence, so its absence does not imply His absence.
This resolves the logical problem and confirms the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأولى. ومعلوم أن ذلك محال باطل، فوجب القول بأن تأثير العلة الأولى في هذه الآثار ليس على سبيل الوجوب واللزوم، بل على سبيل الصحة والاختيار، وذلك يدل على أن إله العالم فاعل بالاختيار لا موجب بالذات. فإن قالوا: نحن لا نقول إن عدم المعلول يوجب عدم العلة، بل نقول إنه يكشف عنه، بمعنى أنا نقول: لولا أن العلة عدمت أولا، حتى لزم من عدمها عدم هذا المعلول، وإلا لم يعدم هذا المؤثر. قلنا: الحق ما ذكرتم إلا أنه لا يقدح في غرضنا. فإنا نقول: إذا كان عدم المعلول يدل على أن العلة قد ارتفعت أولا، وقدمت أولا، حتى لزم من عدمها عدم هذه المعلولات.. فعدم هذه المعلولات يدل على أن تلك العلة الأولى قد ارتفعت أولا، وحينئذ يلزم المحال المذكور. أما إذا قلنا: إنه تعالى يؤثر في وجود العالم على سبيل الصحة والاختيار، فحينئذ لا يكون وجود هذه الآثار من لوازم ذاته، فلم يلزم من عدم هذه الآثار وارتفاعها عدم تلك الذات المخصوصة
The first. And it is known that that is impossible and false. So it is necessary to say that the effect of the First Cause upon these effects is not by way of necessity and inherence, but by way of validity and choice. And that indicates that the God of the world is an Agent by choice, not a necessitator by essence. For if they say: We do not say that the non-existence of the effect necessitates the non-existence of the cause; rather we say that it reveals it, meaning we say: If not that the cause had ceased first, so that from its non-existence the non-existence of this effect necessarily follows, otherwise this effect would not have ceased. We say: The truth is what you have mentioned, except that it does not prejudice our purpose. For we say: If the non-existence of the effect indicates that the cause was removed first and ceased first, so that from its non-existence the non-existence of these effects necessarily follows, then the non-existence of these effects indicates that that First Cause was removed first. And then the mentioned impossible follows. As for if we say: He, exalted, influences the existence of the world by way of validity and choice, then the existence of these effects is not among the necessary concomitants of His essence, so the non-existence of these effects and their removal does not necessitate the non-existence of that particular essence.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-and-answer pair concerning the relationship between cause and effect, leading to the conclusion that God acts by choice rather than by necessity. The objector claims that the non-existence (ʿadam) of an effect does not necessitate the non-existence of its cause, but merely reveals that the cause had ceased to act. Sanusi responds that this objection actually supports his argument: if the non-existence of effects indicates the prior cessation of the First Cause (al-ʿillah al-ūlā), then positing a necessary cause (mūjib bi-dh-dhāt) leads to the impossible consequence that the First Cause must cease for the world to be absent, which is false. Instead, if God acts by choice (fāʿil bil-ikhtiyār), the existence of the world is not a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of His essence, so the absence of the world does not entail the absence of God. Thus the objection fails to undermine the proof that God is a free agent.

معلول
فاعل بالاختيار
موجب بالذات
عدم
لزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection might be understood as a successful refutation of the proof that God is a free agent.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the objection actually supports his argument: if the absence of effects indicates the cause's cessation, then a necessary cause would be impossible, whereas a free agent avoids this problem.

Checkpoint

C) Because it demonstrates that the cause is eternal and unchanging.
D) Because it denies any relationship between cause and effect.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi demonstrates that the objection based on causal necessity actually strengthens the case for God acting by free choice.

The Six Proofs Refute Necessary Causality
Author concludes six proofs disprove necessary cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the author concludes the six proofs collectively demonstrate that the world's cause is a free agent, not a necessary cause.

Core Idea
The six proofs show the world's cause must be a free agent.

The author here steps back from the detailed proofs presented earlier to deliver a firm conclusion. He asserts that all six proofs together decisively refute the philosophers' claim that the cause of the world is necessary by essence. This sets the stage for the philosophers' counterarguments, which we will examine in subsequent lessons.

Guided Path
1
Read the author's conclusion
Read the single Arabic sentence carefully. Notice that it is a conclusion, not a new argument.
The six proofs are not repeated here; only their implication is stated.
2
Identify the opposing view
The author refutes the statement that the influence on the world is 'موجب بالذات' (necessary by essence) and affirms it is 'فاعل بالاختيار' (free agent).
This is the key doctrinal contrast.
3
Reflect on the role of this sentence
This sentence serves as a bridge: it ends the presentation of proofs and introduces the philosophers' objections. Understand that the author is confident in his case.
Be aware that objections will follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الدلائل السنة دالة على أن القول بأن مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار قول باطل · وقالت الفلاسفة : أما الحجة الأولى : فضعيفة . لأنا على تقدير أن نسلم أن العالم محدث ، وأن القول بحوادث لا أول لها باطل . إلا أنا نقول لهم لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العلة الأولى علة ، موجبة لذاتها وجود شيء ، هو فاعل مختار . ثم إن ذلك الفاعل المختار ، أحدث هذا العالم باختياره ؟ فإن بهذا التقدير تكون العلة الأولى موجبة بالذات، ويكون فاعل هذا العالم فاعلاً ، فيثبت أن هذا الدليل لا يفيد أن واجب الوجود لذاته فاعل مختار . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن تكون العلة الأولى موجبة بالذات ، ثم يصدر عنها المعلول في بعض الأوقات دون البعض ؟ ( وهو محال ) فنقول : وهذا أيضاً لازم في القادر ، فإن عندكم العالم صدر عن القادر المختار في بعض الأوقات دون البعض من غير مخصص البتة . فإن عقل ذلك فلم لا يعقل مثله في العلة الأولى ؟ والسؤال الثالث : إن صحة وجود العالم إما أن يكون لها أول ، وإما أن لا يكون لها أول ، والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن يقال : إن قبل ذلك الأول ما كانت الصحة الذاتية حاصلة ، فيلزم أن يقال : العالم كان ممتنعاً لعينه ، ثم انقلب ممكناً لعينه . وذلك محال . ولما بطل إثبات الأول لتلك الصحة ثبت أنه لا أول لها ، فيلزم أن يقال العالم صحيح الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فمع هذا القول يمتنع أن يقال : إن كون العالم أزلياً محال . وهذا يوجب سقوط هذه الحجة بالكلية .
These six proofs indicate that the statement that the influence on the world is necessary by essence, not a free agent by choice, is false. The philosophers said: As for the first proof, it is weak. For supposing we accept that the world is temporally originated and that the statement of temporally originated things with no beginning is false, yet we say to them: Why is it not permissible to say that the first cause is a cause, necessitating by its essence the existence of something that is a free agent? Then that free agent originated this world by his choice? For in this estimation, the first cause would be necessary by essence, and the agent of this world would be an agent, thus it is proven that this proof does not show that the Necessary Existence by essence is a free agent. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the first cause be necessary by essence, and then the effect emanates from it at some times and not others? (And that is impossible) We say: This is also necessary for the capable; for according to you, the world emanates from the capable free agent at some times and not others without any particularizer at all. If that is conceivable, then why is the like of it not conceivable in the first cause? The third question: The soundness of the existence of the world either has a beginning or has no beginning. The first is false; otherwise it would necessitate saying that before that beginning, the intrinsic soundness was not present, so it would necessitate saying that the world was impossible in itself, then turned possible in itself. That is impossible. When the establishment of a beginning for that soundness is invalidated, it is established that it has no beginning, so it necessitates saying that the world is sound in existence in pre-eternity. If this is the case, then with this statement it is impossible to say that the world being eternal is impossible. This necessitates the fall of this proof entirely.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage begins with the author's concluding statement that the six proofs (presumably presented earlier) demonstrate the falsehood of the claim that the efficient cause of the world is a necessary cause (موجب بالذات) rather than a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار). Then the philosophers respond with an objection to the first proof. The philosophers first grant, for the sake of argument, that the world is created and that infinite temporal regress is impossible. However, they propose a two-step model: the first cause (العلة الأولى) could be necessary by essence but produce a free agent, and that free agent then creates the world. In this scheme, the first cause is still necessary, and the creator is a free agent, so the original proof does not show that the First Cause itself must be free. They then raise a further point: even if the first cause is necessary, why can't its effect occur at some times and not others? Although this is considered impossible for a necessary cause in the philosophers' own system, they note that the Muslim position on the free agent faces a similar problem: the free agent chooses one time over another without an external particularizer. The philosophers then introduce a 'third question' (السؤال الثالث) concerning the possibility of the world's existence. They argue that if the world's possibility has no beginning, then the world is possible in pre-eternity, so it cannot be absurd to say it is eternal. This, they claim, undercuts the proof entirely. The passage thus presents a sophisticated philosophical challenge to the Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
موجب بالذات
فاعل مختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the author claim about the six proofs?
A) They prove the world is eternal.
B) They prove the cause is necessary by essence.
C) They prove that the view that the cause is necessary by essence is false.
D) They prove that the philosophers are correct.

Key Takeaway
The author asserts that the six proofs conclusively show the world's cause is a free agent, not a necessary cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the universe—the consistent laws of nature—do you see the impersonal operation of necessity, or do you see the free choice of a Creator? Which view changes how you face trials?
?
If you truly believed that every event in your life comes from a free, wise Agent rather than blind necessity, would you still worry about tomorrow? What would you need to let go of to live that conviction today?
?
The author calls the statement that the cause is necessary 'false' (قول باطل). Do you ever live as if God's hands are tied by natural laws or human free will? Confess one area where you have doubted His freedom to act, and commit to seeing His free agency there this week.

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
First Objection: A Necessary Cause Can Produce a Free Agent
Philosophers propose a two-step causal model to undermine the first proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first objection: a necessary first cause could produce a free agent who then creates the world.

Core Idea
A necessary first cause can still be compatible with a world created by a free agent.

The philosophers begin their critique by granting the basic premises of the first proof—that the world is created and that an infinite regress is impossible. However, they propose an alternative model: a necessary first cause that necessarily produces a free agent, who then freely creates the world. This, they argue, shows that the first proof does not force the First Cause itself to be a free agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' objection
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Note how they start by accepting some premises for argument.
They say 'على تقدير أن نسلم' (on condition we accept).
2
Identify the proposed causal chain
The philosophers suggest: First Cause (necessary) → something that is a free agent → world. Trace this two-step model.
The first cause remains necessary; only the intermediate agent is free.
3
Understand the conclusion
They conclude that the first proof does not prove that the Necessary Existence (واجب الوجود) is itself a free agent. The proof only shows that the ultimate cause of the world is necessary.
This undermines the intended conclusion of the first proof.

?

Second Objection: The Problem of Temporal Particularization
Philosophers challenge the temporal particularization of the free agent's act.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second objection: the free agent's choice of time for creation faces a similar difficulty as a necessary cause's temporal delay.

Core Idea
The free agent's temporal choice is as problematic as a necessary cause's temporal delay.

The philosophers raise a further objection: if a necessary cause cannot produce its effect at some times and not others (as is generally accepted), then why is the same not true for a free agent? They argue that the Muslim view—that a free agent creates the world at a specific time without an external particularizer—faces an analogous difficulty. This objection challenges the coherence of free agency if it is supposed to explain temporal origination.

Guided Path
1
Read the second objection
Read the Arabic passage. Notice it begins with 'ثم نقول' (then we say) introducing a new point.
The phrase 'وهو محال' in parentheses is likely the author's interjection.
2
Understand the question
The philosophers ask: why can't a necessary cause produce its effect at some times and not others? They acknowledge this is impossible for a necessary cause.
This is a standard Kalam position.
3
Grasp the analogy
They point out that the free agent's act of creation at a specific time (from eternity) also lacks an external particularizer. If that is acceptable, then the necessary cause's temporal delay should also be acceptable.
This is a tu quoque (you too) argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the timing of your own decisions—why you do something now rather than later—do you rely on external circumstances or an internal will? How does this passage challenge you to see your every act as dependent on Allah's eternal will, not on a self-sufficient free will? Are there areas in your life where you act as if your choices are self-caused, forgetting that Allah is the only true 'first cause'? What concrete step can you take today to acknowledge His sovereignty over your choices?
?
The philosophers point to an apparent irrationality in the free agent's temporal choice. In your personal relationship with Allah, do you find it troubling that Allah chooses to respond to your dua at certain times and not others? How does accepting His absolute sovereignty over time deepen your reliance on Him? Will you commit to trusting His timing even when you don't understand it, and how will you remind yourself of this daily?
?
If you truly believed that every moment of your existence is directly caused by Allah without any secondary cause, how would it change the way you plan your day? What specific habit could you adopt to live in constant awareness of His moment-to-moment creation? Will you start today by pausing before each action and mentally saying 'Bismillah' to acknowledge His immediate involvement?

Causality and Created Things

Third Objection: The Eternal Possibility of the World
Philosophers argue that eternal possibility of the world makes its eternity conceivable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' third objection: if the world's possibility is eternal, then its eternity is not impossible, undermining the proof of temporal origination.

Core Idea
Eternal possibility of the world implies its eternity is not impossible.

In their third and final objection, the philosophers target the notion that the world must be temporally originated. They argue about the possibility (صحة وجود) of the world: it either has a beginning or does not. They show that a beginning for possibility leads to an absurd change, so possibility must be eternal. If possibility is eternal, then the world's actual existence in pre-eternity is not impossible, and thus the proof that the world must be temporal collapses.

Guided Path
1
Read the third objection
Read the Arabic passage. Note that it is introduced as 'السؤال الثالث' (the third question).
This objection deals with the concept of intrinsic possibility (صحة ذاتية).
2
Understand the dichotomy
The philosophers present a dichotomy: the possibility of the world either has a beginning or does not. They then argue against the first option.
Follow the logical steps carefully.
3
Trace the refutation of a beginning
If possibility had a beginning, then before that beginning the world would be impossible in itself, which would then change to possible—an absurdity.
This is a standard Kalam argument against change in essential attributes.
4
Derive the conclusion
Therefore, possibility has no beginning (it is eternal). Thus the world could be eternal, and the proof that the world must be temporal is invalid.
The philosophers claim this undercuts the entire first proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهذه الدلائل السنة دالة على أن القول بأن مؤثر العالم موجب بالذات لا فاعل بالاختيار قول باطل · وقالت الفلاسفة : أما الحجة الأولى : فضعيفة . لأنا على تقدير أن نسلم أن العالم محدث ، وأن القول بحوادث لا أول لها باطل . إلا أنا نقول لهم لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن العلة الأولى علة ، موجبة لذاتها وجود شيء ، هو فاعل مختار . ثم إن ذلك الفاعل المختار ، أحدث هذا العالم باختياره ؟ فإن بهذا التقدير تكون العلة الأولى موجبة بالذات، ويكون فاعل هذا العالم فاعلاً ، فيثبت أن هذا الدليل لا يفيد أن واجب الوجود لذاته فاعل مختار . ثم نقول : لم لا يجوز أن تكون العلة الأولى موجبة بالذات ، ثم يصدر عنها المعلول في بعض الأوقات دون البعض ؟ ( وهو محال ) فنقول : وهذا أيضاً لازم في القادر ، فإن عندكم العالم صدر عن القادر المختار في بعض الأوقات دون البعض من غير مخصص البتة . فإن عقل ذلك فلم لا يعقل مثله في العلة الأولى ؟ والسؤال الثالث : إن صحة وجود العالم إما أن يكون لها أول ، وإما أن لا يكون لها أول ، والأول باطل وإلا لزم أن يقال : إن قبل ذلك الأول ما كانت الصحة الذاتية حاصلة ، فيلزم أن يقال : العالم كان ممتنعاً لعينه ، ثم انقلب ممكناً لعينه . وذلك محال . ولما بطل إثبات الأول لتلك الصحة ثبت أنه لا أول لها ، فيلزم أن يقال العالم صحيح الوجود في الأزل ، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك فمع هذا القول يمتنع أن يقال : إن كون العالم أزلياً محال . وهذا يوجب سقوط هذه الحجة بالكلية .
These six proofs indicate that the statement that the influence on the world is necessary by essence, not a free agent by choice, is false. The philosophers said: As for the first proof, it is weak. For supposing we accept that the world is temporally originated and that the statement of temporally originated things with no beginning is false, yet we say to them: Why is it not permissible to say that the first cause is a cause, necessitating by its essence the existence of something that is a free agent? Then that free agent originated this world by his choice? For in this estimation, the first cause would be necessary by essence, and the agent of this world would be an agent, thus it is proven that this proof does not show that the Necessary Existence by essence is a free agent. Then we say: Why is it not permissible that the first cause be necessary by essence, and then the effect emanates from it at some times and not others? (And that is impossible) We say: This is also necessary for the capable; for according to you, the world emanates from the capable free agent at some times and not others without any particularizer at all. If that is conceivable, then why is the like of it not conceivable in the first cause? The third question: The soundness of the existence of the world either has a beginning or has no beginning. The first is false; otherwise it would necessitate saying that before that beginning, the intrinsic soundness was not present, so it would necessitate saying that the world was impossible in itself, then turned possible in itself. That is impossible. When the establishment of a beginning for that soundness is invalidated, it is established that it has no beginning, so it necessitates saying that the world is sound in existence in pre-eternity. If this is the case, then with this statement it is impossible to say that the world being eternal is impossible. This necessitates the fall of this proof entirely.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage begins with the author's concluding statement that the six proofs (presumably presented earlier) demonstrate the falsehood of the claim that the efficient cause of the world is a necessary cause (موجب بالذات) rather than a free agent (فاعل بالاختيار). Then the philosophers respond with an objection to the first proof. The philosophers first grant, for the sake of argument, that the world is created and that infinite temporal regress is impossible. However, they propose a two-step model: the first cause (العلة الأولى) could be necessary by essence but produce a free agent, and that free agent then creates the world. In this scheme, the first cause is still necessary, and the creator is a free agent, so the original proof does not show that the First Cause itself must be free. They then raise a further point: even if the first cause is necessary, why can't its effect occur at some times and not others? Although this is considered impossible for a necessary cause in the philosophers' own system, they note that the Muslim position on the free agent faces a similar problem: the free agent chooses one time over another without an external particularizer. The philosophers then introduce a 'third question' (السؤال الثالث) concerning the possibility of the world's existence. They argue that if the world's possibility has no beginning, then the world is possible in pre-eternity, so it cannot be absurd to say it is eternal. This, they claim, undercuts the proof entirely. The passage thus presents a sophisticated philosophical challenge to the Ash'ari position.

Key Terms
صحة ذاتية
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers claim the world is actually eternal.
Correction: They only argue that its eternity is not impossible, not that it is actual.

Checkpoint
What conclusion do the philosophers draw from the third objection?
A) The world must be eternal.
B) The possibility of the world has a beginning.
C) The proof that the world must be temporal collapses.
D) The world was impossible in itself.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' third objection uses the timelessness of possibility to argue that the world's eternity is not impossible, thereby undermining the proof of temporal origination.

?
This objection shows that even logical proofs about God can be dismantled. Are you attached to your own understanding of God, or are you open to having your intellectual assumptions challenged? What does your resistance to deep theological questions reveal about your faith?

The Philosophers' Second Objection: The Problem of Equivalence
Philosophers argue that the same problem of preponderance applies to both the necessary cause and the free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers argue that the problem of preponderance without a particularizer applies equally to the free agent and the necessary cause, and how this challenges the second proof.

Core Idea
The problem of balancing equal alternatives plagues the free agent as much as the necessary cause.

In this passage, the philosophers turn the tables on the mutakallimun. Having earlier argued that a necessary cause cannot arbitrarily produce one of two equally possible effects without a particularizer, the philosophers now claim that a free agent (Qadir) faces exactly the same difficulty. This objection aims to show that the Ash'ari appeal to a free will does not escape the logical problem. We will examine the argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once carefully, then the English translation. Notice the key terms and the logical flow.
The argument is concise but dense.
2
Identify key terms
Highlight the terms: Qadir (capable), Mujib (necessary cause), Tarjih (preponderance), al-Mithlayn (the two equals), Murajjih (particularizer). Understand their roles.
These terms are central to the objection.
3
Understand the reasoning
Trace the philosophers' logic: they claim that the free agent cannot prefer one equal over another without a particularizer, just like the necessary cause. If the world's relation to all times and places is equal, then both agents face the same impossibility.
This shows the objection is about logical parity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثانية : فالكلام عليها : إن عين ما التزمتموه في الموجب، فهو بعينه لازم في القادر . فإنا بينا أن القادر لا يمكن أن يرجح أحد المثلين على الآخر، إلا المرجح . وإذا أثبتم أن نسبة حدوث العالم إلى جميع الأوقات على التسوية ، ونسبة حصول العالم إلى جميع الأحياز على التسوية ، فكما يمتنع وقوع بعض تلك الجائزات دون بعض بالموجب ، فكذلك يمتنع وقوعه بالقادر . فيثبت أن هذا الإشكال مشترك . والله أعلم
As for the second proof, the discussion about it: Indeed, the very same thing that you have committed to concerning the necessary cause is also necessary for the capable. For we have shown that the capable cannot prefer one of the two similar things over the other except with a preponderant. If you establish that the relation of the world's temporally origination to all times is equal, and the relation of the world's occurrence to all places is equal, then just as the occurrence of some of those possibilities without others is impossible for the necessary cause, so too its occurrence is impossible for the capable. Thus it is established that this problem is common. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection to the second proof (الحجة الثانية). They argue that the very problem that was raised against the necessary cause (موجب) applies equally to the free agent (قادر). The philosophers state that they have previously shown that a free agent cannot give preponderance to one equal thing over another without a preponderating factor (مرجح). If the relation of the world's occurrence to all times is perfectly equal, and similarly to all places, then just as it is impossible for a necessary cause to produce one possibility without another, it is also impossible for a free agent. Therefore, the problem is common to both positions. The objection aims to show that the Ash'ari thesis of a free agent does not escape the difficulty of specifying one time or place for creation without a determining factor.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers are denying that a free agent can act at all.
Correction: The philosophers are not denying freedom; they are pointing out that the problem of preponderance without a particularizer applies to both positions, showing that the Ash'ari appeal to free will does not solve the logical difficulty.

The philosophers argue that the problem of preponderance without a particularizer is not unique to the necessary cause; it also challenges the free agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Pause and ask yourself: in your last major decision, did you consciously acknowledge your dependence on Allah's guidance, or did you assume your own will was sufficient?
?
The philosophers here claim the problem is shared—both necessary cause and free agent face the same impasse. If that is true, then your ability to act is not ultimately your own achievement. Where in your life have you been proud of your own willpower, assuming you deserved credit for choosing rightly? Consider one area where you consistently make good choices: is it possible that you have been attributing that to yourself rather than to the particularizer who made it possible? What would change if you started each day explicitly asking Him to tip your scales toward what pleases Him?
?
This passage confronts you with a hard truth: even your capacity to choose is not self-sufficient. You cannot prefer one equal thing over another without a preponderant. So when you rationalize your sins as 'just a mistake' or 'I couldn't help it,' are you not claiming that your own will was somehow able to act without a particularizer? That is a lie you tell yourself. Repent now—recognize that every right choice is by His permission and every wrong choice reveals your absolute need for His continuous guidance. Will you humble yourself and beg Him to be your Murajjih every moment?

One Cause, Many Effects: A Response to the Philosophers
Author responds that one thing can be cause for many things, contrary to the philosophers' claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's position that one cause can produce many effects and how he refutes the philosophers' argument against it.

Core Idea
A single cause can produce many effects; the philosophers' proofs are weak.

The philosophers argued that one cause cannot produce many effects, which would limit divine power. The author counters that this is not impossible, and he dismisses the philosophers' proofs as very weak. This lesson examines his response and its implications for causality and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the gist of the author's response to the third proof.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the main claim
The author asserts that it is not impossible for one thing to be a cause for many things. This directly contradicts the philosophers' principle.
The author uses 'الأقرب' (the closest view) to indicate probability, not certainty.
3
Understand the refutation
The author dismisses the philosophers' proofs as very weak. He then notes that because the philosophers accepted this principle, they had to propose a specific emanationist scheme, which has already been refuted.
This refutation preserves God's power to create directly without intermediaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الثالثة : فالجواب عنها : أن الأقرب أنه لا يمتنع كون الشيء الواحد علة لأشياء كثيرة ، والدلائل التي يذكرها الفلاسفة في تقرير هذا الأصل ضعيفة جداً . وهذا هو الوجه المتحقق المعتمد وأما الفلاسفة . فإنهم لما سلّموا هذا الأصل ، لا جرم ذكروا طريقة في كيفية ترتيب الوجود . وقد سلف إبطالها .
As for the third proof, the answer to it: That the closest view is that it is not impossible for one thing to be a cause for many things, and the proofs that the philosophers mention in establishing this principle are very weak. And this is the verified and relied-upon view. As for the philosophers, since they accepted this principle, they necessarily mentioned a method for the arrangement of existence. And its refutation has preceded.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the third proof (الحجة الثالثة). The philosophers apparently argued that one cause cannot produce many effects. The author rejects this, stating that the closest (most probable) position is that it is not impossible for a single thing to be a cause for many things. He dismisses the philosophers' arguments for their principle as very weak. The author then adds a remark: since the philosophers accepted this principle (of one cause for many effects), they were compelled to propose a specific emanationist scheme for the order of existence—but its refutation has already been given earlier. This passage thus affirms the power of God to create multiple things directly, without the need for intermediaries.

The Free Agent and the Preponderating Motive
Author argues that the free agent together with a motive constitutes a complete cause, turning the objection back.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the philosophers' objection by demonstrating that the free agent's power and motive together constitute a necessitating cause, making the objection mutual.

Core Idea
Power plus motive equals a complete cause — turning the objection back on the objector.

This passage is the author's reply to the fourth proof brought by the philosophers. They argued that a free agent cannot choose between two identical options without a cause, which would imply arbitrariness. The author shows that in his school, the combination of power and motive does constitute a complete cause, so any criticism they level at those who affirm a necessary cause applies equally to them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the compact argument structure.
The passage contains three sentences: two premises and a conclusion.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms قادر, داعية مرجحة, القدرة, and موجب تام in the text. Understand what each refers to.
These are central to the argument.
3
Trace the logical move
Follow how the author uses the two premises to derive the conclusion that the objection applies to both sides.
This is a classical dialectical response (إلزام).
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Note that the author is not denying free will but showing that even free agency involves necessitation when a motive is present.
This avoids the misconception that the argument rejects divine free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : فالجواب عنها : أنا بينا : أن القادر لا يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر ، إلا لداعية مرجحة ، وبينا : أن القدرة مع الداعي يكون مجموعها موجباً تاماً لوجود الفعل. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ كل ما أوردتموه على القائلين بالموجب ، فهو لازم على القائلين بالمختار .
As for the fourth proof, the answer to it: We have shown that the capable cannot prefer one of the two similar things over the other except with a preponderating motive, and we have shown that the power together with the motive is a complete necessitating cause for the existence of the act. If this is the case, then everything that you have brought against the proponents of the necessary cause is also necessary against the proponents of the free agent.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the author's answer to the fourth proof (الحجة الرابعة). The philosophers had likely objected that the free agent cannot choose between equals without a cause. The author responds by stating that his school has already shown that a free agent cannot give preponderance to one equal over another without a preponderating motive (داعية مرجحة). Moreover, the combination of power (القدرة) and motive constitutes a complete necessitating cause (موجب تام) for the act. Therefore, any objection raised against those who affirm a necessary cause (the philosophers) applies equally to the proponents of a free agent (the Ash'arites). In other words, the philosophers' own reasoning turns back against them, since the Ash'ari account of free agency involves a necessitating cause and thus does not escape the problem.

Key Terms
قادر
داعية مرجحة
القدرة
موجب تام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author is denying free will and arguing that even God's actions are necessitated by an external cause.
Correction: The author is not denying free will; he is explaining that the free agent's action, when considered with the motive, is necessitated, but the motive is internal to the agent. Thus the objection from the philosophers is mutual and does not refute the Ash'ari position.

Checkpoint

B) He states that power and motive together constitute a complete cause, so the objection applies to both sides.
C) He argues that the free agent does not face the problem of equals.
D) He concedes the objection but says it does not affect God.

Key Takeaway
The free agent's power combined with a motive forms a complete cause, making the philosophers' objection against necessitation also apply to their own view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Cross-Referencing Arguments in Classical Texts
Author refers fifth proof to a separate discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical authors handle objections by directing the reader to a more detailed discussion elsewhere in the same work.

Core Idea
Not every proof is refuted on the spot – some are deferred.

In this brief passage, the author encounters a proof that a thing's persistence requires a cause. Instead of refuting it immediately, he directs the reader to a separate section where the topics of origination and non-existence are examined in depth. This lesson explores why authors use cross-references and what we can learn from this practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Note that the author mentions a 'fifth proof' and then says the topic is discussed elsewhere.
2
Identify the proof being referenced
The proof is that a thing in its state of persistence (بقاء) needs the influence (مؤثر) of a cause. The author does not argue against it here.
3
Understand the cross-reference
The author says the investigations are in a separate article (مقالة مفردة) and specifically in the sections on temporality (الحدوث) and non-existence (العدم). This is a common technique to avoid repetition.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think the author is conceding the proof, but he is simply postponing the discussion. This illustrates the importance of reading classical texts with an awareness of their organization.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
Test your understanding by answering the multiple-choice question based solely on this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الخامسة : وهي أنه يلزم افتقار الشيء حال بقائه إلى المؤثر . فاعلم أن المباحث المتعلقة بهذا الباب مذكورة في مقالة مفردة ، فذكروه في أبواب الحدوث والعدم .
As for the fifth proof, which is that a thing in the state of its persistence necessarily needs the influence (of the cause). Know that the investigations related to this topic are mentioned in a separate article, so they mentioned it in the sections on temporally origination and non-existence.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a brief answer to the fifth proof (الحجة الخامسة). The proof states that a thing, even after its origination, continues to need the causal influence (المؤثر) for its persistence. The author does not provide a detailed refutation here; instead, he directs the reader to a separate discussion where this topic is treated in depth, namely in the sections on temporally origination (حدوث) and non-existence (عدم). This is a common practice in classical texts where arguments are cross-referenced.

Key Terms
بقاء
مؤثر
الحدوث
العدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author accepts the proof and admits inability to refute it.
Correction: The author is not conceding the proof; he is simply postponing the discussion to a more appropriate place in the work.

Checkpoint
How does the author handle the fifth proof about persistence needing a cause?
A) He refutes it with a counter-argument.
B) He accepts it and changes his position.
C) He refers it to a separate discussion in other sections.
D) He dismisses it as irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Classical authors often use cross-references to avoid redundancy; a deferred argument is not a conceded one.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Shared Difficulty of Election: Necessitarian and Voluntary Agent
Argues that the difficulty of election is shared by necessitarian and voluntary agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the problem of specifying when and where the world was created is equally challenging for both the necessitarian and the voluntary agent, and how this refutes an objection against divine choice.

Core Idea
The difficulty of election is common to both causal necessity and divine choice.

In this passage, the author concludes a rebuttal to an objection that only the voluntary agent faces a problem of election. He shows that the necessitarian, who believes in a necessary cause, faces the same difficulty in choosing one time or place over another. This shared problem demonstrates that the objection does not uniquely challenge the view of a freely choosing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the literal translation. Notice the key terms: al-murajjiḥ (preponderator), al-mūjib (necessitarian), al-qādir (powerful agent).
The passage is concise; pay attention to the conditional structure.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The passage begins with a phrase from the objection ('Other than the preponderator'), then presents a conditional: if the world's occurrence is equally possible at all times and places, then both the necessitarian and the voluntary agent cannot specify one without a preponderating factor.
The comparison shows the shared difficulty.
3
Understand the refutation
The objection likely claimed that only a voluntary agent faces the problem of election. The author responds by showing that the necessitarian faces the same problem, so the objection fails to refute the voluntary agent's view.
The conclusion 'thus this difficulty is common' is the key point.
4
Reflect on the significance
This argument defends the possibility of divine choice against necessitarian critiques. It demonstrates that the need for a preponderator applies equally to both positions.
Consider how this strengthens the case for a freely choosing Creator.

الآخر، إلا المرجح . وإذا أثبتم أن نسبة حدوث العالم إلى جميع الأوقات على التسوية ، ونسبة حصول العالم إلى جميع الأحياز على التسوية ، فكما يمتنع وقوع بعض تلك الجائزات دون بعض بالموجب ، فكذلك يمتنع وقوعه بالقادر . فيثبت أن هذا الإشكال مشترك . والله أعلم

Thus it is established that this difficulty is common. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion of an answer to an objection. It begins with a phrase likely from the objection: 'Other than the preponderator.' Then the author argues that if one holds that the world's occurrence is equally possible at any time or place, then neither the necessitarian (who believes in a necessary cause) nor the voluntary agent (who acts by choice) can specify one time or place over another without a preponderating factor. Thus the problem of election is common to both positions, and the objection does not refute the voluntary agent's view. The author ends with 'And God knows best.'

Correction: The passage argues that both the necessitarian and the voluntary agent face the same difficulty, because in both cases a preponderating factor is needed to select one possibility over another.

Response to the Third Objection: One Cause Can Produce Many Effects
Answers the objection that one cause cannot produce many effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the correct view in kalam is that one cause can produce many effects, and that the philosophers' arguments for the opposite are weak.

Core Idea
One cause can produce many effects; the philosophers' proofs against it are weak.

In this passage, the author responds to the third objection that a single cause cannot produce many effects. He affirms that the closer view is that this is not impossible, and he dismisses the philosophers' arguments as very weak. He also notes that the philosophers themselves conceded this principle and built a system that has already been refuted.

Response to Fourth Objection: Power with Motive Necessitates
Answers the fourth objection by showing that power plus motive necessitates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari response to the fourth objection shows that power together with a motive constitutes a complete necessitating cause, making the objection backfire on the objector.

Core Idea
Power plus motive is a complete necessitator, not free choice.

In this passage, the author addresses the fourth objection raised against the necessitarian view. The objection claimed that if acts are necessitated, then free will is impossible. The author responds by showing that the voluntary agent's power, when accompanied by a preponderating motive, itself constitutes a complete necessitating cause. Thus, the same objection applies to the proponent of free will as well.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Notice that the author restates two previously established points: (a) a powerful agent cannot prefer one similar over another without a motive, and (b) power with motive is a complete necessitator.
3
Understand the conclusion
The author then concludes that the objection against the necessitarian applies equally to the advocate of free will.
4
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself why this response is considered a successful rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الحجة الرابعة : فالجواب عنها : أنا بينا : أن القادر لا يمكنه ترجيح أحد المثلين على الآخر ، إلا لداعية مرجحة ، وبينا : أن القدرة مع الداعي يكون مجموعها موجباً تاماً لوجود الفعل. وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ، فحينئذ كل ما أوردتموه على القائلين بالموجب ، فهو لازم على القائلين بالمختار .
As for the fourth proof: its answer is that we have shown: that the powerful agent cannot preponderate one of the two similars over the other except by a preponderating motive, and we have shown: that power together with the motive constitutes a complete necessitator for the existence of the act. And if the matter is so, then everything you have objected against the proponents of the necessitator is incumbent upon the proponents of the free choice.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage answers the fourth objection. The author first restates two points already established: (1) a voluntary agent cannot prefer one identical possibility over another without a preponderating motive, and (2) power combined with the motive forms a complete necessitating cause of the act. Therefore, the objection raised against the necessitarian (that an act cannot occur without a necessitating cause) also applies to the proponent of free will (al-mukhtār), because power plus motive is itself a necessitator. So the objection backfires.

Key Terms
الحجة الرابعة
القادر
الداعي
الموجب
المختار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The voluntary agent's act is completely free from any necessitating cause.
Correction: The passage shows that power together with a motive is a complete necessitator, so the act is necessitated by the internal state of the agent, not by an external compulsion.

Checkpoint

A) Power alone.
B) The motive alone.
C) Power together with the motive.
D) The absence of any obstacle.

Key Takeaway
Power plus motive forms a complete necessitating cause, so the objection against necessitarians applies to free will advocates as well.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Response to the Fifth Objection: Deferred Discussion
Defers discussion of an effect's need for a cause during its persistence to another chapter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that some theological objections require separate, more detailed treatment, and how the author uses methodological deferral.

Core Idea
Not every objection gets an immediate answer; some are deferred to their appropriate place.

In this brief passage, the author encounters the fifth objection, which argues that a thing needs a cause for its continued existence. Rather than giving a quick answer here, he does something important: he directs the reader to a separate discussion. This teaches us about the structured methodology of kalam works, where complex issues are handled in dedicated chapters.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once. Notice its brevity and the phrase 'فاعلم أن المباحث متعلقة بهذا الباب مذكورة في مقالة مفردة' which signals a reference to another section.
2
Understand the fifth objection
The objection claims that a thing (an effect) needs a cause not only for its origin but also for its continued existence (persistence). This is a key issue in causality.
This objection is about the need for a cause during 'baqā'' (persistence).
3
See the author's response strategy
Note that the author does not refute the objection here. Instead, he refers the reader to a separate treatise, specifically the chapters on origination (ḥudūth) and non-existence (ʿadam). This is a common practice in scholarly works to avoid digression.
4
Reflect on the methodology
Consider why the author would defer this discussion. This shows the importance of proper organization and the assumption that the reader will follow cross-references.
This approach helps keep each chapter focused on its main topic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the fifth proof: it is that the thing is necessarily in need of the effecting agent during its persistence. So know that the investigations related to this topic are mentioned in a separate treatise; thus mention it in the chapters on origination and non-existence.

Commentary
This passage is a brief response to the fifth objection, which asserts that a thing requires a cause for its continued existence (not just for its coming into being). The author does not provide a full refutation here but instead refers the reader to a separate discussion devoted to this topic, specifically in the chapters on origination (ḥudūth) and non-existence (ʿadam). This is a common kalam method of deferring detailed arguments to later sections.

Key Terms

Two Proofs for the Impossibility of Multiple Effective Causes
The author presents two arguments for taking certainty and dismissing doubt about multiple causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two arguments used to establish the impossibility of having more than one effective cause: a methodological principle of certainty and a theological proof from God's power.

Core Idea
Certainty over doubt, and God's power over all, lead to one cause.

In this passage, the author presents two distinct arguments that together rule out the possibility of multiple independent effective causes. The first is a principle of reasoning: we must hold to what is certain and set aside what is doubtful. The second builds on the prior proof of God's ability to create and originate, concluding that only one effective cause can exist. These arguments eliminate the need for intermediaries that might be thought to have causal power.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arguments
The passage states two 'ways' (طريقان) to prove the impossibility of multiple causes. Read the Arabic and find the beginning of each.
The first is introduced by 'دليل فوجب...' and the second by 'الثاني...'.
2
Analyze the first argument
The first argument is methodological: we must take what is certain (المتيقن) and discard what is doubtful (المشكوك).
Think about how this principle applies to the question of multiple causes: since multiple causes are doubtful, we reject them.
3
Analyze the second argument
The second argument builds on a prior proof that God is capable of origination. From that, we prove that it is impossible to have more than one effective cause.
Notice the logical sequence: first prove God's power, then deduce the impossibility of multiple causes.
4
Understand the conclusion
With these two proofs, the claim of intermediaries (وسائط) falls away. Only Allah is the effective cause.
This supports Islamic tawhid in causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دليل فوجب الأخذ بالمتيقن ، وطرح المشكوك المشتبه ، فهذا أحد الطريقين . الثاني : لما دللنا على أنه تعالى قادر على الإيجاد والتكوين ، فحينئذ نقيم الدلالة على أنه يستحيل في الوجود أن يكون أكثر من مؤثر واحد . وعند هذا يسقط القول بإثبات الوسائط. فهذا ما يمكن ذكره في هذا المقام
A proof: so one must take what is certain and reject the doubtful and ambiguous. This is one of two ways. The second: when we prove that He, exalted, is capable of origination and creation, then we establish the proof that it is impossible in existence to have more than one effective cause. And with this, the claim of affirming intermediaries falls away. This is what can be mentioned in this context.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments (two ways) for establishing the impossibility of multiple effective causes in existence. The first way is methodological: one must hold to what is certain and discard what is doubtful. The second way is theological: after proving that God is capable of origination and creation, we then prove that it is impossible for there to be more than one effective cause in existence. This eliminates the possibility of intermediaries (wasāʼiṭ) that might be thought to have independent causal power.

Key Terms
متيقن (certain)
مشكوك (doubtful)
مؤثر واحد (one effective cause)
وسائط (intermediaries)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The impossibility of multiple causes can be proven by pure logic alone, without any theological premises.
Correction: The second argument explicitly builds on a prior proof that God is capable of origination. Without this premise, the proof does not stand. The first argument is a methodological principle that also depends on recognizing certainty and doubt.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of multiple effective causes is established both by the principle of preferring certainty over doubt and by the theological proof from God's unique power.

First Argument: Perfect Act Possible from Ignorant Person
First argument: perfect act possible from ignorant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first argument in Al-Matalibul Aliyah demonstrates that a perfect, precise act can possibly originate from an ignorant agent, using examples and the rational principle of 'like ruling for like'.

Core Idea
A single perfect act does not necessarily prove knowledge in its agent.

This lesson presents the first of several arguments that challenge the necessary link between perfect action and knowledge. The author uses everyday examples—a non-poet speaking a perfect line, an illiterate person writing a perfect letter—to show that occasional perfect acts can occur without knowledge. Then a rational principle is invoked to generalize this possibility. This argument lays the groundwork for deeper discussions about causation and divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage once, noticing the examples and the logical flow. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the examples
The passage gives two cases where a perfect act comes from an ignorant person. Find them and understand why they are agreed upon as rare occurrences.
3
Understand the rational principle
The argument uses the principle 'the ruling of a thing applies to its like'. Reflect on how this principle extends the possibility from small perfect acts to larger ones.
4
Connect to the conclusion
The conclusion is that it is possible (ja'iz) for an ignorant person to produce a perfect act. Notice that this does not deny that God's acts are perfect, but only shows that occasional perfect acts do not require knowledge.
5
Answer the checkpoint
Test your understanding with the multiple-choice question about the rational principle cited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقن على سبيل الإتقان مرة واحدة . فالعاجز عن نظم الشعر قد ينطق على سبيل الإتقان بمصراع من الشعر والجاهل بالخط، قد يكتب حرفاً واحداً على الإحكام والإتقان ؛ بالاتفاق والندرة . وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : قد ثبت في العلوم العقلية : أن حكم الشيء حكم مثله . فلما ثبت أن العمل القليل قد يوجد من الجاهل ، وجب أن يكون حكم أمثاله وأشباهه كذلك . وهذا يدل على أن صدور الفعل المحكم المتقن من الجاهل : جائز .
The perfect (act) can occur once. So the incapable of poetry may utter a perfect line of poetry, and the ignorant of writing may write a single letter with precision and perfection, by agreement and rarity. And if this is established, we say: It has been established in the rational sciences that the ruling of a thing is the ruling of its like. So when it is established that a small act can exist from the ignorant, then the ruling of its likes and similars must be likewise. And this indicates that the occurrence of a precise, perfect act from the ignorant is possible.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument against the claim that a perfect, well-ordered action necessarily indicates that its agent possesses knowledge. It begins by observing that a person incapable of composing poetry can occasionally utter a perfect line, and someone ignorant of calligraphy can write a single perfect letter—these are rare but agreed upon occurrences. Then it invokes a rational principle: the ruling of a thing applies to its like. Since a small perfect act can come from an ignorant person, the same ruling applies to larger similar acts, so it is possible for an ignorant person to produce a perfect act. This undermines the necessary link between perfect action and knowledge.

Key Terms
فعل محكم متقن
جاهل
حكم الشيء حكم مثله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies that God's perfect acts prove His knowledge.
Correction: The argument only shows that occasional perfect acts, such as a single line of poetry, do not require knowledge in the agent. It does not deny that continuous perfect acts, like God's creation, indicate knowledge. In fact, the argument is used to oppose a certain philosophical claim, not to negate divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the rational principle cited in this argument?
A) The ruling of a thing applies to its opposite.
B) The ruling of a thing applies to its like.
C) Every action has an agent.
D) Perfect acts require perfect knowledge.

Key Takeaway
Occasional perfect acts from an ignorant person are possible, challenging the necessary link between perfect action and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever judged someone's entire character based on a single impressive act, ignoring their ignorance in other areas? How does this lesson call you to be more nuanced and merciful in your assessments?

Animal Instinct Versus Learned Knowledge
Second argument using bees, spiders, ants as examples of perfect acts without knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how animal instinct challenges the assumption that perfect design requires conscious knowledge.

Core Idea
Perfect acts do not always prove knowledge; animals show instinct surpasses human reason.

This lesson presents a fascinating argument from observation: bees, spiders, and ants produce remarkably precise structures without formal knowledge. If we claim that perfect action indicates knowledge, then these creatures are more knowledgeable than humans—an absurd conclusion. This forces us to reconsider the link between perfect action and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the specific examples: bees, spiders, ants.
2
Identify the three examples
List the three animals and their specific actions. The bee builds hexagonal houses, the spider repairs its web with amazing actions, and the ant splits grains to prevent sprouting.
All three acts are described as 'precise, perfect actions' (أفعال محكمة متقنة).
3
Understand the logical structure
The argument follows a reductio ad absurdum: if perfect acts imply knowledge, then animals have more knowledge than humans (since their acts are more perfect). This is absurd, so the premise is false.
The conclusion is expressed as 'وذلك بعيد جداً' (that is very far-fetched).
4
Reflect on the implication
Why does this refute the claim that perfect acts require knowledge? Because it shows that instinct can produce perfection without conceptual learning.
This argument supports the view that divine causation can be direct, not mediated by created knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : وهو أنا نشاهد أن النحل يبني البيوت المسدسة من غير مسطرة ولا فرجار، على أحسن الوجوه، بل العقلاء الحصفاء الكاملون ، لو أرادوا بناء البيوت المسدسة من الشمع مثل ما يبنيه النحل ، يعجزون عنه والعنكبوت أيضاً . إذا أرادت إصلاح بيتها ، فإنها تأتي بأعمال عجيبة في ذلك البناء . والنملة إذا خبات في جحرها حبات الحنطة فإنها تقلق كل حبة إلى نصفين، لأجل أنه إذا أصابتها الرطوبة ، فإنها لا تنبت . وعجائب أفعال الحيوانات مذكورة في الكتب . فهذه أفعال محكمة متقنة . فإن دل الفعل المحكم المتقن على علم الفاعل ، وجب القول بأنها أكثر علماً من الإنسان ( لأن ما ) في هذه الأنواع من الأفعال من وجوه الإحكام والإتقان ، أكثر مما في أفعال الناس . وذلك بعيد جداً .
And the second: It is that we observe that the bee builds hexagonal houses without ruler or compass, in the best manner; rather the intelligent, prudent, perfect [human] would be unable to build hexagonal wax houses like the bee builds. The spider also, when it intends to repair its web, performs amazing actions in that construction. And the ant, when it stores wheat grains in its burrow, splits each grain into two halves, so that if moisture affects them, they will not sprout. The wonders of animal actions are mentioned in books. So these are precise, perfect actions. If the precise, perfect act indicates the knowledge of the agent, then one must say that they are more knowledgeable than humans, because the aspects of precision and perfection in these types of actions are more than what is in human actions. And that is very far-fetched.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument: observable animal behaviors produce perfectly designed structures without apparent learning or knowledge. Bees construct hexagonal honeycombs without instruments—a feat that even intelligent humans cannot replicate with wax. Spiders weave webs with amazing intricacy. Ants split grains to prevent sprouting. These are all precise, perfect acts. If one insists that perfect action implies knowledge, then we would have to say these animals have more knowledge than humans, since their products are more perfect. This conclusion is absurd, so the premise that perfect action always indicates knowledge is false.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies any purpose or intelligence in animal actions.
Correction: The argument only denies that perfect animal actions require conscious conceptual knowledge; it does not deny that animals act with purpose or instinct.

B) To prevent sprouting if moisture hits.

D) To feed the young.

Key Takeaway
Animal instinct produces perfect acts without knowledge, disproving the claim that perfect acts always indicate knowledge.

?

?
If animal actions are instinctual, what does that tell you about the Creator who placed that instinct? How does this perspective affect your sense of dependence on Allah's guidance versus your own intellectual abilities?

Perfect Action Without Knowledge: The Argument from Skill
Third argument from skill acquisition: perfect action without conscious knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the acquisition of a habitual faculty (malakah) demonstrates that perfect action does not require conscious knowledge of its details.

Core Idea
Skill becomes effortless habit, proving perfect acts need no detailed awareness.

In this passage, Sanusi presents a third argument drawn from everyday observation: when we learn a craft, we initially need to consciously think about each step. But with practice, a wondrous state called malakah (faculty) develops, allowing us to perform perfectly even while distracted. This shows that perfect action can occur without the agent having explicit knowledge of each detail—a key point in the debate about divine action and knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the contrast between the beginner stage and the expert stage.
Pay attention to the term 'malakah' (ملكة) as it is central.
2
Identify the three stages
The passage describes three phases: initial conscious recall, practice leading to malakah, and effortless perfect action. Identify each and note how knowledge is present or absent.
Also note the concluding claim: 'It is established by this that the workmanship of the precise, perfect act does not depend on its agent being knowledgeable.'
3
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: How does this argument support the idea that Allah can create perfect acts without needing to think about each detail? Connect this to the broader discussion of divine attributes.
Remember that this argument distinguishes explicit knowledge from the dispositional state (malakah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : وهو أنا ترى أن الإنسان إذا أراد أن يتعلم صنعة الكتابة أو ضرب الطنبور أو صنعة أخرى، فإنه في أول الأمر يحتاج إلى أن يستحضر في ذهنه صورة حرف حرف ، وصورة نقرة نقرة ، ومتى كان الإنسان باقياً في هذه الدرجة ، فإنه يكون مقصراً في تلك الصناعة ، ثم إذا واظب على تلك الحرفة مدة مديدة ، وتمرن فيها حصلت له حالة عجيبة تسمى بالملكة في اصطلاح الحكماء ، بأن يصير بحيث يكتب على أحسن الوجوه من غير أن يحتاج إلى استحضار حرف حرف في الخيال ، بل ربما كان مشغول القلب بمهم ، وهو يكتب على أحسن الوجوه . فههنا الفعل المحكم المتقن الواقع على أكمل الوجوه حاصل مع أن العلم بتلك التفاصيل والأحوال غير حاصل . فيثبت بهذا أن صنعة الفعل المحكم المتقن ، لا تتوقف على كون فاعله عالماً. بل قد ذكرنا : أن استحضار العلم بكل واحد من تلك الحروف والأشكال ربما منع ذلك الفاعل من الإتيان بذلك العمل على وجه الكمال والتمام .
The third aspect: It is that we see that when a person wants to learn the craft of writing, or playing the tambour, or another craft, he needs at first to recall in his mind the image of each letter and each stroke. As long as he remains at this stage, he is deficient in that craft. Then, if he persists in that craft for a long period and trains in it, there comes about for him a wondrous state called 'al-malakah' (faculty) in the terminology of the philosophers, such that he comes to write in the best manner without needing to recall each letter in imagination; rather, perhaps his heart is occupied with some important matter, and he writes in the best manner. So here, the precise, perfect act occurring in the most complete manner is present, even though knowledge of those details and states is not present. It is established by this that the workmanship of the precise, perfect act does not depend on its agent being knowledgeable. Rather, we have mentioned that recalling knowledge of each of those letters and shapes may prevent that agent from performing that work in a perfect and complete manner.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument from the phenomenon of skill acquisition. When learning a craft like writing or playing a musical instrument, the beginner must consciously focus on each detail. At that stage, performance is clumsy. With practice, a habitual faculty (malakah) develops, allowing effortless perfect execution. The expert can perform perfectly even while distracted. This shows that perfect action can occur without the agent having conscious, detailed knowledge of each component; indeed, conscious recall would hinder perfection. Therefore, perfect action does not require the agent to be knowledgeable in the sense of having explicit awareness.

Key Terms

صنعة
علم
فعل محكم متقن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some equate 'knowledge' with any state that enables action, assuming that if an action is perfect, the agent must have detailed explicit knowledge.
Correction: This argument shows that a habitual disposition (malakah) can produce perfect action without explicit conscious knowledge. Thus, knowledge in the sense of detailed awareness is not required.

Checkpoint

B) Al-malakah (faculty)
C) Al-quwwah (power)

Key Takeaway
The acquisition of a habitual faculty (malakah) through practice demonstrates that perfect action does not require conscious, detailed knowledge.

Fourth Argument: Nature and Perfect Action
Philosophers' argument using nature and craftsmanship analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that perfect actions can arise from non-conscious nature, and how this is used to challenge the necessity of knowledge in causation.

Core Idea
Perfect action does not require knowledge; even philosophers admit nature's actions surpass human craft.

This lesson examines the fourth argument in a theological debate, where philosophers are cited as claiming that nature, despite lacking consciousness, produces perfect animal bodies. They support this with an analogy from craftsmanship: a skilled artisan's work is said to be 'natural' to him. This argument challenges the assumption that perfect action must come from a knowing agent.

Guided Path
1
Read the philosophers' claim and objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note how the philosophers attribute animal bodies to nature despite nature's lack of consciousness, and the objection that such perfect effects seem impossible from an unconscious cause.
The objection is implied in the text's rhetorical question.
2
Examine the craftsmanship analogy
Focus on their answer: when an artisan becomes perfect in his craft, people say it has become natural to him. This analogy is meant to show that perfect action can be 'natural' even without awareness.
This is the core of their argument.
3
Understand the underlying implication
Reflect on how this argument undermines the idea that knowledge is necessary for perfect action. The text presents this as a challenge to the philosophers' own position, exposing inconsistency.
The argument is used to critique necessitarian philosophers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الرابع : هو أن الفلاسفة القائلين بالموجب إضافوا تكون أبدان الحيوانات إلى تأثير الطبيعة ، واقتضاء القوة المصورة ، مع أن الطبيعة ليس لها شعور وإدراك البتة ، فإذا قيل لهم : كيف يعقل إسناد هذه الأفعال العجيبة والآثار المحكمة المتقنة إلى قوة ليس لها شعور ولا إدراك ؟ أجابوا بأن قالوا : إن هذا غير مستبعد في العقول ، وذلك لأن الفاعل إذا صار ما هي التي صنعته بكاملاً في حرفته ، وأراد العقلاء مدحه بأنه صار كاملاً في تلك الحرفة قالوا : إن هذه الحرفة صارت كالأمر الطبيعي له . فهذا إقرار من العقلاء بأن كمال القادر الصانع أن يقيسه في كيفية أفعاله بالطبيعة . وهذا يدل على إقرار العقلاء بأن أفعال الطبيعة أكمل وأفضل من أفعال الصناعة .
And the fourth aspect: It is that the philosophers who hold to necessitation attribute the formation of animal bodies to the influence of nature and the requirement of the formative power, even though nature has no consciousness or perception at all. So if it is said to them: How can it be reasonable to attribute these wondrous actions and precise, perfect effects to a power that has no consciousness or perception? They answer by saying: This is not improbable in minds, because when an agent becomes perfect in his craft, and the intelligent want to praise him for becoming perfect in that craft, they say: This craft has become like a natural thing for him. So this is an acknowledgment from intelligent people that the perfection of the powerful craftsman is to be measured in the manner of his actions by nature. And this indicates the admission of intelligent people that the actions of nature are more perfect and better than the actions of craft.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument, drawing from the philosophers (al-falasifah) who are 'qailun bil-mujib' (those who hold to necessitation, often referring to natural causation). They attribute the formation of animal bodies to nature and the 'formative power' (al-quwwah al-musawwirah), despite nature lacking any consciousness. When challenged how such wondrous effects can come from an unconscious cause, they respond by analogy: when an artisan becomes extremely skilled, people say his craft has become second nature to him. This indicates that intelligent people measure perfect craftsmanship by how natural it seems. Thus, even philosophers admit that nature's actions are more perfect than human craft, implying that perfect action can come from a non-conscious source. This further undermines the necessity of knowledge for perfect action.

Key Terms
فلاسفة
موجب
طبيعة
قوة مصورة
شعور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Perfect actions can only come from a knowing agent.
Correction: The philosophers argue that non-conscious nature can produce perfect effects, as shown by the craftsmanship analogy. This challenges the necessity of knowledge for perfect causation.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers cited, what is the analogy used to justify attributing perfect actions to nature?
A) A craftsman who has become perfect in his craft is said to have made his craft natural.
B) Nature is like a watchmaker.
C) Animals act by instinct.
D) Perfect actions can only come from a knowing agent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' nature argument uses the craftsmanship analogy to claim that perfect actions don't require consciousness, challenging theological notions of causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
