Fifth Argument: The Mutakallimun's Dilemma on Specific Time and Space
Fifth argument using the mutakallimun's answer about why the world began at a specific time.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mutakallimun's own answer to the question of why the world began at a specific time is used to argue that divine action does not require a rational cause.

Core Idea
If any time would raise the same question, the question itself proves nothing.

This passage presents a critical moment in the debate about the world's origin. The mutakallimun argue that the world began at a specific time and occupies a specific space, even though all times and spaces are similar. When asked why, they give an answer that seems to undermine their own position. Let's examine their first answer and see why it is used against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: spaces are equal, times are equal; the world is specific; the question; the answer.
The passage is incomplete; only the first of two answers is preserved.
2
Analyze the first answer
The mutakallimun say if the world had begun at another time, the same question would apply. Think about why this is not a satisfying answer.
This is a regress argument; it doesn't explain why this time, it just says any time would have the same problem.
3
Connect to the broader argument
See how this supports the overall argument that perfect action does not require knowledge of reasons.
The fifth argument uses the mutakallimun's own reply to show their inconsistency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الخامس : وهو أن الأحياز متساوية . والأوقات متساوية . ثم إن المتكلمين قالوا : إنه اختص حدوث العالم بوقت معين دون سائر الأوقات ، واختص حصول ذات العالم بحيز معين دون سائر الأحياز ، فلما قيل لهم : ولم اختص حدوث العالم بذلك الوقت دون سائر الأوقات ؟ ولم حصل العالم في ذلك الحيز دون سائر الأحياز ؟ والأوقات بأسرها متماثلة ، والأحياز بأسرها متماثلة . أجابوا عنه بوجهين : الأول : إنه لو حصل حدوث العالم في وقت آخر غير هذا الوقت ، لكان هذا السؤال المذكور باقياً ، ولو حصل العالم في حيز آخر سوى هذا الحيز ...
The fifth aspect: It is that spaces are equal, and times are equal. Then the mutakallimun said: The origination of the world was specific to a particular time to the exclusion of other times, and the existence of the world's substance was specific to a particular space to the exclusion of other spaces. So when it was said to them: Why was the origination of the world specific to that time to the exclusion of other times? And why did the world occur in that space to the exclusion of other spaces? While all times are similar, and all spaces are similar. They answered with two aspects: The first: If the origination of the world had occurred at another time besides this time, this aforementioned question would remain. And if the world had occurred in another space besides this space...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument, which focuses on the mutakallimun's (theologians') own position regarding the origination of the world. They assert that the world began at a specific time and occupies a specific space, despite times and spaces being essentially similar. When challenged to justify that specific choice, they give two answers. The first answer (the only one fully preserved here) is a regress or self-refutation argument: if the world had begun at any other time, the same question would apply, so the question itself is not unique to this particular time; it would persist regardless. This answer, the argument suggests, is an admission that they have no real justification other than mere choice—similar to the point that a perfect action can originate without a reason based on knowledge.

Key Terms
أحياز
أوقات
متكلمين
حدوث العالم
اختصاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think this passage endorses the mutakallimun's view or presents a convincing answer.
Correction: In reality, the passage uses their own answer to show that they cannot give a rational cause for the specificity, thus supporting the argument that divine action does not require knowledge.

Checkpoint
What is the first answer the mutakallimun give for why the world began at that specific time?
A) God chose it arbitrarily.
B) If it had been at another time, the same question would remain.
C) Times are not actually equal.
D) It was the best possible time.

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun's first answer—that the same question would apply to any time—fails to provide a reason for the specific time, thus undermining their claim and supporting the view that divine action does not require a rational cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a trial or blessing, do you ever find yourself silently asking, 'Why now, and not another time?' What if Allah's answer is simply that any other time would leave the same question unanswered? Will you accept that, or will you keep searching for a reason?
?
The mutakallimun's best explanation for why the world began when it did was to say, 'If it began at another time, the same question would apply.' Have you ever given a similar circular excuse to justify God's decree to yourself or others? How does this passage reveal the emptiness of that approach?
?
If you truly believe that Allah's will is sufficient without explanation, what is one area of your life where you still demand to see the 'why' before you submit? Are you willing to stop asking and simply obey?

The Fourth Argument: Nature vs. Divine Craftsmanship
Razi presents and refutes the philosophers' analogy on animal bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the philosophers' claim that blind nature can produce perfect effects, by turning their own analogy against them.

Core Idea
The philosophers' analogy backfires: calling a craftsman 'natural' implies nature is superior, not that it is intelligent.

In this fourth argument, Razi tackles the philosophers' attempt to attribute the intricate design of animal bodies to blind nature. When challenged, they argue that a perfect craftsman's skill becomes 'natural'—but Razi shows this very comparison undermines their position. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
Notice the structure: claim, objection, response, refutation.
2
Identify the philosophers' claim and their analogy
The philosophers attribute animal bodies to blind nature. When challenged, they compare a perfect craftsman's skill to a natural thing. Note the key terms: الطبيعة, القوة المصورة.
3
Understand Razi's refutation
Razi points out that the rational people's praise implies that nature's acts are more perfect. Ask: why does this refute the philosophers?
The analogy backfires: if nature is superior, then it is not blind but intelligent.
4
Reflect on the theological significance
This argument defends divine craftsmanship by showing that attributing design to blind nature is incoherent. Consider how this fits into larger debates on causality.
This connects to the broader theme of necessitarianism vs. free will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الرابع: هو أن الفلاسفة القائلين بالموجب أضافوا تكون أبدان الحيوانات إلى تأثير الطبيعة، واقتضاء القوة المصورة، مع أن الطبيعة ليس لها شعور وإدراك البتة، فإذا قيل لهم: كيف يعقل إسناد هذه الأفعال العجيبة والآثار المحكمة المتقنة إلى قوة ليس لها شعور ولا إدراك؟ أجابوا بأن قالوا: إن هذا غير مستبعد في العقول، وذلك لأن الفاعل إذا صارت ما هي التي صنعته بكاملاً في حرفته، وأراد العقلاء مدحته بأنه صار كاملاً في تلك الحرفة قالوا: إن هذه الحرفة صارت كالأمر الطبيعي له. فهذا إقرار من العقلاء بأن كمال [؟] القادر الصانع إنما نقيسه في كيفية أفعاله بالطبيعة. وهذا يدل على إقرار العقلاء بأن أفعال الطبيعة أكمل وأفضل من أفعال الصناعة.
And the fourth aspect: that the philosophers who affirm the necessitarian added the formation of animal bodies to the influence of nature, and the requirement of the formative power, while nature has absolutely no consciousness or perception. So when it is said to them: How is it rationally acceptable to attribute these wondrous acts and perfect, precise effects to a power that has no consciousness or perception? They answered by saying: This is not remote in the intellects, because when the agent becomes perfect in his craft, and the rational people intend to praise him for becoming perfect in that craft, they say: This craft has become like a natural thing for him. So this is an acknowledgment from the rational people that the perfection of [؟] the capable artisan is measured by how his acts are with nature. And this indicates the rational people's acknowledgment that the acts of nature are more perfect and better than the acts of artifice.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument in a series refuting the philosophers' necessitarianism. The philosophers attribute the formation of animal bodies to nature and the formative power, despite nature having no consciousness. When asked how such precise effects can come from a blind force, they respond by analogy: a perfect craftsman's skill becomes natural, and people acknowledge his perfection by comparing his acts to nature. Razi then turns this analogy against them: this very acknowledgment implies that nature's acts are considered more perfect than deliberate human craft, thus undermining the philosophers' attempt to reduce divine action to blind nature.

Key Terms
الطبيعة
القوة المصورة
الفاعل القادر
الصناعة
الإقرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the philosophers' analogy supports their position that blind nature can produce perfect designs.
Correction: Razi shows the analogy backfires: by comparing a craftsman's skill to nature, rational people implicitly acknowledge that nature's acts are superior, contradicting the idea that nature is blind and inferior.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' response in the fourth argument, what do they claim becomes like a natural thing?
A) The formation of animal bodies
B) The craft of a perfect artisan
C) The act of the free agent
D) The effect of the formative power

Key Takeaway
Razi refutes the philosophers by showing that their own analogy implies nature's actions are more perfect than deliberate craft, contradicting their claim that nature is blind.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you praise someone's skill by saying 'it comes naturally to them,' what does that reveal about your assumptions regarding where true perfection originates? How can you consciously redirect that acknowledgment to Allah, the ultimate creator of natural laws?
?
Consider a talent or ability you have that feels 'natural' to you—something you do with ease. How often do you reflect that this ease is a gift from Allah, not merely a result of your own nature? What concrete step can you take this week to remind yourself of this truth?
?
The philosophers measured divine action by the standard of blind nature. In your own life, do you ever evaluate Allah's actions or decrees by human standards of logic or fairness? Write down one situation where you have done so and commit to re-framing your perspective in light of Allah's perfect wisdom and power.

Why the Ash'ari View Undermines the Argument from Design
Objection that Ash'ari causality undermines the argument from design for God's knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Ash'ari doctrine of habit (al-‘ādah) undercuts the teleological argument from design (al-ihkām wa al-itqān) for God's knowledge.

Core Idea
If causality is mere habit, design proves nothing about the designer's knowledge.

The argument from design observes purposeful structures in creation and infers a knowledgeable Creator. But what if the connection between structures and their functions is not necessary but merely habitual, as the Ash'ari school holds? This passage shows how such a view collapses the argument, making it impossible to claim that organs were created for specific purposes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation to get the overall flow.
2
Identify the core objection
Notice that the passage argues that on the Ash'ari view, the structure of organs has no effect on their functions. Find the key phrase stating this.
3
Trace the consequence
See how this leads to the conclusion that the argument from design (al-ihkām wa al-itqān) is invalidated, so it cannot prove God's knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this critique matters for theology and the debate over causality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واختلت هذه الأعضاء فإنه تبطل الحياة والعلم والقدرة. قال: ذاك بحسب عادة أجراها الله تعالى. فإما أن يقال: إن حصول الحياة مفتقر إلى حصول هذه البنية. وحصول الإبصار والسماع مفتقر إلى خلقة العين والأذن. فليس الأمر كذلك. إذا عرفت هذا فنقول: إن على مذهبه ليس لشيء من التركيبات والتصورات أثر في حصول شيء من المنافع والمصالح. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه لا يمكننا أن نقول: إنه تعالى إنما خلق العين على هذه الخلقة لأجل أن يكمل بها الإبصار. وإنما خلق المعدة على هذا الوجه لأجل أن يكمل بها فعل الهضم. وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يبطل ما يذكره أصحاب التشريح من منافع الأعضاء ونحن لا نفهم من لفظ الإحكام والإتقان إلا هذه الوجوه. فإذا بينا أن شيئاً منها لا يصح على مذهب الأشعري فقد بطل الإحكام والإتقان على مذهبه فكيف يمكنه يستدل بالإحكام والإتقان على علم الفاعل القادر؟
And if these organs are disturbed, then life, knowledge, and power become void. He said: That is according to a habit that God has put in place. Then it may be said: The occurrence of life is dependent on the occurrence of this structure. And the occurrence of sight and hearing is dependent on the creation of the eye and ear. But it is not so. When you know this, we say: According to his school, no combination or form has any effect in obtaining any benefit or interest. And on this premise, we cannot say that God created the eye in this particular form in order to perfect sight through it, and that He created the stomach in this manner in order to perfect the act of digestion through it. And on this premise, what the anatomists mention about the benefits of organs becomes invalid. And we understand from the term 'precision and perfection' (الإحكام والإتقان) nothing but these aspects. Since we have shown that none of these is valid according to the Ash'ari school, then precision and perfection are invalidated according to his school. So how can he use precision and perfection as evidence for the knowledge of the powerful agent (الفاعل القادر)?
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the Ash'ari position on causality and its impact on the argument from design (الإحكام والإتقان) for God's knowledge. The discussion begins with a hypothetical: if the organs are disturbed, life and cognitive faculties cease. The opponent responds that this is merely according to God's habit, not necessary causation. The objection then argues that on the Ash'ari view, the structure of organs (تركيبات) and forms (تصورات) have no real effect in producing benefits. Hence, one cannot claim that God created the eye for sight or the stomach for digestion. Consequently, what anatomists say about the benefits of organs becomes baseless. Since 'precision and perfection' (الإحكام والإتقان) is understood precisely in terms of such purposeful design, and since such design is not valid on the Ash'ari school, the argument from design cannot be used to prove that the powerful agent (الفاعل القادر) has knowledge.

Key Terms
الإحكام والإتقان
مذهب الأشعري
العادة
التركيبات والتصورات
الفاعل القادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This passage is often misunderstood as a denial of causality altogether.
Correction: It is specifically a critique of how the Ash'ari occasionalist position undercuts teleological arguments, not a denial of causality per se.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the consequence of the Ash'ari view on causality for the argument from design?
A) The argument from design becomes stronger because it relies on habit.
B) The argument from design collapses because purposeful design loses its basis.
C) The argument from design is unaffected because it depends on divine attributes.
D) The argument from design is replaced by a stronger proof.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari view of habit breaks the link between structure and function, making the argument from design for God's knowledge invalid.

First proof: knowledge of essence does not entail knowledge of all
The author presents the first of three proofs against the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof against the premise that knowledge of one essence requires knowledge of all its necessary properties, and how the author uses the threefold classification of relations between essences to build a reductio argument.

Core Idea
If knowing one essence implies knowing all its relations, we would know every essence—which is false.

The author now launches the first of three proofs against the idea that knowing an essence gives us knowledge of its necessary properties. He begins by classifying the possible relations any essence can have with other essences into three categories: necessitating, incompatible, or neutral. Then he shows that if knowledge of an essence entailed knowledge of these relations, we would be forced to know all other essences, which is absurd. This short but powerful argument is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without interruption, then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the author's concise style.
2
Identify the three types of relations
The author classifies relations between any essence and others into three categories. Find them in the text and understand each one.
These are: necessitating (mustalzima), incompatible (munafiya), and neutral (la mustalzima wa la munafiya).
3
Follow the conditional reasoning
The author sets up a conditional: if knowledge of an essence implies knowledge of its necessary properties, then from knowing one essence we would know all its relations to other essences.
This is the core of the reductio.
4
Understand the absurd conclusion
Since knowing all relations would mean knowing all other essences (which are infinite), and we do not have such knowledge, the original premise must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : إنا نعلم ماهيات كثيرة ، وحقائق كثيرة . وكل حقيقة يشير العقل إليها، فإن لها بالنسبة إلى سائر الحقائق أحوال ثلاثة : إما أن تكون مستلزمة لها ، أو منافية لها، أو لا مستلزمة لها ولا منافية . وكونها مستلزمة للأمر الفلاني لازم من لوازم تلك الماهية ، وكونها منافية لنوع آخر من الماهيات ، لازم آخر من لوازمها ، وكونها بحيث لا تكون مستلزمة لنوع ثالث ولا منافية له ، نوع ثالث من اللوازم . فلو لزم من العلم بماهية من الماهيات ، العلم بلوازمها - ونحن قد علمنا بعض الماهيات - وجب أن يعلم كونها مستلزمة للنوع الفلاني ، وكونها منافية للنوع الثاني ، وكونها غير مستلزمة للنوع الثالث ولا منافية له . ومتى علمنا ذلك وجب أن نعلم جميع الماهيات ، فلو لزم من المعلم بالماهية العلم بلوازمها ، لزم من المعلم بماهية واحدة العلم بجميع الماهيات التي لا نهاية لها . ولما كان ذلك باطلاً فكذلك ما ذكروه يجب أن يكون باطلاً.
The first: we know many essences and realities. And every reality the intellect points to, it has with respect to other realities three states: either it necessitates them, or is incompatible with them, or neither necessitates nor is incompatible. And its necessitating a certain thing is a necessary property of that essence, and its being incompatible with another type of essences is another necessary property, and its being such that it neither necessitates a third type nor is incompatible with it is a third type of necessary properties. So if knowledge of an essence necessitated knowledge of its necessary properties—and we have known some essences—then it would be necessary to know that it necessitates such and such type, and that it is incompatible with the second type, and that it is neither necessitating nor incompatible with the third type. And whenever we know that, we would have to know all essences. So if knowledge of the essence necessitated knowledge of its necessary properties, then from knowledge of a single essence we would have knowledge of all infinite essences. And since that is false, so likewise what they mentioned must be false.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs against the premise that knowledge of an essence implies knowledge of its necessary properties. The author classifies the relations between any essence and other essences into three categories: necessitating (mustalzima), incompatible (munafiya), and neutral (la mustalzima wa la munafiya). He argues that if knowing one essence gave us knowledge of its necessary properties in these three categories, then from knowing one essence we would have to know all other essences that it necessitates or is incompatible with, ad infinitum. Since we do not have such knowledge, the premise is false. This is a reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
ماهيات
أحوال ثلاثة
مستلزمة
منافية
لوازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument claims that we cannot know any properties of an essence.
Correction: The argument does not deny knowledge of properties; it denies that knowledge of an essence automatically gives knowledge of all its necessary properties, especially those involving relations to all other essences.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, how many types of relations does an essence have with other essences?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) Four

Key Takeaway
The first proof invalidates the premise that knowledge of an essence entails knowledge of all its necessary properties by showing it would require omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a person or concept you believe you fully understand. Where are you assuming that your partial knowledge covers all their essential relations? How might that assumption be a form of arrogance, similar to the false premise refuted here? Will you commit today to asking at least one question that reveals your ignorance rather than asserting your knowledge?
?
This proof shows that even a single essence, if known completely, would require knowing all other essences. In your daily judgments, do you ever claim to know someone 'inside out' while ignoring the infinite network of relations that shape them? What would it mean to approach others with humility about your limited knowledge?
?
The author uses logic to expose the absurdity of a claim. Are there beliefs you hold about Allah or about yourself that, if followed to their logical conclusion, would require you to be omniscient? What does that tell you about the need to submit your intellect to revelation? How can you actively resist the temptation to overstep your cognitive limits?

Second Proof: The Essence of Body and Its Temporality
The author presents the second proof using the essence of body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that challenges the premise that knowledge of an essence implies knowledge of its necessary properties, using the example of the essence of body.

Core Idea
Intuitive knowledge of an essence does not give intuitive knowledge of its disputed properties.

The author presents a second, concrete argument to challenge the premise that knowing an essence automatically gives knowledge of its necessary properties. He uses the example of the essence of body, which we know intuitively, yet we do not intuitively know whether it is temporal or eternal. This observation reveals a flaw in the original claim.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure of the argument.
This is a short argument with a clear logical flow.
2
Identify the premise being refuted
The argument targets the premise that knowledge of an essence necessitates knowledge of its necessary properties.
3
Examine the example: essence of body
We have an intuitive conception of 'body' but we do not know intuitively whether it is eternal or temporal—that is disputed.
This shows that not all necessary properties are known by intuition of the essence.
4
Follow the logical conclusion
If the premise were true, we would have intuitive knowledge of body's temporality/eternity. Since we don't, the premise is false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Reflect on the significance
This proof demonstrates that knowledge of an essence does not automatically yield knowledge of its necessary properties, especially when those properties are subject to dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : إنا نعقل ماهية الجسم بالبديهة. ومن لوازم هذه الماهية إما حدوثها أو قدمها - على اختلاف القولين - فلو كان العلم بالماهية يوجبه العلم بلوازمها لزم من العلم البديهي بماهية الجسم حصول العلم البديهي بكونه قديما أو محدثاً ، ولما كان ذلك باطلاً لزم بطلان ما ذكروه ....
The second: we conceive the essence of body by intuition. Among the necessary properties of this essence are either its temporality or its eternity—according to differing opinions. So if knowledge of the essence necessitated knowledge of its necessary properties, then from intuitive knowledge of the essence of body we would have intuitive knowledge of its being eternal or temporal. And since that is false, what they mentioned is false.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This is the second proof. The author uses the example of the essence of body. We intuitively know what 'body' means, but we do not intuitively know whether it is eternal or temporal—that is a disputed matter. If knowledge of the essence implied knowledge of its necessary properties, then from our intuitive knowledge of body we would have intuitive knowledge of its being eternal or temporal. Since we lack that intuitive knowledge, the premise is false.

Third Proof: The Soul and Its Necessary Properties
The author presents the third proof using the soul's self-knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument that demonstrates that knowledge of a thing's essence does not automatically give knowledge of all its necessary properties.

Core Idea
Knowing your own soul does not mean you know all its properties.

The author presents a third proof, this time grounded in our direct awareness of our own soul. We know our soul's essence directly, yet we do not have intuitive knowledge of its key attributes like being non-corporeal or being originated. This discrepancy reveals an important logical principle about the limits of essential knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the logical flow from premise to conclusion.
Look out for the word 'بديهي' (intuitive).
2
Identify the Argument's Structure
The passage presents a conditional argument: if knowledge of essence implied knowledge of all properties, then we would have intuitive knowledge of the soul's abstractness, origination, and permanence. Since we do not, the conditional is false.
3
Reflect on the Implication
Ask yourself: why does the author choose the soul as an example? How does our direct self-awareness make this a strong argument?
This connects to the broader proof against exhaustive essential knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وهو أن جوهر نفسنا جوهر قائم بنفسه مجرد [يفه؟] مجرد عن الجسمية وعلائقها . فعلى ما تعين (١) قولكم نفسنا لنفسنا ، فنفسنا عالمة بماهية نفسنا ، وجب أن يلزم من علمها بنفسها المخصوصة علمها بجميع آثارها ولوازمها ، فكان يجب أن يكون علم النفس بكونها مجردة عن الجسمية ويكونها واجبة الحدوث ويكونها واجبة البقاء ، علماً بديهياً . وحيث لم يكن الأمر كذلك علمنا أنه لا يلزم من العلم بالماهية العلم بجميع لوازمها
The third: that the substance of our soul is a substance subsisting by itself, abstract [؟], abstract from corporeality and its attachments. So according to what has been determined (1) your statement: our soul is [known] to itself, so our soul is knowledgeable of the essence of our soul, then it must follow from its knowledge of its specific self its knowledge of all its effects and necessary properties. So it would be necessary that the soul's knowledge of its being abstract from corporeality and its being necessarily originated and its being necessarily permanent be intuitive knowledge. And since it is not the case, we know that knowledge of the essence does not necessitate knowledge of all its necessary properties.
Source pages p. 126

Commentary
This is the third proof. The author considers the substance of the human soul. We know our own soul directly, but we do not have intuitive knowledge of its necessary properties such as its being abstract from matter, its origination, and its permanence. If knowledge of an essence implied knowledge of all its necessary properties, then from our knowledge of our soul we would have intuitive knowledge of these properties. Since we do not, the premise is false.

Fourth Proof: The Symmetry Argument about Cause and Effect
Author presents a symmetry proof against necessary knowledge from cause to effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of an effect does not necessarily give knowledge of its specific cause, based on the symmetry argument presented in the fourth proof.

Core Idea
Cause and effect are not symmetric in knowledge—knowing the effect does not imply knowing the cause.

The author presents a clever argument using the relationship between cause and effect. He points out that if knowing a cause gave knowledge of its effect, then by symmetry, knowing the effect should also give knowledge of the cause. But we have intimate knowledge of ourselves, which are effects of higher causes, yet we lack detailed knowledge of those causes. Thus the initial premise is false.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main claim
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Identify the claim being refuted: that knowledge of a cause gives knowledge of its effect.
2
Follow the symmetry argument
Notice how the author establishes symmetry between cause and effect regarding concomitance. Then trace the conditional: if knowledge of cause gives knowledge of effect, then knowledge of effect should give knowledge of cause.
The argument assumes the relationship is reciprocal.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author uses our knowledge of our own selves (effects) and our lack of detailed knowledge of higher causes. See how this counterexample disproves the conditional.
This shows that knowledge of a thing does not necessarily give knowledge of its concomitants.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that knowledge of a thing does not necessitate knowledge of its concomitants. Consider why this matters for the larger argument about causality.
This proof undermines the claim that knowing the cause yields full knowledge of the effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كما أن المعلول من لوازم العلة ، فكذلك العلة أيضاً من لوازم المعلول ، فلو لزم من العلم بالعلة ، العلم بالمعلول وجب أيضاً أن يلزم من العلم بالمعلول العلم بالعلة ، لكنا نعلم ذواتنا المخصوصة وأحوال ذواتنا . ولا نزاع أن ذواتنا وأحوال ذواتنا ، معلولات للعلل العالية ، فلو لزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بلوازمه، لزم أن يلزم من علمنا بذواتنا وأحوال ذواتنا ، علمنا بالعلل العالية على التفصيل التام. وحيث لم يلزم ذلك ، علمنا : أن العلم بالشيء لا يوجب العلم بلوازمه
The fourth [proof]: Just as the effect (ma'lūl) is among the concomitants (lawaazim) of the cause ('illa), likewise the cause is also among the concomitants of the effect. So if knowledge of the cause necessitated knowledge of the effect, then it would also be necessary that knowledge of the effect necessitated knowledge of the cause. But we know our particular selves (dhawāt) and the states of our selves. There is no dispute that our selves and our states are effects of the higher causes (al-'ilal al-'āliya). So if knowledge of a thing necessitated knowledge of its concomitants, then our knowledge of our selves and our states would necessitate our knowledge of the higher causes in complete detail. But since that does not happen, we know that knowledge of a thing does not necessarily give knowledge of its concomitants.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof, which exploits the symmetry between cause and effect. The author argues that if knowledge of the cause entailed knowledge of the effect, then by the same logic, knowledge of the effect should entail knowledge of its specific cause. However, we have knowledge of our own selves and our states, and it is agreed that these are effects of higher causes (such as God and the celestial intelligences). But we do not have detailed knowledge of those higher causes. Therefore, the original premise is false. The author here anticipates a possible objection about the asymmetry of cause and effect, which will be addressed in the next lesson.

Key Terms
معلول
علة
لازم
ذات
علل عالية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If we know an effect, we should know its cause.
Correction: The author shows that knowing an effect does not entail detailed knowledge of its cause, as seen with our selves and higher causes.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth proof, what would necessarily follow if knowledge of a thing gave knowledge of its concomitants?
A) Knowledge of our selves would give knowledge of all higher causes in detail.
B) Knowledge of our selves would give knowledge only of material causes.
C) Knowledge of causes would give no knowledge of effects.
D) Knowledge of effects would give knowledge only of immediate causes.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of a thing does not necessarily give knowledge of its concomitants, as shown by the asymmetry of cause and effect.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know the states of your own soul—your fears, desires, and failings—but do you honestly trace each one back to its ultimate cause in your relationship with God? Or do you stop at the surface, content with knowing the effect without seeking the Cause? What would it cost you to dig deeper today?
?
This lesson exposes the gap between knowing an effect and knowing its cause. Where in your life have you assumed that because you experience something, you fully understand its source? Will you settle for the effect, or will you press into the Cause until He reveals Himself—even if the knowledge remains incomplete?
?
You have undeniable knowledge of yourself—your pain, your sin, your need. That knowledge is an effect pointing to a higher Cause. But are you using that knowledge to draw nearer to the Cause, or are you using it to flee? What step will you take today to let your self-knowledge lead you to the One who created you?

The Asymmetry Between Cause and Effect Knowledge
The author explains the asymmetry between knowing the cause and knowing the effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of a cause entails knowledge of its effect, but not vice versa, due to the nature of contingency.

Core Idea
Knowledge flows from cause to effect, not from effect to cause.

In this passage, the author addresses a potential objection that might challenge his earlier proof. The objection assumes that just as knowing a cause gives you its effect, knowing an effect should give you its cause. The author explains why this is not so by examining the relationship between a specific cause and a specific effect, and the role of contingency. This lesson will clarify this essential asymmetry.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full translation. Let the author's argument come through before analyzing.
Pay attention to the key terms: cause (علة), effect (معلول), contingency (إمكان).
2
Identify the two claims
The passage states two things: knowledge of cause gives knowledge of effect, and knowledge of effect does not give knowledge of cause. Notice the asymmetry.
3
Understand why cause gives effect
The author argues that a specific cause, by its essence and powers, necessitates that specific effect. So if you know the cause, you know the effect.
This is because the cause is sufficient for the effect.
4
Understand why effect does not give cause
The effect is contingent (imkan). Its contingency only requires some cause in general, not that specific cause. Therefore, knowing the effect does not reveal which cause brought it about.
Otherwise, every contingent would need that same cause, which is false.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes this asymmetry is clear and reasonable. Consider why this matters for arguments about causality in theology.
This supports the earlier proof that the contingency argument does not require symmetry.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جرم واعلم أنه يمكن الجواب عن هذا السؤال . فيقال : العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول . أما العلم بالمعلول ، فإنه لا يوجب العلم بالعلة . والفرق بين الصورتين : أن العلة المعينة توجب لعينها وقواتها ذلك المعلول المعين ، فلا صح أن يقال : إنه يلزم من العلم بتلك العلة المعينة العلم بذلك المعلول المعين . أما المعلول المعين فإنه إنما يفتقر لإمكانه إلى العلة : والإمكان لا يحوجه إلى تلك العلة ، وإلا لزم أن يكون كل ممكن محتاجاً إلى تلك العلة المعينة بعينها . ومعلوم أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، بل الإمكان يحوجه إلى علة ما ، فأما وقوعه بتلك العلة المعينة ، فإنما كان لأجل أنه لما وجدت تلك العلة ، وأوجبته ، لا جرم استند ذلك المعلول إليه ، فيثبت أن ذلك المعلول لا يقتضي الاحتياج إلى هذه العلة بعينها . وإنما يقتضي الاحتياج إلى مطلق العلة ، فلا جرم لم يلزم من العلم بالمعلول المعين ، العلم بعلته المعينة . وهذا فرق واضح معقول بين الصورتين . وبالله التوفيق
And know that it is possible to answer this question. It is said: Knowledge of the cause (al-'illa) necessitates knowledge of the effect (al-ma'lūl). As for knowledge of the effect, it does not necessitate knowledge of the cause. The difference between the two cases is that the specific cause, by its very essence and its powers, necessitates that specific effect, so it cannot be said that knowledge of that specific cause necessitates knowledge of that specific effect. As for the specific effect, it is only in need of a cause due to its contingency (imkān); contingency does not require it to need that specific cause, otherwise every contingent would have to need that same specific cause. And it is known that this is not the case; rather, contingency requires it to need some cause (mutlaq al-'illa). So its occurrence through that specific cause is only because when that cause existed and necessitated it, that effect became dependent on it. This establishes that that effect does not entail the need for this specific cause, but rather entails the need for an absolute cause. Therefore, knowledge of the specific effect does not necessitate knowledge of its specific cause. And this is a clear and reasonable difference between the two cases. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage provides the author's response to a possible objection arising from the fourth proof. The objection might claim that if knowledge of a cause gives knowledge of its effect, then by symmetry knowledge of effect should give knowledge of cause. The author refutes this by pointing out the asymmetry between cause and effect in terms of necessity. A specific cause, by its own essence and powers, necessarily gives rise to a specific effect. Therefore, knowing the specific cause does allow one to know that specific effect. However, a specific effect, being contingent, only requires some cause or other, not that specific cause. Its contingency does not tie it to a particular cause, so knowing the effect does not reveal which specific cause brought it about. This difference explains why the symmetry does not hold. The author concludes that this distinction is clear and reasonable.

D) The effect is independent of the cause.

Key Takeaway
Knowing a cause entails knowing its effect, but knowing an effect does not entail knowing its cause because the effect's contingency ties it to some cause, not a particular one.

The Asymmetry of Causal Knowledge
The author presents a fourth objection and answer about knowledge of things and their necessities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of an effect does not necessarily entail knowledge of its specific cause, even though knowledge of a cause does entail knowledge of its effect.

Core Idea
Causal knowledge is asymmetric: cause implies effect, but effect does not imply cause.

This lesson tackles a subtle but important objection in the philosophy of causation. The objection claims that if knowing a cause gives knowledge of its effect, then knowing an effect should also give knowledge of its cause. However, our everyday experience shows we know ourselves without knowing the higher causes that created us. The author resolves this by explaining the asymmetry: a specific cause uniquely determines its effect, but an effect's possibility only requires some cause, not a particular one. Let's work through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the full passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the structure: first the objection, then the answer.
Pay attention to the terms 'cause' (علة) and 'effect' (معلول).
2
Understand the objection
The objection claims symmetry: if knowledge of cause implies knowledge of effect, then knowledge of effect should imply knowledge of cause. The counterexample is our knowledge of ourselves without knowledge of higher causes.
3
Analyze the answer
The author distinguishes: a specific cause by its essence produces a specific effect, so knowing the cause yields knowledge of that specific effect. But a specific effect only requires a cause in general, not a specific one, due to its possibility (إمكان). Hence knowledge of the effect does not pinpoint its specific cause.
The key concept is 'إمكان' (possibility) which does not necessitate a particular cause.
4
Reflect on the asymmetry
Reflect on why this asymmetry is rational and not contradictory. The difference lies in the nature of causation: cause is sufficient for effect, but effect is not sufficient for cause because many causes could produce the same effect.
This resolves the initial objection without contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع : كما أن المعلول من لوازم العلة ، فكذلك العلة أيضاً من لوازم المعلول ، فلو لزم من العلم بالعلة ، العلم بالمعلول وجب أيضاً أن يلزم من العلم بالمعلول العلم بالعلة ، لكنا نعلم ذواتنا المخصوصة وأحوال ذواتنا . ولا نزاع أن ذواتنا وأحوال ذواتنا ، معلولات للعلل العالية ، فلو لزم من العلم بالشيء العلم بلوازمه، لزم أن يلزم من علمنا بذواتنا وأحوال ذواتنا ، علمنا بالعلل العالية على التفصيل التام. وحيث لم يلزم ذلك ، علمنا : أن العلم بالشيء لا يوجب العلم بلوازمه [جرم؟] واعلم أنه يمكن الجواب عن هذا السؤال . فيقال : العلم بالعلة يوجب العلم بالمعلول . أما العلم بالمعلول ، فإنه لا يوجب العلم بالعلة . والفرق بين الصورتين : أن العلة المعينة توجب لعينها وقواتها ذلك المعلول المعين ، فلا يصح أن يقال : إنه يلزم من العلم بتلك العلة المعينة العلم بذلك المعلول المعين . أما المعلول المعين فإنه إنما يفتقر لإمكانه إلى العلة : والإمكان لا يحوجه إلى تلك العلة ، وإلا لزم أن يكون كل ممكن محتاجاً إلى تلك العلة المعينة بعينها . ومعلوم أن الأمر ليس كذلك ، بل الإمكان يحوجه إلى علة ما ، فأما وقوعه بتلك العلة المعينة فإنما كان لأجل أنه لما وجدت تلك العلة وأوجبته ، لا جرم استند ذلك المعلول إليه ، فيثبت أن ذلك المعلول لا يقتضي الاحتياج إلى هذه العلة بعينها . وإنما يقتضي الاحتياج إلى مطلق العلة ، فلا جرم لم يلزم من العلم بالمعلول المعين العلم بعلته المعينة . وهذا فرق واضح معقول بين الصورتين . وبالله التوفيق.
Fourth: Just as the effect is among the necessary concomitants of the cause, so too the cause is among the necessary concomitants of the effect. So if knowledge of the cause entails knowledge of the effect, it must also be that knowledge of the effect entails knowledge of the cause. Yet we know our specific selves and the states of our selves. There is no dispute that our selves and our states are effects of the higher causes. So if knowledge of a thing entailed knowledge of its necessary concomitants, then it would follow that from our knowledge of our selves and our states, we would have knowledge of the higher causes in full detail. And since that does not follow, we know that knowledge of a thing does not necessitate knowledge of its necessary concomitants. [Then a response:] And know that it is possible to answer this question. It is said: Knowledge of the cause necessitates knowledge of the effect. As for knowledge of the effect, it does not necessitate knowledge of the cause. The difference between the two situations is that the specific cause by its very essence and powers necessitates that specific effect, so it is not correct to say that knowledge of that specific cause entails knowledge of that specific effect. As for the specific effect, it only depends for its possibility on the cause; possibility does not make it need that particular cause, otherwise every possible thing would need that specific cause. And it is known that matters are not so; rather, possibility makes it need some cause. As for its occurrence with that specific cause, that is only because when that cause existed and necessitated it, that effect was necessarily dependent on it. So it is established that that effect does not require need for this specific cause; rather, it requires need for an absolute cause. Hence knowledge of the specific effect does not entail knowledge of its specific cause. This is a clear and reasonable difference between the two situations. And with Allah is the granting of success.
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage presents a fourth objection and answer within a larger discussion about whether knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of its necessary concomitants. The objection: if knowledge of a cause (عِلَّة) implies knowledge of its effect (مَعْلُول), then conversely, knowledge of the effect should also imply knowledge of the cause. However, we know ourselves and our particular states (أحوال ذواتنا) without detailed knowledge of the higher causes (العلل العالية) that produce them. Since our selves are effects of those higher causes, if knowledge of the effect entailed knowledge of the cause, we would have full knowledge of those causes—which we do not. Therefore, the original claim that knowledge of a thing entails knowledge of its necessities is false. The author then provides an answer: knowledge of a cause does necessitate knowledge of a specific effect because a specific cause uniquely produces that effect. However, knowledge of a specific effect does not necessitate knowledge of its specific cause because the effect's possibility (إمكان) only requires some cause in general, not that particular cause. If it required a specific cause, then every possible thing would be dependent on that same specific cause, which is absurd. Thus the entailment is asymmetric. The difference is clear: a specific cause is sufficient to determine its effect, but a specific effect is not sufficient to determine its specific cause.

Key Terms
علة (cause)
معلول (effect)
لازم (necessary concomitant)
علم (knowledge)
إمكان (possibility)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the entailment between cause and effect is symmetric: if knowledge of cause implies effect, then knowledge of effect implies cause.
Correction: The author shows that a specific cause determines its effect uniquely, but a specific effect does not determine its cause because its possibility only requires some cause in general, not a specific one.

Checkpoint

A) Because the effect depends on multiple causes.
B) Because the effect's possibility only requires some cause, not this particular one.
C) Because the cause is unknown to us.
D) Because the effect is not a necessary concomitant of the cause.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge of an effect does not entail knowledge of its specific cause because the effect's possibility only requires a cause in general, not a particular one, making causal knowledge asymmetric.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know your own self and its states, but do you really know why you exist? The lesson says you don't know the higher cause in detail. How does this gap between self-knowledge and ultimate cause make you feel about the need for revelation?

The Need for a Sufficient Cause for Divine Knowledge
Objection that actual knowledge needs a sufficient cause beyond possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that possibility alone does not guarantee actual knowledge; a sufficient cause (muqtada) is required.

Core Idea
Possibility is not enough; actuality demands a sufficient cause.

The objector acknowledges that God's essence could be knowing, but argues that actual knowledge requires a specific cause. This objection highlights the gap between mere capacity and actual occurrence, emphasizing the need for a sufficient cause (muqtada). Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
2
Identify the core objection
Notice the distinction between possibility (ḥukm) and actuality. The objector concedes possibility but insists on a sufficient cause for actual knowledge.
The key term is 'muqtada' (sufficient cause).
3
Understand the explanation
The passage explains that the sufficient cause is either God's essence or something else. If neither works, the effect is absent.
This shows the objector's logical structure.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this objection matters for divine attributes: it challenges the actuality of God's knowledge without a cause.

The ninth question: Suppose it is established that it is valid for God's essence to be knowing of things. However, the ruling (of possibility) is not sufficient for the occurrence of the speaker being a speaker, rather a sufficient cause (al-muqtada) must also be present. So this state, although possible in relation to the speaker, yet the sufficient cause for it does not exist, so it remains impossible due to the non-existence of the sufficient cause, not due to the lack of capability. The explanation of the non-existence of the sufficient cause: The sufficient cause for the occurrence of that capability is either that specific essence or something else. If that specific essence does not necessitate this effect and no other is suitable for this necessitation, then the sufficient cause is absent, and the effect is lost because of the absence of the sufficient cause, even if the capability exists.

Commentary

The sufficient cause could be God's essence itself or something else. If God's essence does not necessitate knowledge of things (as the opponent holds) and nothing else can cause it, then knowledge does not actually occur. The objector distinguishes between mere possibility and actual existence, emphasizing that without a sufficient cause, knowledge remains impossible.

Objection and Answer on Causal Efficacy and Neediness
Objection that causal efficacy implies dependence with a brief answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand a classic kalam objection that causal efficacy implies dependence on the recipient, and the standard approach of defusing it via a distinction.

Core Idea
Causal efficacy creates a relation that may imply neediness.

This lesson presents a typical dialectical move in kalam: an opponent argues that if something is effectual in another, it must stand in a relation that depends on that other, thereby needing it. The author responds by hinting that a distinction can be drawn—perhaps between the agent's essence and its relation—to preserve independence. Understanding this exchange is key to grasping how Muslim theologians defended divine causation without compromising God's self-sufficiency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation once. Notice that the passage has two parts: an objection (starting with 'ولقائل أن يقول') and a brief answer ('ويمكن ذكر الفرق').
The objection uses conditional logic: if effectual then neediness.
2
Identify the objection's premise
The objector claims that 'effecting in another' is a specific relation ('نسبة مخصوصة') that cannot be completed without the other. Understand why this makes the agent dependent on the other.
The term 'نسبة مخصوصة' refers to a relation that is not part of the agent's essence.
3
Analyze the conclusion
From the premise, the objector concludes that if something were effectual, it would need the other ('افتقاره إلى الغير'). Consider what kind of being would be contradicted by such neediness.
In kalam, this objection is often aimed at those who affirm real causal powers in creatures.
4
Examine the answer
The author says a distinction can be mentioned between the two ('الفرق بينهما'). Think about what two things might be distinguished—likely the relation itself and the agent's essence or self-sufficiency.
The answer is only sketched; the author does not elaborate, leaving room for further discussion.
5
Reflect on the dialectical function
Recognize that this objection is not a denial of causation but a test of conceptual coherence. Reflect on how such objections help clarify theological positions.
This method is common in kalam to sharpen definitions and expose hidden assumptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول : فهذا الكلام يمنع من كونه مؤثراً في الغير، لأن التأثير في الغير نسبة مخصوصة بينه وبين الغير، وذلك مما لا يتم إلا بالغير، فلو كان مؤثراً في الغير لزم افتقاره إلى الغير، ويمكن ذكر الفرق بينهما من بعض الوجوه. والله أعلم.
And someone may say: This statement prevents it from being effectual in the other, because effecting in the other is a specific relation between it and the other, and that cannot be completed except by the other. So if it were effectual in the other, it would necessitate its neediness of the other. And a distinction can be mentioned between them in some respects. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 164

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a brief answer concerning the notion of causal efficacy. The objector argues that if something is said to be effectual in another (i.e., to have an effect on something else), then by definition this involves a specific relation between the agent and the recipient. This relation cannot exist without the recipient. Therefore, if the agent were to be effectual in the other, it would be dependent on that other, contradicting the idea of independent agency. The author then notes that a distinction can be drawn between the two (possibly between the relation and the agent's self-sufficiency) without elaborating, and concludes with 'And God knows best.' This is a standard format in kalam: raising an objection and then indicating a possible answer without fully expanding.

Key Terms
مؤثر (effectual)
تأثير (effecting)
نسبة مخصوصة (specific relation)
افتقار (neediness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection is a denial of causation itself.
Correction: It is a dialectical move to test the coherence of the opponent's position, not a rejection of causation.

Checkpoint
What does the objector claim about the relation required for causal efficacy?
A) It is innate to the agent.
B) It is a specific relation that depends on the other.
C) It is impossible to define.
D) It is the same as the agent's essence.

Key Takeaway
The objection that causal efficacy implies dependence hinges on the inseparability of the relation from the relata, but a distinction can preserve the agent's independence.

The Second Proof: Non-existence Cannot Be Known
The second proof that non-existent cannot be known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical argument that non-existent things cannot be known by God, including the objection and its rebuttal.

Core Idea
Pure non-existence has no distinctions, so it cannot be known.

This lesson presents the second proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah against the claim that God knows non-existent objects. The argument is a syllogism that derives a contradiction from the opponent's position. We will walk through the premises, the objection, and the response.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the structure: a premise, an objection, and a rebuttal.
2
Identify the syllogism's premises
The first half states: (1) a thing before existence is pure non-existence, (2) pure non-existence cannot have distinction, (3) every known thing must be distinct and have attributes. Therefore, non-existence cannot be known.
3
Understand the objection
The opponent says quiddities are determined in non-existence. The author responds that those who affirm non-existence as a thing do not attribute composition or sensible qualities to it.
This is a dialectical move.
4
Reflect on the implication
The author points out that if the opponent accepts God knows essences with attributes before existence, they should also accept that God knows quiddities before occurrence. This undermines their own objection.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : قالوا الشيء قبل دخوله في الوجود عدم محض ونفي صرف ، والعدم المحض يمتنع أن يحصل فيه الامتياز والاختصاص ( بالصفـة والخاصية ، وكل معلوم فإنه لا بد وأن يكون متميزاً عن غيره ، وأن يكون موصوفاً بصفات ) (۱) وخواص لأجلها يحصل ذلك الامتياز . وهذا القياس ينتج أن المعدوم يمتنع كونه معلوماً ، ولا يقال : إنا إذا قلنا : الماهيات متقررة حال العدم . ، فقد زال هذا السؤال . لأنا نقول : هذا لا يفيد المطلوب . وذلك لأن القائلين بأن المعدوم شيء ، لا يقولون : إن تلك الذوات المعدومة تكون مؤلفة مركبة موصوفة بالشكل واللون والحصول في الحيز (۳) ، فيلزمكم أن تقولوا : إنه تعالى لا يعلم كون الذوات موصوفة بهذه الصفات ، إلا عند دخولها في الوجود ، وأنتم لا تقولون به ، وإذا جاز أن تقولوا : إنه تعالى يعلم كون هذه الذوات موصوفة بهذه الصفات . مع أن هذا غير حاصل في العلم ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال أيضاً : إنه تعالى يعلم بالماهيات قبل حصولها . مع أنها لا تكون حاصلة في العدم ؟
The second proof: They said: The thing before its entry into existence is pure non-existence and absolute negation, and pure non-existence is impossible for distinction and specification (with attribute and property, and every known thing must necessarily be distinct from others and be described with attributes) (1) and properties by which that distinction occurs. And this syllogism yields that the non-existent is impossible to be known. And let it not be said: When we say that the quiddities are determined in the state of non-existence, this objection has been removed. For we say: This does not achieve the desired end. That is because those who say that the non-existent is a thing do not say that those non-existent essences are composed, composite, described with form, color, and location in space (3), so it would be necessary for you to say that He, exalted, does not know the essences described with these attributes except upon their entry into existence, and you do not say that. And if it is permissible for you to say that He, exalted, knows these essences described with these attributes despite that not being present in knowledge, then why is it not permissible to also say that He, exalted, knows the quiddities before their occurrence, even though they are not present in non-existence?
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a complete argument: the second proof against the claim that God knows non-existent things. The proof begins by stating that a thing before existence is pure non-existence, which cannot have distinctions or qualities. Since every known thing must be distinct and qualified, a non-existent cannot be known. The author then anticipates an objection: some say quiddities are determined in non-existence, which would avoid the problem. The response is that this objection fails because those who affirm that the non-existent is a thing do not claim that non-existent essences have composition, form, color, or spatial location. If one accepts that God knows these essences with such attributes even when they are not present in knowledge, then one should also accept that God knows quiddities before they occur, even though they do not exist. Thus the objection leads to a contradiction for the opponent.

Misconception: That the argument denies God's knowledge of all things, including those that exist.
Correction: The argument only targets knowledge of non-existent things before creation, not God's knowledge of existent things.

Checkpoint

B) Because God's knowledge is limited to existents
C) Because non-existence is eternal

The second proof uses the impossibility of distinction in pure non-existence to show that non-existent things cannot be objects of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Does your existence now give you an identity that even God's knowledge depended on your coming into being?
?
The opponent claims quiddities are determined in non-existence, but the proof counters that they lack composition and attributes. When you imagine yourself before you existed, do you truly believe God saw you as a complete person? Or is that belief a cover for not wanting to admit you are entirely dependent on His creative act for every detail of who you are?
?
If God's knowledge of a thing is impossible before it exists because it has no distinctions, then how does that affect your view of your own self-importance? Does your existence make you 'knowable' to God in a way you weren't before, or is God's eternal knowledge something else entirely? Sit with the discomfort that your very identity is a gift of existence, not a pre-existing fact.

The Third Proof: Circularity in Knowing Non-existents
Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents the third proof showing circularity in knowing non-existents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical circularity argument against the claim that God knows pure non-existent things.

Core Idea
Knowledge requires an established known; non-existents cannot be known without circularity.

The third proof in Al-Matalib al-Aliyah targets the claim that God knows non-existent things. It argues that knowledge is a relation needing both terms established, but a non-existent cannot be established without prior knowledge, leading to an impossible circularity.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the logical flow from definition to conclusion.
The proof is concise; every phrase is important.
2
Understand the definition of knowledge
The passage defines knowledge as a specific relation (نسبة مخصوصة) between knower and known. Note that this relation requires both terms to be established (تقرير) before the relation can exist.
This is a relational definition common in Islamic philosophy.
3
Apply to the non-existent case
If the known is non-existent (معدوم), it only comes into existence through divine power (قدرة). But that power itself depends on God's knowledge of that thing. Thus, knowledge would be needed before the known exists, but the known must exist before knowledge—creating a circle.
This is the core of the circularity argument.
4
Recognize the logical conclusion
The proof concludes that such circularity (دور) is impossible (محال). Therefore, the premise that God knows pure non-existents is false.
Circularity is a logical fallacy; this argument uses it to refute the claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن نقول : قد بينا أنه لا معنى للعلم إلا أنه نسبة مخصوصة وإضافة مخصوصة تحصل بين العالم وبين المعلوم ، وحصول الإضافة بين الأمرين مسبوقة بتقرير كل واحد منهما ، فوجب أن يكون العلم بالمعلوم مسبوقاً بتقرير ذلك المعلوم في نفسه ، لكن المعلوم إذا كان معدوماً فهو إنما ينتقل إلى الوجود بتأثير القدرة فيه، فيكون حصوله متأخراً عن تأثير القدرة فيه ، الذي هو مشروط بكونه عالماً به. فيلزم وقوع الدور وهو محال .
The third proof: That we say: We have shown that the meaning of knowledge is nothing but a specific relation and a specific addition that obtains between the knower and the known, and the obtaining of the addition between the two things is preceded by the establishment of each one of them. So it is necessary that knowledge of the known is preceded by the establishment of that known in itself. But if the known is non-existent, it only transitions to existence through the effect of power upon it, so its occurrence is posterior to the effect of power upon it, which is conditioned by His being knowing of it. So circularity occurs, and it is impossible.
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage is a concise proof against knowledge of non-existents. It defines knowledge as a relation (نسبة) between knower and known. For this relation to exist, both terms must be established (متقرر) beforehand. If the known is non-existent, it only comes into existence through divine power, which is itself conditioned by God's knowledge of it. This creates a circular dependency: knowledge requires the known to exist, but the known's existence requires knowledge. Such circularity (دور) is impossible. Thus, the claim that God knows non-existent things is false.

Key Terms
نسبة مخصوصة
إضافة مخصوصة
تقرير
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof denies God's knowledge of future contingents.
Correction: The proof only targets knowledge of a pure non-existent before any creative act. It does not address God's knowledge of future events through other means.

Checkpoint
What logical problem does the third proof identify in the claim that God knows non-existent things?
A) Infinite regress.
B) Circularity.
C) Contradiction with divine simplicity.
D) Incompatibility with free will.

Key Takeaway
The argument shows that knowing a pure non-existent leads to circularity, so it is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Before you existed, God knew you in a way that does not require your prior existence. Does that truth make you feel more secure in His plan for you, or does it challenge you to take your purpose more seriously? How will you respond to that knowledge today?
?
If God's knowledge of you is not dependent on your actions, does that change how you view your own shortcomings? Will you let that drive you to repentance or to complacency? Choose one and commit to it.
?
The proof shows that knowledge of a pure non-existent is impossible. But God knows you now that you exist. Are you living as if you are truly known by the One who created you? What would change if you fully believed that? Name one concrete action you will take this week.

The Philosophers' Objection: Contingent Causation is Unintelligible
The philosophers present two objections to the theologians' definition of will, focusing on the unintelligibility of contingent causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' logical objection that the theologians' definition of will implies an unintelligible concept of contingent causation.

Core Idea
A cause either necessarily produces its effect or is no cause at all.

The theologians defined will as an attribute that tips the scales of a possible act without necessitating it. The philosophers reject this, arguing that if an agent truly acts, its effect must follow necessarily. In this passage, they present their first major objection: the very notion of a contingent cause is logically incoherent. We also see the beginning of a second objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice that the philosophers present two objections; the first is detailed, the second is cut off.
The passage references an earlier discussion on the efficacious agent.
2
Examine the first objection
The philosophers analyze the definition implying an agent that acts without necessary causation. They pose a dilemma: either the agent meets all conditions for efficacy (effect necessary) or not (effect impossible). Thus 'possible efficacy' is unintelligible.
This is a classic deterministic argument in Islamic philosophy.
3
Consider the second objection
The second objection begins by conceding the possibility of conceiving a contingent agent, but it is incomplete in our text. Reflect on what might follow.
The full objection may have been given on the next page.
4
Summarize the philosophers' position
In your own words, explain why the philosophers think the theologians' definition of will is problematic. The key point is that cause and effect are either necessarily connected or not at all.
This objection challenges the very foundation of free will in divine action.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قالت الفلاسفة : أما قولكم : الإرادة صفة تقتضي ترجيح أحد طرفي : الممكن على الآخر من غير وجوب ، ومن غير تأثير كلام مشكل من وجهين : الأول : أن هذا الكلام يقتضي إثبات مؤثر يؤثر لا على سبيل الوجوب ، وحينئذ تعود المباحث المذكور في باب تأثير القادر. وذلك لأن المؤثر إما أن يكون مستجمعاً لجميع الأمور المعتبرة في المؤثرية. وإما أن لا يكون . فإن كان الأول وجب ترتب الأثر عليه، فيكون ذلك المؤثر مؤثراً على سبيل الوجوب ، لا على سبيل الصحة ، وإن كان الثاني كان ترتب الأثر عليه ممتنعاً فيكون ذلك ممتنع التأثير، لا يمكن التأثير ( فعلمنا أن الشيء إما أن يكون واجب التأثير أو ممتنع التأثير، فأما أن يكون ممكن التأثير ) فهذا غير معقول ، وتمام تقرير هذا البحث قد سبق ذكره في باب القادر . والثاني : هب أنا إذا عقلنا وجود مؤثر يؤثر على سبيل الصحة - إلا أن
The philosophers said: As for your statement: 'Will is an attribute that necessitates the preponderance of one of the two sides of the possible over the other, without necessity and without causation,' it is problematic in two respects: The first: That this statement entails affirming an agent that acts not in a necessary manner. Then the discussions mentioned in the chapter on the efficacy of the powerful agent return. That is because the agent either possesses all the conditions considered in efficacy, or it does not. If the first, then the effect must follow from it, so that agent is an agent in a necessary manner, not in a contingent manner; and if the second, then the effect following from it is impossible, so that agent is incapable of causation, it cannot cause. (Thus we know that a thing is either necessarily efficacious or impossibly efficacious; as for being possibly efficacious, that is unintelligible.) The full elaboration of this discussion has already been mentioned in the chapter on the powerful agent. The second: Even if we conceive the existence of an agent that acts in a contingent manner—
Source pages p. 177

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' objection to the theologians' definition of will. They raise two problems. The first objection argues that the definition implies an agent that acts without necessity, but then the analysis of causation shows that an agent either has all conditions for effect (making the effect necessary) or not (making it impossible), so a 'possible' causation is incoherent. This objection references an earlier discussion about the powerful agent. The second objection begins but is cut off in the available text; it concedes the possibility of conceiving a contingent cause but then presumably raises further issues. The passage demonstrates the philosophers' commitment to a deterministic understanding of causation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers reject the concept of will entirely.
Correction: The philosophers do not reject will; they reject the logical coherence of a cause that acts without necessity. They argue that causation must be necessary if all conditions are present.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the theologians' definition of will implies an incoherent notion of contingent causation; causation must be either necessary or impossible.

Are you ready to take full ownership of your actions without hiding behind 'possibility'?
