Refutation of the Objection on Choice Without Preference
The author refutes the claim that choice can occur without any preference or benefit.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why every rational choice requires a preferrer (murajjih) and how even seemingly equal options are chosen due to subtle benefits or desires.

Core Idea
No choice occurs without a mental preferrer tied to benefit or harm.

This passage tackles a classic objection to the necessity of a preferrer in human choice. The objector claims that a person can pick one of two identical loaves or cups without any preference. The author dismantles this argument by first clarifying that 'preference' (tarjīḥ) and 'creation' (takwīn) are one and the same, then exposing hidden mental preferrers in allegedly equal alternatives.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main parts
The passage has two sections: a clarification of the relationship between tarjīḥ and takwīn, and a two-aspect answer to the objection. Recognize these before diving into details.
2
Understand the identity of preference and creation
The author argues that we only understand preference as the occurrence of one possibility, which is exactly creation. So the objector's distinction collapses.
3
Analyze the first aspect of the answer
The author denies that the two options are truly equal. He shows that the agent always imagines differences—closeness, ease, benefit—and that the strength of desire for one prevents considering the other.
Note the distinction between occurrence, persistence, and recollection of the mental preferrer.
4
Analyze the second aspect of the answer
Even if we grant equality of options, the underlying desire for eating/drinking is itself a preference for benefit over harm. Thus the action is still motivated by benefit-seeking.
This shows that all human actions are fundamentally based on benefit and harm.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the objection fails because every choice has a preferrer. Consider how this applies to daily decisions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هذا - هو القدرة فيها الفرق بين القدرة وبين الإرادة ؟ أما قوله : إنها تؤثر في الترجيح لا في التكوين فنقول : هذا الكلام إنما يتم ببيان الفرق بين الترجيح وبين التكوين ، فإنا لا نعقل من الترجيح إلا وقوع أحد جانبي الممكن : بسببه ، وهذا هو التكوين. وإثبات مفهوم آخر يسمى بالترجيح والتخصيص مغاير للمفهوم الحاصل من التكوين أمر غير معقول . وأما قوله : المخير بين أكل الرغيفين وشرب القدحين ، يختار أحدهما من غير جلب منفعة ، أو دفع مضرة يختص بها ذلك الواحد . فنقول : الكلام عليه من وجهين : الأول : لا نسلم أنهما يتساويان في جلب المنافع ، والمصالح المتعلقة بالأبدان ، بل لا بد وأن يتخيل أن أحدهما أقرب إليه ، وأحدهما أسهل عليه ، وهو أنفع في نفسه ، وربما كان عظيم الحرص على الأكل والشرب ، فلما وقع بصره على أحدهما عظمت رغبته فيه . فصارت قوة رغبته فيه مانعة له عن تحويل النظر عنه إلى غيره ، فبقيت رغبته مقصورة عليه وغير متعدية منه إلى غيره . وبالجملة : فحصول المرجح الذهني غير، وبقاء ذلك المرجح غير . وتذكر ذلك المرجح بعد نسيانه غير. ولا يلزم من فقدان هذا الثالث فقدان الثاني والأول . والوجه الثاني في الجواب : هب أنه يأخذ أحدهما دون الآخر لا لمرجح . إلا أن الذي لأجله صدر عنه هذا الفعل شدة رغبته في أصل الأكل والشرب وتلك الرغبة عبارة عن طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة، فلولا طلب النفع لما أقدم على أخذ أحد الرغيفين، وشرب أحد القدحين ، ولولا الفرار من دفع ضرر السبع ، لما اختار سلوك أحد الطريقين ، فيثبت أن الحامل له على هذا الفعل ليس إلا طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة . وحينئذ يعود ما ذكرناه .
This—is there a difference between power and will? As for his saying: 'It affects preference (tarjīḥ) not creation (takwīn),' we say: This statement is only complete by explaining the difference between preference (tarjīḥ) and creation (takwīn), for we do not conceive of preference (tarjīḥ) except as the occurrence of one of the two sides of the possible, and that is creation (takwīn). And affirming another concept called preference and specification (al-takṣīṣ) distinct from the concept obtained from creation (takwīn) is an unintelligible matter. And as for his saying: 'The one given a choice between eating two loaves and drinking two cups chooses one of them without bringing benefit or repelling harm specific to that one,' we say: The discussion about it is from two aspects: First, we do not concede that they are equal in bringing benefits and bodily interests; rather he must imagine that one is closer to him, one is easier for him, and it is more beneficial in itself, and perhaps he has great eagerness for eating and drinking. So when his sight falls on one of them, his desire for it intensifies. Thus the strength of his desire for it prevents him from turning his gaze from it to another, so his desire remains confined to it and not transferred from it to another. In summary: the occurrence of the mental preferrer (al-murajjiḥ al-dhihnī) is one thing, the persistence of that preferrer is another, and the recollection of that preferrer after forgetting it is yet another. And the loss of this third does not necessitate the loss of the second and the first. The second aspect of the answer: Grant that he takes one without a preferrer; however, what caused this action from him is the intensity of his desire for the very act of eating and drinking, and that desire is an expression of seeking benefit and repelling harm. So were it not for seeking benefit, he would not have proceeded to take one of the two loaves or drink one of the two cups; and were it not for fleeing from repelling the harm of the lion, he would not have chosen to take one of the two paths. Thus it is established that what impels him to this action is nothing but seeking benefit and repelling harm, and then our statement returns.
Source pages p. 178

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection regarding human choice without preference. The objector argues that will (irāda) affects preference (tarjīḥ) but not creation (takwīn), and that one can choose between two equal options without seeking benefit or avoiding harm. The author responds by first clarifying that preference and creation are identical; we only understand preference as causing one possibility to occur, which is creation. Then he addresses the example of choosing between two loaves and two cups with two arguments. First, he denies that the options are truly equal: the agent always imagines one as nearer, easier, or more beneficial, and his desire concentrates on it. The strength of that desire prevents him from considering the other. He distinguishes between the occurrence of the mental preferrer, its persistence, and its recollection; loss of recollection does not negate the occurrence or persistence. Second, even if we grant choice without a specific preferrer, the underlying motive is the intense desire for eating and drinking itself, which is itself seeking benefit and avoiding harm. Thus all human action is driven by benefit and harm.

Key Terms
ترجيح
تكوين
مرجح
رغبة
منفعة
مضرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A person can choose between two completely equal alternatives without any reason or preference.
Correction: The author shows that there is always a mental preferrer, either a subtle difference in the options or the underlying desire for benefit, which serves as the preferrer.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument in this passage, what prevents a person from turning his attention from one option to another?
A) The person’s lack of desire for the other option.
B) The strength of his desire for the first option becomes a barrier.
C) The options are inherently unequal in benefit.
D) The person forgets the other option.

Key Takeaway
Every human choice, even between seemingly equal options, is ultimately driven by a mental preferrer linked to seeking benefit or avoiding harm.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever convinced yourself that you made a choice without any conscious reason, when in fact your heart was already attached to a hidden benefit? How does this lesson expose the self-deception in that claim? Will you now examine your motives more honestly before Allah?

The author argues that even your simplest choices—like which loaf to eat—are driven by a mental preferrer. What does this say about your accountability before Allah for every seemingly trivial decision? Are you conscious of the motives behind your daily choices, and how can you align them with seeking His pleasure?
?
If all your actions are ultimately motivated by seeking benefit or avoiding harm, what does that reveal about your relationship with Allah? Is your worship driven by genuine love or by the pursuit of reward and fear of punishment? How can you purify your intentions to make your choices purely for Him?

Refutation of the objection on desire versus will
The author refutes the claim that one can desire something but not will it, using benefit and harm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the claim that desire and will are independent by showing both are driven by perceived benefit and harm, and why this makes divine will unintelligible.

Core Idea
Will and desire both stem from seeking benefit and avoiding harm; God, having no needs, cannot have will in this sense.

In this passage, the author addresses a second objection: that a person can desire something without willing it. He uses two examples—the sick person craving fruit and the ascetic willing worship without craving—to argue that both involve weighing present and future benefit and harm. This leads to a key conclusion about the nature of will and its inapplicability to God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of the argument. Notice the objection and the two examples.
The passage is a continuous refutation; follow the reasoning step by step.
2
Identify the two examples
Find the example of the sick person who craves fruit but does not will it, and the ascetic who wills worship without craving. Notice how the author analyzes each.
These examples challenge the idea that desire and will are independent.
3
Understand the weighing process
The author explains that in both cases, the agent weighs immediate pleasure/pain against future consequences using the analogy of cash vs. credit.
This is the core mechanism: will follows the preponderant imagined benefit.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The author concludes that will and aversion are nothing but nature's inclination toward benefit and away from harm. Since this is impossible for God, attributing will to Him is unintelligible.
This refutes the objection and clarifies the meaning of will.
5
Reflect on significance
Consider why this argument matters for the concept of divine attributes. How does it affect the understanding of God's will?
This connects to broader discussions in Islamic theology about divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما قوله ثانياً : إن المريض قد يشتهي أكل الفاكهة ثم لا يريد أكلها ، والزاهد العابد قد يريد الطاعات والعبادات ، مع أنه لا يشتهيها فنقول : هذا أيضاً مغالطة. وذلك لأن المريض يميل طبعه إلى تحصيل اللذات الحاصلة في الحال بسبب أكل الفاكهة، وينفر طبعه عن الآلام التي تحصل بسبب ذلك الأكل في الزمان المستقبل ، فراعى مراتب المنفعة والمضرة ، فإن كان جانب اللذة والمنفعة راجحاً في خياله على جانب الألم والمضرة ، أقدم على الأكل ، وإلا تركه ، فيثبت أن الحامل والداعي مهنا ليس إلا طلب المنفعة ودفع المضرة إلا أن اللذة الحاصلة بسبب الأكل حاضرة في الحال ، والألم الحاصل بسبب ذلك الأكل مستقبل . وقد ذكرنا في باب الدواعي والصوارف : أن النقد خير من النسيئة بسبب كونه نقداً لا نسيئة ، إلا أن النسيئة قد تكون أعظم حالا من النقد ، بسبب القوة والكثرة ، فتصير راجحة على النقد، فإن قضى الفكر والخيال أحد الجانبين حصل الرجحان لا محالة ، وإن لم يقص فيه بالترجيح ، بل بقي مضطرباً فيه ، لا جرم يصير مضطرباً في الفعل والشرك [؟] . وهذا هو الجواب بعينه عن قولهم الزاهد العابد قد يريد الطاعات الشاقة ، مع أنه لا يميل طبعه إليها ، فإنا نقول تلك الطاعات مؤلمة في الحال ، ولكنها نافعة في المستقبل فالألم نقد ، والمنفعة نسيئة إلا أن تلك المنافع بحسب الجبلة عظيمة ، وتلك المضار قليلة ، فيقع الخاطر ههنا في باب المعارضة والترجيح فثبت بما ذكرنا : أنا لا نعرف البتة من معنى الإرادة والكراهية إلا ميل الطبع إلى جلب المنافع وميله إلى دفع المضار، ولما كان ذلك في حق الله تعالى ممتنعا كان إثبات الإرادة في حقه غير معقول . وهذا تمام الكلام في البحث عن معنى الإرادة والكراهية
And as for his second saying: 'The sick person may crave eating fruit, then not will to eat it, and the ascetic worshiper may will acts of obedience and worship while not craving them,' we say: This too is a fallacy. That is because the sick person's nature inclines toward obtaining the pleasures that occur immediately due to eating the fruit, and his nature recoils from the pains that occur due to that eating in the future; so he considers the degrees of benefit and harm. If the side of pleasure and benefit is preponderant in his imagination over the side of pain and harm, he proceeds to eat; otherwise he leaves it. Thus it is established that the impeller and motivator here is nothing but seeking benefit and repelling harm, except that the pleasure due to eating is present immediately, while the pain due to that eating is future. And we have mentioned in the chapter on motives (dawā'ī) and deterrents (ṣawārif) that ready cash is better than credit because it is cash, not credit, but credit may be greater in amount due to quantity and strength, so it becomes preponderant over cash. If thought and imagination decide one side, preponderance (rujḥān) occurs inevitably; and if it does not decide with preference but remains wavering, then he inevitably becomes wavering in action and doubt [؟]. And this is the very same answer to their saying: 'The ascetic worshiper may will arduous acts of obedience while his nature does not incline to them.' For we say: Those acts of obedience are painful immediately, but beneficial in the future; so the pain is cash, and the benefit is credit, except that those benefits are great by nature and those harms are small, so the mind falls into comparison and preference (mu'āraḍa and tarjīḥ). Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that we know no meaning of will (irāda) and aversion (karāha) except the inclination of nature toward bringing benefits and its inclination toward repelling harms. And since that is impossible with respect to God, affirming will (irāda) in His case is unintelligible. This completes the discussion on the meaning of will and aversion.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a second objection to the theory that will is always driven by benefit or harm. The objector gives two examples: a sick person craves fruit but does not will to eat it, and an ascetic wills worship without craving it. The author explains that in both cases, the agent weighs immediate pleasure vs. future pain (or vice versa). For the sick person, the present pleasure of eating competes with future harm; if the imagined benefit outweighs, he acts, otherwise he refrains. For the ascetic, the present pain of worship is like cash, while the future reward is like credit, but because the reward is vastly greater, it becomes preponderant. The author then generalizes: will is nothing but nature's inclination toward benefit and away from harm. Since this is impossible for God (Who has no nature or need), attributing will to God in this sense is unintelligible. This concludes the section on the meaning of will and aversion.

كراهية
منفعة
مضرة
دواعي
صوارف
نقد
نسيئة
ترجيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That will and desire are completely independent; a person can desire something without willing it.
Correction: The author shows that both desire and will are based on perceived benefit and harm. The sick person's craving is a desire for immediate pleasure, but he does not will it because he perceives future harm as outweighing it. Thus will follows the stronger imagined benefit.

Checkpoint
How does the author explain the sick person who craves fruit but does not will to eat it?
A) The sick person lacks the power to eat.
B) There is an internal conflict between pleasure and future pain.
C) The sick person actually wills but is prevented.
D) The craving is not real.

Key Takeaway
Will and desire are both rooted in seeking benefit and avoiding harm; since this is impossible for God, attributing will to Him in this sense is meaningless.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you weigh immediate pleasure against future harm in your own life, do you ever excuse yourself by saying your 'will' is separate from your 'desire'? How does this passage expose that as self-deception, and what will you do differently starting today?
?
Consider an act of worship you avoid because it feels hard or uncomfortable. According to this lesson, your lack of desire is a calculation that the immediate pain outweighs the future reward. Are you willing to recalibrate that calculation by increasing your faith in the immense credit of the afterlife?
?
If will means nothing more than a nature inclined toward benefit and away from harm, then what does your daily behavior reveal about what you truly believe is beneficial? How can you reshape your desires so they align with what God declares is truly beneficial?

Refutation of the Argument that Sound Passes Through Wall Pores
Refutes the claim that sound reaches the ear through pores in a wall.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the claim that sound travels through wall pores is false according to the author's reasoning.

Core Idea
Sound undulations cannot maintain their form through dense walls.

In this passage, the author tackles a common objection to the theory of sound transmission: that it passes through pores in a wall. He first presents the counterargument along with its supporting evidence, then systematically dismantles it by appealing to the physical nature of sound waves and the density of walls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the passage beginning with 'First: If a person speaks...' and identify the objection being presented: that sound can travel through pores in a wall.
The objection is that the wall has pores and openings that allow the undulating air to reach the ear.
2
Examine the evidence
Notice the example given: a person in a dark room with a door – hearing is clearer when the door is open than when closed. This is supposed to support the pore theory.
The opponents use this analogy to argue that sound passes through openings.
3
Follow the refutation
The author refutes by asserting that the wall is dense; the delicate wave form cannot survive contact with it, and any air that passes through pores loses its shape, making coherent hearing impossible.
Since we do hear through walls, the pore explanation must be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول : أن الإنسان إذا تكلم من وراء الجدار ، فإنا قد نسمع كلامه بالتمام والكمال ، ومن المحال أن يتأدى ذلك التموج إلينا بكماله وتمامه . لأن الجدار الحائل بين القائل وبين السامع عائق عن ذلك وليس لهم أن يقولوا : إنه حصل في الجدار مسام ومنافذ وثقب . فلا يمتنع ( تأدي ) (۱) ذلك الهواء المتموج في تلك الثقب والمنافذ إلى سطح الصماخ . قالوا : والدليل عليه : أن الإنسان إذا جلس في بيت ( مظلم ) (۲) ولذلك البيت باب ، وتكلم إنسان خارج ذلك البيت ، فإن كان باب البيت مفتوحاً كان السمع أكمل ، وإن كان باب البيت مردوداً صار السمع أضعف وأخفي . وذلك يدل على ما قلناه فإنا نقول : هذا القدر باطل . وذلك لأن الجدار جسم كثيف فالتموج الحاصل بسبب نطق ذلك المتكلم وكلامه ) إذا وصل إلى ذلك الجدار فإنه لا يبقى على صورته وعلى شكله ، فإن الشكل الحاصل في الهواء اللطيف ) (۳) ممتنع البقاء عند وصوله إلى الجدار الكثيف ، والذي ينفذ من ذلك الهواء في ثقب الجدار ، وفي مسامه الضيقة ، لا يبقى على ذلك الشكل الذي حدث خارج ، فوجب أن يمتنع سماع ذلك الكلام على شكله ونظمه ، وحيث حصل ، علمنا أن الذي قالوه باطل
First: If a person speaks from behind a wall, we may hear his speech completely and perfectly, yet it is impossible for those waves to reach us in their completeness and perfection, because the wall intervening between the speaker and the hearer is an obstacle to that. They cannot say: 'Pores, openings, and holes have occurred in the wall, so it is not impossible for that undulating air to travel through those holes and openings to the surface of the tympanum.' They said: The evidence for it is that if a person sits in a dark room that has a door, and a person outside that room speaks, if the door of the room is open, hearing is more complete; if the door is closed, hearing becomes weaker and fainter. That indicates what we said. Then we say: This amount is false, because the wall is a dense body. The undulation resulting from that speaker's speech when it reaches that wall does not remain in its form and shape. The shape occurring in the subtle air is impossible to remain upon reaching the dense wall. That which penetrates from that air through the holes of the wall and its narrow pores does not remain in that shape which occurred outside. Therefore, it is necessary that hearing that speech in its form and order is impossible. And since it occurs, we know that what they said is false.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of a counterargument against the thesis that hearing is not merely the transmission of sound waves. It begins by presenting an objection: one might claim that sound can pass through a wall via small pores and openings, and they provide an example of a door. The author refutes this by noting that the wall is a dense body; the delicate form of the undulation cannot survive contact with the dense wall, and any air that penetrates through narrow pores loses its original shape, making coherent hearing impossible. Since we do hear speech through walls, the objection is false.

Key Terms
تموج (tamawwuj)
جدار (jidaar)
مسام (masaam)
صماخ (simaak)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sound reaches the ear through pores in a wall.
Correction: The undulation loses its form when contacting the dense wall, and any air passing through pores cannot preserve the original shape, so hearing coherent speech through a wall cannot be explained by pores.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the claim that sound passes through a wall via pores?
A) Because the wall is too thick for any air to pass through.
B) Because the undulation loses its form when contacting the dense wall, and what passes through pores cannot retain the original shape.
C) Because the wall absorbs all sound completely.
D) Because the example of the door is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
The claim that sound passes through wall pores is false because the delicate wave form cannot survive contact with a dense wall.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

What will you do today to train yourself to see Allah's direct action behind every phenomenon?

This passage demonstrates that what seems physically obvious (sound passing through pores) can be false. Second Proof: Hearing Includes Direction Awareness
Proves hearing distinct by directional awareness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ability to perceive sound direction proves that hearing is a distinct cognitive state beyond mere physical ear affection.

Core Idea
Directional awareness proves hearing is more than eardrum vibration.

This passage presents the second argument from Imam al-Razi (or author) demonstrating that hearing is not identical to the physical impact on the eardrum. By reflecting on our immediate awareness of sound direction, we realize that hearing involves an extra cognitive state. The argument uses a clever analogy with touch to clarify the point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how the author states the observation about hearing direction. Then read the translation to grasp the full argument.
2
Identify the logical structure
The argument has four parts: observation, assumption, comparison with touch, conclusion. Find each part in the text.
3
Reflect on the analogy
Consider why the author compares hearing to touch. What does touch lack that hearing has? How does this strengthen the proof?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثانية : وهو أنا إذا سمعنا الصوت، فإنا نعلم أنا سمعناه من الجانب الأيمن أو الجانب الأيسر. وهذا يدل على أنا سمعنا الصوت في مكانه الذي حدث فيه. إذ لو قلنا : بأن المسموع ليس إلا القرع الذي حدث في داخل الصماخ فقط ، فإنه يجب أن لا يعرف بحسب القوة السامعة مكان ذلك الصوت وجهته ، كما أن القوة اللامسة لما لم تُحس إلا بالكيفية المدموسة حال وصولها إلى العضو فإنها لا تميز بين الجهة التي منها جاءت تلك الكيفية المحسوسة وبين غيرها . فيثبت بهذا أن الإدراك المسمى بالسماع حالة مغايرة لتأثر الصماخ وانفصاله من التموج الواصل إليه
The second argument: That when we hear a sound, we know that we heard it from the right side or the left side. This indicates that we heard the sound in its place where it occurred. For if we said that what is heard is only the striking that occurs inside the tympanum alone, then it would be necessary that the hearing faculty does not know the place of that sound and its direction, just as the faculty of touch, when it feels only the quality (of an object) upon its contact with the organ, does not distinguish between the direction from which that perceived quality came and other directions. Thus, it is established that the perception called hearing is a state different from the impression on the tympanum and its separation from the undulation reaching it.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument that hearing is not identical to the mere physical effect on the eardrum. The author points out that when we hear a sound, we know from which direction it came (right or left). If hearing were just the vibration of the eardrum, we would not have directional awareness, just as touch does not give direction. Therefore, hearing involves an additional state beyond the physical impression.

Key Terms
صماخ (simaak)
قوة سامعة (quwwa sāmi'a)
قوة لامسة (quwwa lāmasa)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that direction is inferred from differences in sound intensity or timing between ears.
Correction: The author argues that directional awareness is immediate and not derived from such physical differences, as evidenced by the fact that we intuitively know the direction without calculation. The argument shows that if hearing were only physical impact, direction would be unknowable, so direction must be a direct property of the hearing faculty.

Checkpoint
What does the second argument use to prove that hearing is distinct from mere tympanum impression?
A) The fact that sound can be heard from far away.
B) The ability to distinguish the direction of sound.
C) The variation in sound intensity.
D) The fact that we hear with both ears.

Key Takeaway
Directional awareness proves that hearing is a distinct cognitive state, not reducible to physical ear vibration.

Starting today, try to consciously notice the direction of sounds and let each one remind you that your perception is a direct gift from Allah.

Third Proof: Analogy with Vision Establishes Perception as Superadded
Uses logical conditional to prove hearing is superadded.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a logical conditional (modus tollens) is used to argue that hearing (and vision) are states additional to knowledge and physical impression.

Core Idea
Perception is not just physical impression; it is a superior state of disclosure.

This lesson presents the third of three arguments that perception (vision and hearing) is not merely a physical impression on the sense organ. Using a conditional logical form, the author shows that if hearing were merely impression, then a stronger impression would always yield stronger hearing. Since this is not observed, the premise is false, establishing that hearing (and vision) are superadded states of disclosure.

Guided Path
1
Understand the conditional structure
The argument is a modus tollens: if A then B, not B, therefore not A. Identify A (hearing equals impression) and B (stronger impression always gives stronger hearing).
Note the logical form: this is a standard deductive argument.
2
Examine the analogy with vision
The author says 'What we mentioned in [the case of] vision' – the same argument was applied to vision earlier. Reflect on why vision and hearing are analogous.
Both perceptions involve a physical impression but go beyond it.
3
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that hearing (and vision) is a state additional to both knowledge and the organ's impression. It provides a disclosure (inkishāf) and manifestation (tajallī) stronger than knowledge.
This elevates perception from mere sensation to a cognitive state.
4
Connect to broader context
This is the third argument; together with previous ones it establishes that perception is superadded. This matters for aqeeda – sense experience is not merely mechanical but a direct divine gift.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : ما ذكرناه في الإبصار وهو أنه لو كانت الحالة المسماة بالسماع عبارة عن هذا التأثر ، وجب أن يقال : كلما كان التأثر أقوى وأمكن ( وجب أن يكون السماع أكمل وأقوى ((۱) ولما كان الثاني باطلا . كان المقدم باطلاً . قيثبت بهذه الوجوه التي أوضحناها ، أن الإبصار حـالـة زائدة على العلم ، وعلى تأثر العين ، أو أن السماع حالة زائدة على العلم وعلى تأثر الأذن . ولا شك أنها حالة تفيد نوعاً من الانكشاف والتجلي ، أكمل وأقوى من الحالة المسماة بالعلم .
The third argument: What we mentioned in [the case of] vision: that if the state called hearing were this impression, it would be necessary to say: Whenever the impression is stronger and more powerful, hearing must be more perfect and stronger. And since the consequent is false, the antecedent is false. Thus, it is established by these arguments that we have clarified that vision is a state additional to knowledge and to the impression on the eye, or that hearing is a state additional to knowledge and to the impression on the ear. And there is no doubt that it is a state that conveys a kind of disclosure and manifestation, more perfect and stronger than the state called knowledge.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument using an analogy with vision. The author sets up a conditional: if hearing were merely the physical impression on the ear, then a stronger impression should yield stronger hearing. Since this is not always the case (the second part is false), the premise is false. He then concludes from all three arguments that vision and hearing are states additional to both knowledge and the physical impression on the respective organs. These states provide a disclosure (inkishāf) and manifestation (tajallī) that is superior to mere knowledge.

Key Terms
تأثر (ta'aththur)
انكشاف (inkishāf)
تجلي (tajallī)
علم (ʿilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that hearing involves any physical impression.
Correction: The argument does not deny that hearing involves an impression, but that it is not reducible to it. Hearing is a state additional to the impression.

Checkpoint
What is the logical structure of the third argument?
A) If hearing is impression, then stronger impression always gives stronger hearing; the consequent is false, so the antecedent is false.
B) Hearing is exactly the same as impression.
C) Stronger impression always gives weaker hearing.
D) The argument is analogical and not logical.

Key Takeaway
The third proof uses a conditional to show that perception is not reducible to physical impression but is a superior state of disclosure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the adhān, do you experience it as a mere sound wave hitting your eardrum, or as a divine call that discloses something greater? What would change in your heart if you consciously treated your hearing as an act of tajallī?

?

The Mutakallimun's Proof from Dependence
The mutakallimun's proof that everything other than God is temporally originated due to dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the mutakallimun's argument that everything other than God is temporally originated because its dependence cannot be a permanent feature.

Core Idea
Dependence ties everything to a moment of origination, making all non-God temporally originated.

The mutakallimun (theologians) build on the premise that everything other than God depends on Him. They argue that this dependence cannot be a permanent attribute of the dependent thing, for that would imply an absurdity: bringing something already existing into existence. Therefore, the dependence must be linked to the beginning of existence or to non-existence. This leads to the conclusion that everything other than God is temporally originated (muhdath), preceded by non-existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and its translation to get the overall flow of the argument. Notice the logical connectors.
The passage is a single argument, so follow each step.
2
Identify the key premise
Find where the mutakallimun state that everything other than God depends on Him. This is the foundation.
Dependence (iftiqar) is crucial.
3
Understand the impossibility argument
Why can't dependence be in the state of permanence? They say it would lead to 'takwin al-ka'in' (bringing into existence of the already existent), which is absurd.
This is the core logical move.
4
Explore the two alternatives
If not permanence, dependence must be either at origination or non-existence. In both cases, the thing is temporally originated.
Both options lead to the same conclusion.
5
Grasp the conclusion
Everything other than God is muhdath (preceded by non-existence). Thus God is First both as cause and temporally.
This establishes two senses of 'First'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المتكلمون القائلون بالحدوث ، فقالوا : ثبت بما ذكرنا افتقار كل ما سواه إليه وذلك الافتقار يمتنع أن يكون حال البقاء ، وإلا لزم تكوين الكائن ، وهو محال ، فوجب أن يكون إما حال الحدوث أو حال العدم . وعلى التقديرين فيلزم أن يكون كل ما سواه محدثاً . والمحدث ما سبقه عدم ، والفاعل كان موجوداً حال عدمه ، فيثبت أنه - سبحانه - كما أنه أول بمعنى التأثير والعلية فهو أيضاً أول بمعنى أنه كان موجوداً ، عندما كان كل ما سواه معدوماً .
As for the mutakallimun who affirm temporal origination, they said: It has been established by what we have mentioned that everything other than Him depends on Him, and that dependence is impossible to be in the state of permanence, for otherwise it would entail the coming-to-be of the already-existent, which is absurd. So it must be either in the state of origination or in the state of non-existence. On both suppositions, it follows that everything other than Him is temporally originated. The temporally originated is that which non-existence preceded, and the agent existed at the time of its non-existence. So it is established that He—glorified—just as He is First in the sense of effective influence and causality, He is also First in the sense that He existed when everything other than Him was non-existent.
Source pages p. 251

Commentary
This passage presents the argument of the mutakallimun (theologians) for the temporal origination (huduth) of everything other than God. They build on the previously established point that all things besides God depend on Him. This dependence, they argue, cannot be a permanent characteristic of the dependent thing while it continues to exist, because that would imply that something already existent is still coming into being (takwin al-ka'in), which is absurd. Therefore, the dependence must relate either to the moment of its origination (when it comes into existence) or to the state of its non-existence before it came to be. In either case, it follows that everything other than God is temporally originated (muhdath), meaning it is preceded by non-existence. And since the agent (God) existed at the time of its non-existence, God is First not only in the sense of being the efficient cause but also in the temporal sense of existing when all else was non-existent.

Key Terms
حدوث (temporal origination)
بقاء (permanence)
محدث (temporally originated)
عدم (non-existence)
افتقار (dependence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think dependence is a static relation that does not affect the thing's origin.
Correction: The mutakallimun insist that if dependence were permanent, it would absurdly imply the thing is still being created while existing; thus dependence must be tied to origin or non-existence, showing the thing is created.

Checkpoint
Why do the mutakallimun argue that dependence cannot be in the state of permanence?
A) Because God would not be independent
B) Because it would entail that an already-existing thing is being brought into existence, which is absurd
C) Because dependence is only possible at the moment of creation
D) Because the thing would be both eternal and originated

Key Takeaway
The mutakallimun show that the dependence of all things on God implies their temporal origination, establishing God as First both as cause and in time.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on God for every moment of your existence. How does knowing that this dependence is tied to your very origin—that you were nothing before He created you—change the way you view your daily reliance on Him? What one habit can you cultivate today to consciously acknowledge that you exist only by His will?
?
The mutakallimun argue that it would be absurd for dependence to be a permanent state, because you cannot be brought into existence after you already exist. Reflect on a time when you acted as if you were self-sufficient. How does this logical truth expose the folly of that attitude? What will you do differently when you feel self-reliant tomorrow?
?
God is First not only as the cause of all things but also in the sense that He alone existed before anything else. If nothing else existed before Him, then everything you own, your relationships, and your own life are gifts from Him. What would change in your heart if you truly internalized that you possess nothing that is not a gift given at a moment in time? Name one possession or achievement you tend to cling to and commit to seeing it as a loan from the Eternal.

Fifth Corollary: Temporal Origination of Everything Else
Proves that everything other than Allah is temporally originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that Allah's sustaining (qayyumiyya) of everything other than Himself implies that everything else is temporally originated (hadith).

Core Idea
Allah's giving existence cannot happen to an already existing thing.

This lesson presents a key proof from kalam that everything other than Allah is temporally originated (hadith). The argument relies on the impossibility of 'obtaining what is already obtained' (tahsil al-hasil). We will unpack this reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the concise style and the key terms.
The Arabic is one continuous sentence, so pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Understand the premise: Allah is the sustainer
The argument begins with the fact that Allah is the sustainer (muqawwim) of the existence of everything else. This is a given from the attribute of qayyumiyya.
3
Grasp the impossibility of tahsil al-hasil
The core of the proof: Allah's act of bringing something into existence cannot be simultaneous with its already being in existence, because that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' – a logical impossibility.
This principle is used in kalam to argue for temporal origination.
4
Conclude that everything else is originated
Therefore, the act must occur either at the time of the thing's existence (i.e., bringing it into being from non-existence) or before it exists. In both cases, the thing is temporally originated.
This covers all possibilities, leaving no room for something eternal besides Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الخامس من لوازم قيوميته : أن القائلين بالحدوث ، قالوا : لما كان مقوماً لوجود كل ما سواه ، كان كل ما سواه محدثاً ، لأن تأثيره في تكوين ذلك الغير يمتنع أن يكون حال بقائه ، لأن تحصيل الحاصل محال ، فهو إما حال وجوده ، أو حال عدمه. وعلى التقديرين فيلزم أن يكون كل ما سواه محدثاً
The fifth type of corollaries of His qayyumiyya: that those who affirm temporal origination (huduth) said: Since He is the sustainer of the existence of everything else, everything else is temporally originated, because His effect in bringing about that other cannot be while it persists, because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible, so it must be either at the time of its existence or before. In both cases, everything else is temporally originated.
Source pages p. 265

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth corollary: the temporal origination (huduth) of everything other than Allah. The argument is that Allah is the sustainer (muqawwim) of all existence. His act of bringing things into existence cannot occur while they already exist, as that would be 'obtaining what is already obtained' (tahsil al-hasil), which is impossible. Therefore, the act must happen either at the time of their coming into existence or before they exist. Hence, all are temporally originated.

Key Terms
حدوث
مقوم
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Allah's sustaining is like maintaining an already existing thing, similar to how we sustain something that already exists.
Correction: The argument shows that sustaining (ta'thir) is actually origination: it cannot be while the thing exists, because that would be obtaining what is already obtained, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why cannot Allah's act of sustaining (ta'thir) be while the other thing already exists?
A) Because sustaining implies preservation, not creation
B) Because obtaining what is already obtained is impossible
C) Because Allah is timeless
D) Because existence is a single moment

Key Takeaway
Allah's giving existence to everything necessitates that everything other than Him is temporally originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If everything other than Allah is temporally originated, including your own soul, your consciousness, your very existence, what does that mean for your sense of self-importance? Let this truth humble you: you are not a self-sustaining being. How will you remind yourself daily that you are a created, contingent being dependent on Allah's constant origination?
?
The proof hinges on the impossibility of 'obtaining what is already obtained.' In what areas of your life do you act as if you can add to what Allah has already given you—like trying to earn more than your provision, or seeking honour from others when Allah has already honoured you? What does this reveal about your trust in Allah's perfection and sufficiency?
?
This lesson shows that Allah's effect in bringing you into existence cannot be while you persist; it must be at your beginning or before. That means your very continuation is not automatic—it is a new act of creation every moment. Do you live with that awareness? If you did, how would your fear of Allah, your gratitude, and your hope change right now?

The Three Principles of Evil and Refutation of the First
The author enumerates three principles and refutes the first.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three principles that underlie the problem of evil and the beginning of the refutation of the first principle.

Core Idea
Goods and evils are not inseparable; a free agent can separate them.

The author now turns to a systematic refutation of the argument from evil. He identifies three key principles that the opponent's argument depends on. He then begins to dismantle the first principle by appealing to the nature of bodies and the free agency of God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Note the enumeration of three principles and the beginning of the refutation.
The passage is cut off, but you have enough to understand the structure.
2
Identify the three principles
Find and list the three principles. They are about goods, evils, and the necessity of bearing evils.
The first principle is the main target of refutation.
3
Understand the refutation of the first principle
The refutation denies the first principle and provides a proof based on bodies being equal in essence and their properties being assigned by a free agent.
The proof argues that the agent can give properties that cause good without evil.
4
Consider the example of fire
The author begins an example of fire as a cause of good, but the passage is cut off. Reflect on how fire can be both beneficial and harmful.
Think about how the free agent could remove the harmful aspect.

الوجه الثاني : إن كلامكم مبني على أصول : أحدها : إن هذه الخيرات الكثيرة لا يمكن تحصيلها إلا مع تلك الشرور . والثاني : إن تلك الشرور مرجوحة بالنسبة إلى تلك الخيرات. والثالث : إنه متى كان الأمر كذلك ، فإنه يجب تحمل تلك المرجوحية القليلة ، فيجب علينا أن نبحث عن كل واحد من هذه الأصول

The second aspect: Your statement is based on principles: One of them: These many goods cannot be obtained except with those evils. The second: Those evils are outweighed relative to those goods. The third: Whenever the matter is so, then it is necessary to bear that slight outweighedness. So we must investigate each of these principles. As for the first principle, which is that these goods cannot be obtained except with these evils, we say: This is denied. The proof of which is: Bodies are equal in corporeality, so the specification of each one with its particular property and particular nature must be due to the free chooser. And if the matter is so, then the powerful chooser is able to bring about those properties and natures while they are a cause for obtaining benefits, and is able to remove those properties and natures while they are a cause for obtaining harms and corruptions. So it is established by this: that it is possible to obtain those goods free from those evils. For example: Fire, if it is a cause for obtaining benefits and goods like cooking food and ripening nourishment, then its fiery nature remains in it. And when it becomes a cause…

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation. It lists three principles that the opponent's argument rests on. Then it begins refuting the first principle, which is that goods cannot be obtained without evils. The refutation uses the fact that bodies are equal in essence, so their specific properties are given by a free agent. That free agent can give properties that cause good without causing evil, or can remove properties that cause evil. The example of fire is given but cut off.

Second Refutation: Needs Arise Only After Creation
Razi argues that needs arise only after creation, so causing them is not harmful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that needs are necessary concomitants that arise only after creation, and that the blessing of existence compensates for any harm, thus refuting the objection that God harms by creating needs.

Core Idea
Needs are not eternal; they arise with existence, and the blessing outweighs the harm.

Razi presents the second way to counter an objection that God harms creatures by giving them needs. He argues that before creation, animals had no needs; needs arise only when they come into existence. If creating the animal necessarily brings needs, then that would be harmful, but Razi reminds us that the blessing of existence is measured against the harm, so the compensation principle still holds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the argument structure
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical flow: from stating that needs are concomitants, to the pre-creation state, to the condition leading to harm, and finally the compensation.
2
Understand key terms: لوازم ذوات, إضرار, نعمة
These terms are central: 'necessary concomitants of essences', 'harm', and 'blessing'. Grasping them helps follow the argument.
3
Reflect on the logical flow and compensation principle
Trace the reasoning: before existence, no needs; after creation, needs arise. If creation necessarily causes needs, it would be harmful. But the author has previously established that the blessing is equal to the harm, so the implication stands.
This shows how Razi leverages a prior principle to neutralize the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: في دفع هذا السؤال أن نقول: إن الحاجات والشهوات من لوازم ذوات الحيوانات، إلا أن ذلك الحيوان كان قبل دخوله في الوجود عدماً محضاً ونفياً صرفاً، وما كان البتة موصوفاً بشيء من الحاجات والشهوات. فلما أوجده الخالق سبحانه صار بعد دخوله في الوجود موصوفاً بهذه الحاجات وبهذه الشهوات. فلو كان إيجاد الله تعالى لذوات هذه الحيوانات مستلزماً لحصول هذه الحاجات والشهوات لكان الإيجاد والتكوين إضراراً من هذا الوجه. لكنا بينا أن النعمة مقدرة بمقدار هذه المضرة، وحينئذ يعود الإلزام المذكور.
The second aspect: in repelling this question, we say: needs and appetites are among the necessary concomitants of the essences of animals, except that that animal before entering into existence was pure non-existence and absolute nothingness, and was not at all described by any need or appetite. So when the Creator—glorified be He—brought it into existence, after entering into existence it became described by these needs and these appetites. For if God's bringing the essences of these animals into existence necessarily entailed the occurrence of these needs and appetites, then the bringing into existence and the constituting would be harmful from this aspect. But we have shown that the blessing is measured by the extent of this harm, and then the aforementioned implication returns.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage presents the second way to refute an objection. The objection likely claimed that God harms by creating needs. The response: needs are not eternal in the animal; before existence the animal had no needs. God brings the animal into existence with needs as part of its constitution. If creating the animal necessarily created the needs, then that would be harmful. But the author says they have already shown that the blessing equals the harm, so the same implication (that God compensates) applies. The argument essentially says that even if the needs are necessary accompaniments of existence, the compensation principle holds, so no net harm.

Key Terms
لوازم ذوات
إيجاد
إضرار
نعمة
مضرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Razi denies that needs are harmful at all.
Correction: Razi affirms that needs are harmful but argues that the blessing of existence compensates, so the objection is answered.

Checkpoint

A) Pure non-existence without needs
B) Potential needs in a latent state
C) An essence that included needs
D) A combination of existence and non-existence

Key Takeaway
Razi's second refutation shows that even if needs are necessary concomitants of existence, the blessing of existence compensates for any harm, so the objection against God's justice fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
If God had left you in non-existence, you would have no needs—but also no joy, no growth, no chance to know Him. Are you willing to accept the struggle of this life as the price of the immense gift of existence? What concrete step will you take this week to embrace that gift with gratitude?

Third Refutation: A Logical Division
Razi uses a logical disjunction to refute the objection about animal needs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi uses a logical division (qisma) to show that whether animal needs are essential or created, the same conclusion about divine justice follows.

Core Idea
Both possibilities lead to the same conclusion.

Razi now presents a third refutation of the objection that animal needs imply divine injustice. He uses a powerful logical tool: a disjunction (qisma) that splits the issue into two mutually exclusive possibilities. By showing that both possibilities lead back to the previous argument, he demonstrates that the objection cannot escape the earlier conclusion. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the clear logical structure.
Note the words 'إما أن يكون ... أو لا يكون' which signal the division.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage divides the issue: either needs are necessary concomitants of the animal's existence or they are not. Underline these two branches.
3
Understand the first branch
If needs are essential, then creating the animal creates harm. But the earlier response (blessing compensates) already addresses this.
The phrase 'يتم الكلام المذكور' refers to the previous compensation argument.
4
Understand the second branch
If needs are not essential, then God creates them directly. The same compensation argument applies because God also creates the means of satisfaction.
The phrase 'يعود الكلام المذكور' indicates the argument applies again.
5
Reflect on the force of the argument
Ask yourself why this division is effective. It leaves no third option, so the objection is neutralized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث: أن نقول: حصول هذه الحاجات وهذه الشهوات إما أن يكون من لوازم وجود الحيوان أو لا يكون. فإن كان الأول كان إيجاد الحيوان إيجاد لشيء يلزمه حصول هذا الضرر، فكان الإيجاد أضراراً. وحينئذ يتم الكلام المذكور. وإن كان الثاني فحينئذ يكون حصول الحاجة والشهوة في الذات ليس إلا لأجل أن خالق العالم أوجدها فيه، وحينئذ يعود الكلام المذكور.
The third aspect: we say: the occurrence of these needs and these appetites either is among the necessary concomitants of the animal's existence or is not. If it is the first, then bringing the animal into existence is bringing into existence something that necessarily entails the occurrence of this harm, so the bringing into existence would be harmful. And at this point the aforementioned discourse is complete. And if it is the second, then the occurrence of need and appetite in the essence is only because the Creator of the world brought them into existence in it, and then the aforementioned discourse returns.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a third refutation using a division (qisma). The author splits the issue: either needs are necessary concomitants of the animal's existence or they are not. In the first case, creating the animal directly creates harm, but the previous argument (that blessing compensates) already addresses that. In the second case, if needs are not essential, they are created separately by God, and again the same compensation argument applies. Thus, in both possibilities the original implication that God's blessing equals the harm stands.

Key Terms
لوازم وجود
إيجاد
ضرر
كلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Razi is asserting both possibilities are actually true.
Correction: Razi is not claiming that needs are both essential and non-essential; he is showing that whichever is the case, the same conclusion follows.

Checkpoint
In the third refutation, what is the second possibility regarding needs and appetites?
A) They are not essential to the animal's existence
B) They are impossible to occur
C) They are caused by the animal itself
D) They are illusions

Key Takeaway
Razi's third refutation shows that regardless of whether animal needs are essential or created, the compensation argument resolves the objection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a deep need or desire that causes you difficulty, do you tend to see it as an unavoidable part of who you are, or as something God placed in you? Which view leads you to trust His compensation more?
?
If you truly believed that every hardship you face will be compensated with a greater good, would you still resent the hardship? What stops you from embracing this logic in your own life?
?
Razi's argument works because it covers all possibilities. Is there a personal area of suffering where you have closed yourself off from the possibility of divine compensation? Will you consciously remind yourself today of this logical certainty?

The Third Argument: Why the Time of Creation Cannot Have Inherent Benefit
The author argues that the specific time of creation cannot have an inherent benefit, refuting a possible objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the specific time of creation cannot have an inherent benefit according to rational theology.

Core Idea
Time before creation is nothing, so it cannot have properties that cause benefits.

In the third argument against the idea that God's actions are bound by human interests, the author tackles a potential objection: that the chosen time for creation might have inherent qualities making it uniquely suitable. He systematically dismantles this by showing that time itself, before creation, is non-existent and cannot possess any essential attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: the author sets up possibilities and then refutes them.
2
Identify the three possible explanations
The author says that if the time of creation has a benefit, that benefit must be due to either the time's essence, its necessary concomitants, or something else. Locate these three divisions.
3
Examine the three arguments against benefit due to essence
Study each of the three reasons the author gives to show that a time cannot inherently possess a benefit. Understand the logic behind each: non-existence cannot have properties, necessity leads to identity, and if time could cause benefit it could cause the world.
These arguments are dense; take your time with each.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
After the three arguments, the author concludes that the claim of an inherent benefit is false. Consider how this supports the broader point that God's actions are not constrained by created interests.
This argument is part of a larger discussion on divine attributes and actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الثالثة : أن نقول : إن الخلق والتكليف يدلان على أن أفعال العباد لا يجب (٣) انطباقها على رعاية مصالح العباد ، ودفع المفاسد عنهم ، ونحن نذكر الآن أن الخلق يدل على ذلك . فنقول : العالم إذا كان محدثاً كان تخصيص الله إحداث العالم بالوقت المعين دون ما قبله وما بعده ، إما أن يكون لأجل اختصاص ذلك الوقت بمصلحة أو بدفع مفسدة - ولا يحصل ذلك المعنى على تقدير حدوث العالم في سائر الأوقـات - وإما أن لا يكون الأمر كذلك ، والأول باطل . وذلك لأن اختصاص ذلك الوقت بتلك المصلحة المخصوصة ، إما أن يكون لذاته، أو لأجل لوازم ذاته ، أولاً لهذين القسمين ، والأقسام الثلاثة باطلة أما الأول : وهو أن يكون اختصاص ذلك الوقت المعين بتلك المصلحة المعينة لذات ذلك الوقت ولعينه . فنقول : هذا باطل لوجوه : أحدها : إن هذه الأوقات عند القائلين بحدوث العالم ، كانت قبل حدوث العالم نفي محض وعدم صرف ، والعدم المحض يمتنع أن يخالف بعضه بعضاً بالخواص الذاتية ، والآثار اللازمة وثانيها : أن نقول : هذه الأوقات المختلفة ، والآثار المختلفة إما أن تكون واجبة الوجود لذواتها أو لا تكون كذلك ، والأول يقتضي امتناع العلم عليها ، فيكون الوقت السابق هو بعينه الوقت اللاحق، وذلك باطل بالضرورة، والثاني يقتضي أن تكون هذه الأوقات والأثار محدثة متعاقبة متلاحقة ، وحينئذ يعود البحث في أنه لم يوجد ذلك الوقت المشتمل على تلك المصلحة في ذلك الوقت ، ولم يحدث قبله ولا بعده ؟ وثالثها : أنه لو كان الوقت المعين له صلاحية أن يقتضي لذاته ، كونه منشئاً لتلك المصلحة المعينة ، فلم لا يجوز أن يقال : إن الوقت المعين يقتضي لذاته حدوث العالم ؟ فإن ذلك الوقت المعين لما صح أن يكون مؤثراً في هذه الآثار ، لم يمتنع أيضاً كونه مؤثراً في سائر الحوادث ، وحينئذ لا يمكننا أن نقطع بأن المؤثر في حدوث العالم، ليس هو ذلك الوقت المعين ، وأي دليل يذكر في إبطال هذا فإن يصير منتقضاً بقولهم : إن ذلك الوقت اقتضى حصول تلك الخاصية لذاته (۱) ، فيثبت بهذه الوجوه الثلاثة : فساد قول من يقول : إن ذلك الوقت المعين، اقتضى لذاته حصول تلك المصلحة .
The third argument: We say: Indeed creation (khalq) and obligation (taklīf) indicate that the actions of servants do not have to conform to the consideration of their interests (maṣāliḥ) and the repulsion of harms (mafāsid) from them. And we now mention that creation indicates that. So we say: If the world is originated (muḥdath), then God's specification of the world's origination to a particular time rather than before or after it is either due to that time being distinguished by a benefit (maṣlaḥa) or repelling a harm—and that meaning does not occur on the assumption of the world's origination at other times—or it is not the case. And the first is false. That is because the specification of that time with that specific benefit is either due to its essence (li-dhātihī), or due to the necessary concomitants of its essence, or not due to either of these two divisions; and the three divisions are false. As for the first—which is that the specification of that particular time with that particular benefit is due to the essence of that time and its very self—we say: This is false for several reasons: First: These times, according to those who affirm the origination of the world, were before the origination of the world pure negation (nafy maḥḍ) and sheer non-existence (ʿadam ṣirf), and sheer non-existence cannot be distinguished from each other by essential properties and necessary effects. Second: We say: These different times and different effects are either necessarily existent (wājib al-wujūd) in themselves or not. The first necessitates the impossibility of diversity among them, so the earlier time would be exactly the later time, which is necessarily false. The second necessitates that these times and effects are originated, successive, and consecutive, and then the inquiry returns as to why that time containing that benefit did not exist in that time and did not occur before or after it? Third: If the particular time had the capacity to necessitate by its essence its being the source of that specific benefit, why is it not permissible to say that the particular time necessitates by its essence the origination of the world? For if that particular time can be effective in these [specific] effects, it is not impossible for it to be effective in other [all] events. And then we cannot be certain that the effective cause in the origination of the world is not that particular time. And any proof mentioned to refute this would be contradicted by their statement that that time necessitated the occurrence of that property by its essence. Thus, by these three arguments, the falsehood of the statement that that particular time necessitated by its essence the occurrence of that benefit is established.
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is the third argument against the idea that God's actions are subject to human considerations of benefit and harm, focusing specifically on the creation of the world at a particular time. The author argues that if the world is created (ḥādith), then God's choice of a specific time for creation must either be because that time inherently possesses a benefit (maṣlaḥa) or because it wards off harm—or not. He refutes the possibility of an inherent benefit. He divides the possibility that the time's benefit is due to its essence, its necessary concomitants, or something else, but declares all three false. He then provides three reasons against the first division (benefit due to essence): (1) Before creation, times are pure non-existence, which cannot have distinguishing properties. (2) If times were necessary existents, they would all be identical (impossible); if contingent, they would be created and successive, leading to an infinite regress. (3) If a time could by essence cause a specific benefit, it could also cause the world itself, undermining the argument for God's exclusive creative power. Thus, the idea that any time has inherent benefit is false.

Key Terms
خلق (creation)
تكليف (obligation)
مصلحة (benefit/interest)
مفسدة (harm)
وقت معين (particular time)
نفي محض (pure negation)
عدم صرف (sheer non-existence)
واجب الوجود (necessary existent)
محدث (originated)
لازم الذات (necessary concomitant of essence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A specific time can inherently possess the quality of being beneficial or harmful.
Correction: Before creation, time is pure non-existence and cannot have any intrinsic properties.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the third argument concerning the time of creation?
A) God chose the best possible time for creation.
B) The time of creation cannot have an inherent benefit because time is non-existent before creation.
C) The time of creation was determined by the benefit it would provide.
D) The creation of the world at a specific time proves that God acts for a purpose.

Key Takeaway
Time before creation is non-existent and cannot have essential properties, so the specific time of creation cannot have inherent benefit.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that before your existence you were absolute nothing—pure non-existence—and God chose a specific time for you to be born, what does that say about your reliance on Him? Do you live as if that moment of creation was arbitrary, or do you recognize it as a deliberate act of divine will that demands your gratitude and obedience?
?
Do you ever attribute special significance to certain times (e.g., your birthday, a holiday, a 'lucky' day) as if they have power in themselves? How does this argument challenge that assumption? What would it mean to see all times as equal in their nothingness before God and only valuable because He chose them?
?
If the time of your own life is just a period of non-existence that God brought into being, what claim does that give Him over your moments? Are there times you act as if your time is your own, forgetting that even its existence is a gift? What would change if you truly believed that no moment has inherent benefit except what God puts into it?

Causality and Created Things
beginner

The First Way of Answering: Creation as Pure Beneficence
The Mu'tazila argue that creation and obligation are pure beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Mu'tazila argue that God's creation and obligation are acts of pure beneficence, and why the disbeliever's disbelief is not attributed to God.

Core Idea
God's actions are pure beneficence; evil outcomes come from human choice.

This lesson explores the first response to objections about divine justice. The Mu'tazila argue that since creation and obligation are the same for believers and disbelievers, they cannot be causes of disbelief. Instead, they are acts of pure beneficence. We'll read their argument and see how they reconcile God's goodness with human evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a premise, an inference, and a conclusion.
The passage is a summary of the Mu'tazila's position.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence that states that everything from God is pure beneficence. Note the reasoning: God gives ability and removes excuses.
3
Understand the logical flow
Ask yourself: Why does the same act (creation/obligation) lead to different outcomes? The Mu'tazila say it is not due to the act itself but to human choice.
This answers the objection that God causes disbelief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن حاصل الجواب عنه يرجع إلى طريقين :
الطريق الأول : وعليه اعتماد جمهور المعتزلة في هذه المضايق أن قالوا : الخلق والتكليف كما حصلا في حق من كفر ، فكذلك حصلا في حق من أطاع . وذلك يدل على أن الخلق والتكليف ليسا سببين الحصول الكفر والمعصية . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كل ما صدر عن الله تعالى من الخلق والتكليف فإنه محض الإحسان ، لأنه تعالى أقدر الكافر على الإيمان ، وأزاح أعذاره وعلله ، وكان هذا التكليف تعريضاً منه تعالى للكافر لأعظم المنافع . أما وقوعه في الكفر فليس ذلك من الله تعالى ، بل كان من قبل نفسه . فلهذا
Know that the summary of the answer to it goes back to two ways: The first way: Upon it relies the majority of the Mu'tazila in these difficulties, as they said: Creation and obligation, just as they occurred with respect to the one who disbelieved, likewise they occurred with respect to the one who obeyed. And that indicates that creation and obligation are not causes for the occurrence of disbelief and disobedience. When this is established, we say: Everything that issues from God Almighty of creation and obligation is pure beneficence, because He Almighty gave the disbeliever the ability to believe, and removed his excuses and pretexts, and this obligation was an exposure from Him Almighty for the disbeliever to the greatest benefits. As for his falling into disbelief, that is not from God Almighty, but was from himself. Therefore...
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents a summary of the answer to the previous objection, dividing it into two ways. Only the first way is given here. The first way is attributed to the majority of the Mu'tazila. They argue that creation and obligation are not causes of disbelief and sin because they occur equally for believers and disbelievers. Since the same act (creation and obligation) produces different outcomes (disbelief in some, obedience in others), the act itself cannot be the cause of the evil outcome. This having been established, the author explains that everything God does in creating and obligating is pure beneficence (محض الإحسان). The reason: God gives the disbeliever the ability to believe, removes his excuses, and exposes him to the greatest benefits through obligation. The disbeliever's fall into disbelief, therefore, is not from God but from the disbeliever himself. The passage ends abruptly with 'فلهذا' (therefore), suggesting the second way and possibly a conclusion are missing from this window.

Key Terms
محض الإحسان (pure beneficence)
أزاح أعذاره (removed his excuses)
تعريضاً (exposure)
سببين (causes)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if God's act is the same for all, then the outcome must be from God.
Correction: The argument shows that the same act can have different outcomes due to human choice, not because of a deficiency in the act itself.

Checkpoint
According to the first way of answering, why is creation and obligation considered pure beneficence?
A) Because God creates only believers.
B) Because God gives the disbeliever the ability to believe and removes his excuses.
C) Because the disbeliever's disbelief is caused by God.
D) Because the benefits of obligation outweigh all harms.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defend God's justice by arguing that creation and obligation are pure beneficence, and human disbelief is self-caused.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see someone who rejects faith, do you truly believe that God's act of creating them was still pure beneficence? How does this belief shape your attitude toward them—do you see them as a victim of their own choice or of divine caprice? Will you commit to treating them as someone fully responsible for their choices, as the Mu'tazila argue?
?
The lesson says that evil outcomes come from yourself, not from God. Think of a recent sin or failure you blamed on circumstances, upbringing, or even 'God's will'. Are you ready to admit that the real source was your own misuse of the ability God gave you? What would change if you owned that fully starting today?
?
God removed all excuses before obligating you. What excuses do you still hide behind to avoid obedience—'I'm too busy', 'I'm not ready', 'It's too hard'? If those excuses are invalid, what concrete step will you take today to respond to God's invitation without delay?

Causality and Created Things

The First Way: Obligation is Pure Beneficence
Mu'tazilite answer: obligation is not a cause; it is pure beneficence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first Mu'tazili response to the objection: that creation and obligation are not causes of disbelief, and that obligation is pure beneficence from God.

Core Idea
Creation and obligation are pure beneficence; disbelief is from the self.

The Mu'tazila answer the objection by arguing that obligation applies to both believers and disbelievers alike, so it cannot be the cause of disbelief. They affirm that God's act is pure beneficence and that the disbeliever is responsible for his own failure. Let's explore their reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the first way
Read the Arabic text from 'الطريق الأول' to 'وهذا هو الطريق الذي عليه يعولون'. Then read the translation to grasp the argument.
Notice how the Mu'tazila first establish a premise about creation and obligation being common to both believers and disbelievers.
2
Understand the key claim
Identify the core claim: creation and obligation are not the causes of disbelief or disobedience, because they occur for both groups.
3
Grasp the argument of pure beneficence
See how they argue that every act from God is pure beneficence: He enables the disbeliever to believe, removes excuses, and exposes him to the greatest benefits.
4
Note the conclusion
The conclusion is that obligating one whom God knows will disbelieve is not ugly, because the disbeliever's failure is from himself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن حاصل الجواب عنه يرجع إلى طريقين :
الطريق الأول : وعليه اعتماد جمهور المعتزلة في هذه المضايق أن قالوا : الخلق والتكليف كما حصلا في حق من كفر ، فكذلك حصلا في حق من أطاع . وذلك يدل على أن الخلق والتكليف ليسا سببين للحصول الكفر والمعصية . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كل ما صدر عن الله تعالى من الخلق والتكليف فإنه محض الإحسان ، لأنه تعالى أقدر الكافر على الإيمان ، وأزاح أعذاره وعلله ، وكان هذا التكليف تعريضاً منه تعالى للكافر لأعظم المنافع . أما وقوعه في الكفر فليس ذلك من الله تعالى ، بل كان من قبل نفسه . فلهذا السبب قلنا : التكليف من علم الله منه أنه يكفر ليس بقبيح . وهذا هو الطريق الذي عليه يعولون
والطريق الثاني : طريقة هشام بن الحكم. وهي : أن هذه الإشكالات إنما تلزم لو قلنا : إنه كان عالماً في الأزل بأن فلاناً يكفر ، فإذا لم نقل بذلك بل قلنا : إنه تعالى لا يعلم هذه الجزئيات إلا عند وقوعها اندفع الإشكال
And know that the summary of the answer to it [the objection] goes back to two ways:
The first way: and upon it is the reliance of the majority of the Mu'tazila in these difficulties, namely that they said: creation and obligation as they occurred in the right of the one who disbelieved, likewise they occurred in the right of the one who obeyed. And that indicates that creation and obligation are not the two causes for the occurrence of disbelief and disobedience. When this is established we say: everything that issues from God Almighty of creation and obligation is pure beneficence, because He the Exalted enabled the disbeliever to believe, and removed his excuses and pretexts, and this obligation was an exposure from Him the Exalted for the disbeliever to the greatest benefits. As for his falling into disbelief, that is not from God Almighty, rather it was from himself. For this reason we say: the obligation of one whom God knows will disbelieve is not ugly. And this is the way upon which they rely.
And the second way: the way of Hisham ibn al-Hakam. It is: that these problems only follow if we say: He was eternally knowing that so-and-so will disbelieve. So if we do not say that, rather we say: He the Exalted does not know these particulars except at the time of their occurrence, then the problem is repelled.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct ways to answer the objection that obligating a person God knows will disbelieve is unjust. The first way, relied upon by most Mu'tazila, argues that creation and obligation are not the causes of disbelief or disobedience because they occur equally for believers and disbelievers. Since God enables the disbeliever to believe and removes excuses, the obligation is pure beneficence; the disbeliever's failure is from himself. Therefore, the obligation is not ugly. The second way, attributed to Hisham ibn al-Hakam, takes a more radical approach: it denies that God has eternal knowledge of particular future events (such as who will disbelieve). If God only knows these particulars at the time they occur, then the objection based on pre-known outcome dissolves. This second way is presented without endorsement, but as a known position.

Key Terms
الخلق والتكليف
محض الإحسان
تعريض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first way denies God's foreknowledge of who will disbelieve.
Correction: The Mu'tazila do not deny foreknowledge; they affirm that God knows who will disbelieve, but they argue that obligation is not a cause of that disbelief. They reject the idea that foreknowledge makes the obligation ugly.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the first way?
A) God does not know who will disbelieve.
B) Creation and obligation are not causes of disbelief because they occur for both believers and disbelievers.
C) God creates disbelief directly in the disbeliever.
D) Obligation is ugly if God knows the outcome.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazila defend divine justice by arguing that obligation is not a cause of disbelief and that God's beneficence is absolute; disbelief stems from the individual's own choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you disobey a clear command of Allah, do you secretly blame your circumstances or upbringing? What would it mean for you to say, like the Mu'tazila, that 'it was from yourself'? Will you own your next sin without shifting responsibility?
?
God has enabled you to believe and removed your excuses. Do you truly live as if every obligation from Him is pure beneficence, even the ones that are hard? What command have you been resenting instead of receiving as a gift?
?
If you knew that someone whom God knows will disbelieve still received mercy and exposure to faith, how does that reshape your view of God's justice? Are you willing to trust that God is perfectly just even when you don't see the full story?
