The Second Way: Denying Foreknowledge of Particulars
Hisham ibn al-Hakam's denial of eternal knowledge of particulars.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Hisham ibn al-Hakam's radical solution: denying that God has eternal knowledge of particular future events, thereby dissolving the objection.

Core Idea
If God does not know particulars beforehand, the objection vanishes.

Hisham ibn al-Hakam offers a more controversial answer: the objection only arises if we assume God eternally knows that a particular person will disbelieve. If we deny that God knows these particulars before they happen, the problem disappears. This view, however, is a minority position with significant theological implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second way
Read the Arabic from 'والطريق الثاني' to the end. Then read the translation to understand the conditional argument.
Notice the structure: 'if we say X, then problem; if we deny X, then solved'.
2
Understand the conditional logic
The objection only follows if God has eternal knowledge of who will disbelieve. If that knowledge is denied, the objection does not arise.
3
Grasp the solution
The solution is to say that God does not know these particulars except at the time they occur. This eliminates the basis for the objection.
4
Reflect on implications
Consider why this view is controversial: it limits God's foreknowledge, which many theologians consider essential to divine omniscience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن حاصل الجواب عنه يرجع إلى طريقين :
الطريق الأول : وعليه اعتماد جمهور المعتزلة في هذه المضايق أن قالوا : الخلق والتكليف كما حصلا في حق من كفر ، فكذلك حصلا في حق من أطاع . وذلك يدل على أن الخلق والتكليف ليسا سببين للحصول الكفر والمعصية . إذا ثبت هذا فنقول : كل ما صدر عن الله تعالى من الخلق والتكليف فإنه محض الإحسان ، لأنه تعالى أقدر الكافر على الإيمان ، وأزاح أعذاره وعلله ، وكان هذا التكليف تعريضاً منه تعالى للكافر لأعظم المنافع . أما وقوعه في الكفر فليس ذلك من الله تعالى ، بل كان من قبل نفسه . فلهذا السبب قلنا : التكليف من علم الله منه أنه يكفر ليس بقبيح . وهذا هو الطريق الذي عليه يعولون

And know that the summary of the answer to it [the objection] goes back to two ways:
The first way: and upon it is the reliance of the majority of the Mu'tazila in these difficulties, namely that they said: creation and obligation as they occurred in the right of the one who disbelieved, likewise they occurred in the right of the one who obeyed. And that indicates that creation and obligation are not the two causes for the occurrence of disbelief and disobedience. When this is established we say: everything that issues from God Almighty of creation and obligation is pure beneficence, because He the Exalted enabled the disbeliever to believe, and removed his excuses and pretexts, and this obligation was an exposure from Him the Exalted for the disbeliever to the greatest benefits. As for his falling into disbelief, that is not from God Almighty, rather it was from himself. For this reason we say: the obligation of one whom God knows will disbelieve is not ugly. And this is the way upon which they rely.
And the second way: the way of Hisham ibn al-Hakam. It is: that these problems only follow if we say: He was eternally knowing that so-and-so will disbelieve. So if we do not say that, rather we say: He the Exalted does not know these particulars except at the time of their occurrence, then the problem is repelled.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct ways to answer the objection that obligating a person God knows will disbelieve is unjust. The first way, relied upon by most Mu'tazila, argues that creation and obligation are not the causes of disbelief or disobedience because they occur equally for believers and disbelievers. Since God enables the disbeliever to believe and removes excuses, the obligation is pure beneficence; the disbeliever's failure is from himself. Therefore, the obligation is not ugly. If God only knows these particulars at the time they occur, then the objection based on pre-known outcome dissolves. This second way is presented without endorsement, but as a known position.

Key Terms
هشام بن الحكم
علم الله بالجزئيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This view is the standard Ash'ari position.
Correction: Hisham's view is a minority Mu'tazili position that denies God's eternal knowledge of particulars; Ash'aris and most Muslims affirm that God knows all things eternally.

Checkpoint
According to the second way, what condition must be met for the objection to arise?
A) God creates disbelief.
B) God does not know the future.
C) God is eternally knowing that a particular person will disbelieve.
D) The person is forced to disbelieve.

Key Takeaway
Hisham ibn al-Hakam's solution avoids the objection by denying God's eternal knowledge of particulars, but this comes at the cost of limiting divine omniscience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that God did not know your future sins until you committed them, would that make you more or less careful about sinning? What does your answer reveal about the state of your heart toward God's knowledge?
?
Hisham's view tries to protect God from the charge of injustice, but at the cost of limiting His foreknowledge. Are you willing to accept a view that reduces God's majesty to make yourself feel more comfortable about His justice?
?
When you sin, do you ever think, 'God already knew I would do this, so it's not my fault'? How does this passage challenge that excuse?

The Ninth Proof: God Does Not Act for a Purpose
The author presents the ninth proof against God acting for a purpose, using a dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical dilemma that theists use to argue that God does not act for any external purpose.

Core Idea
An eternal purpose forces an eternal act; a temporal purpose leads to infinite regress.

In this lesson, we examine a classic kalam argument against the idea that God acts for a purpose. The argument uses a simple but powerful dilemma: any purpose must be either eternal or temporal, and both options lead to rational impossibility. This refines our understanding of divine action as self-sufficient and not dependent on external goals.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to get the meaning.
Focus on the two conditions: eternal or temporal.
2
Understand the two horns of the dilemma
Identify how each possibility leads to a contradiction or impossibility. Notice the logical structure: if-then reasoning.
The dilemma is exhaustive: purpose is either eternal or temporal.
3
Grasp why infinite regress is impossible
Reflect on the concept of tasalsul (infinite regress) and why it is considered impossible in kalam.
Infinite regress is rejected because it would prevent any purpose from ever being realized.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة التاسعة : إنه تعالى لو فعل فعلاً لغرض ، لكان ذلك الغرض ، إن كان قديماً لزم من قدمه قدم الفعل ، وإن كان حادثاً كان إحداثه لغرض آخر ، ولزم التسلسل وأنه محال .
The ninth proof: If He, exalted, performed an act for a purpose, then that purpose, if it were eternal, the eternity of the act would follow from its eternity; and if it were temporal, then its bringing into existence would be for another purpose, and an infinite regress would follow, and that is impossible.
Source pages p. 333

Commentary
This passage presents the ninth proof (al-hujja al-tasi'a) against the idea that God acts for a purpose. The author argues by way of a dilemma: any purpose for which God acts must be either eternal (qadim) or temporal (hadith). If the purpose is eternal, then the act itself would have to be eternal, because an eternal purpose cannot be temporally fulfilled—this is false since the world is temporal. If the purpose is temporal, then its fulfillment (i.e., bringing it into existence) would itself be an act requiring another purpose, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, God does not act for any purpose.

Key Terms
حجة
غرض
قديم
حادث
تسلسل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's actions are futile or random.
Correction: The argument rejects external purposes, not intrinsic wisdom; God's actions are not driven by need.

Checkpoint
According to the ninth proof, why is it impossible for God to act for a temporal purpose?
A) Because temporal purposes are inherently evil.
B) Because it would require an infinite chain of purposes.
C) Because God cannot create temporal things.
D) Because purposes only apply to human actions.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of both eternal and temporal purposes shows that God's actions are not motivated by external goals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that God cannot act for a purpose external to Himself. If your worship is for a purpose like reward or paradise, does that put you in the very position the proof refutes? How would you purify your intention right now to align with this truth?
?
You often ask God for things in dua, expecting Him to act to fulfill your needs. If God's actions are not motivated by purposes, does that mean your requests are meaningless? What would it look like to pray with the awareness that God's will is not moved by your desires, yet He still commands you to ask? Can you accept that?
?
This argument challenges the idea that the world was created for your sake. If your existence is not for a purpose that benefits God, then why are you here? What does this lesson force you to reconsider about your own purpose in life—are you seeking something for yourself or only what God wills?

The Eleventh Proof: God's Creation Precludes Evil in Him
The author asserts that since God creates all things, nothing from Him can be evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that since God creates all things, nothing from Him can be evil, and this is an overwhelming proof in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
If God creates everything, then nothing from Him can be evil.

This lesson introduces the eleventh proof from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah. The author asserts that since God is the sole creator of all originated things, it follows necessarily that no action from God can be considered evil. This argument is presented as an overwhelming proof, laying the foundation for discussions on divine justice and predestination.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage carefully and note its structure: the author states the proof, refers to future evidence, and concludes.
2
Understand the premise
Reflect on the key terms: jabr (compulsion), qadar (predestination), khaliq (creator), muhdathat (originated things), and yuqbah (evil). The proof relies on the absolute creative power of God.
3
Grasp the conclusion
According to the author, since God creates everything, it is impossible for anything from Him to be evil in the rational sense. Consider how this challenges human moral categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحجة الحادية عشر : إنا سنقيم الدلائل القاهرة في باب الجبر والقدر (۳) ، على أن الخالق لجميع المحدثات هو الله تعالى . وعلى هذا التقدير فإنه يجب القطع بأنه لا يقبح من الله تعالى شيء . وهذا برهان قاهر : والله أعلم .
The eleventh proof: We will establish the overwhelming proofs in the chapter of compulsion and predestination that the creator of all originated things is God, exalted. And upon this supposition, it must be categorically affirmed that nothing is evil (la yuqbah) from God, exalted. This is an overwhelming proof. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 334

Commentary
This passage presents the eleventh proof (al-hujja al-hadiya 'ashara). The author refers to the forthcoming proofs in the discussion of jabr (compulsion) and qadar (predestination) which demonstrate that God is the creator of all temporal things (muhdathat). Since God creates everything, no action can be attributed to any other agent independently. Therefore, anything that occurs is from God. Given this, it is impossible for anything from God to be evil (qabih) in the rational sense, because evil would imply a deficiency in the agent, and God is perfect. Thus, the concept of rational evil does not apply to God. The author calls this an 'overwhelming proof' (burhan qahir) and ends with 'wa Allah a'lam' (And God knows best).

Key Terms
جبر
قدر
خالق
محدثات
يقبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof suggests that God may be the author of evil.
Correction: The proof teaches precisely the opposite: because God creates everything, nothing from Him qualifies as evil in any rational sense. Evil is a category that applies to deficient agents, and God is perfect.

Checkpoint
What does the eleventh proof say about evil from God?
A) It is possible but rare.
B) It is impossible because God creates everything.
C) It is a matter of interpretation.
D) It only applies to human actions.

Key Takeaway
Because God is the sole creator of all things, nothing from Him can be evil, making this an overwhelming proof of divine justice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a time when you experienced deep pain or injustice. Did you ever feel that something from God was evil or unfair? How does this lesson's assertion that 'nothing is evil from God' confront that feeling? What specific thought or habit can you adopt to remind yourself of this truth in moments of trial?
?
The author calls this proof 'overwhelming'. Ask yourself: Do I truly live as though I believe that everything that comes from God is good, even when I cannot see it? If not, what area of your life—your health, your relationships, your struggles—reveals a hidden doubt about God's perfection? What are you willing to change today to align your heart with this truth?
?
If you really accept that God is the sole creator of all actions, including your own mistakes, does that make you less responsible or more accountable? The lesson teaches that God's actions are beyond human categories of evil. How does that affect your sense of repentance? Write down one specific sin you have blamed on circumstances or others, and recommit to turning to God alone in repentance.

The First Objection: Action and Motive Dilemma
Al-Razi presents a conditional dilemma: action depends on motive or not; both horns yield unacceptable consequences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first reciprocal objection: how the opponent's view on action and motive leads to either determinism or undermines the evidential value of miracles.

Core Idea
The opponent's position is self-destructing: it leads either to determinism or to the collapse of prophethood.

Al-Razi now turns the tables on the opponent. He shows that the opponent's own criteria for action and motive generate an inescapable dilemma. Either actions depend on motives, leading to determinism (which the opponent rejects), or they do not, making miracles meaningless and destroying all prophethood. This lesson unpacks that powerful argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the English translation. Absorb the flow of the argument before analysis.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then'.
2
Identify the two horns
Pinpoint the two conditional scenarios: actions depend on motives, or they do not. For each, note the consequence.
The first horn leads to determinism; the second undermines miracles.
3
Reflect on the objection's strength
Consider why al-Razi calls this objection 'strong'. How does it force the opponent into an impossible choice?
The opponent's own principle is used against them.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فنقول: هذا الإلزام وارد عليكم أيضاً من وجهين:
الأول: وهو أن الفعل إما أن يتوقف على الداعي، أو لا يتوقف عليه، فإن توقف عليه، لزم أن يتوقف صدور الفعل عن العبد على حصول الداعي ويلزم منه القول بالجبر، وأن يكون فعل العبد فعلا الله (وعلى هذا التقدير فيكون الخير والشر من الله) (١) وعلى هذا التقدير فيلزمكم ما الزمتموه علينا، وإما أن يتوقف الفعل على الدواعي، فحينئذ يصح أن يخلق الله المعجزة (لا لشيء من الدواعي أصلاً. وحينئذ لا تدل المعجزة) (٢) على الصدق، وحينئذ يلزم القدح في نبوة جميع الأنبياء والرسل. وهذا الإلزام قوي.
We say: This objection is also incumbent upon you from two aspects: The first: That the action either depends on the motive (al-dāʿī) or does not depend on it. If it depends on it, then it follows that the occurrence of the action from the servant depends on the attainment of the motive, and from this follows the statement of determinism (al-jabr), and that the action of the servant is the action of God. On this supposition, good and evil are from God. And on this supposition, what you imposed on us becomes incumbent upon you. And if the action does not depend on motives, then it is possible for God to create a miracle without any motive at all. And then the miracle does not indicate truthfulness, and then corruption is necessitated in the prophethood of all prophets and messengers. And this objection is strong.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the first of two objections that al-Razi levels against the opponent. The argument is a conditional dilemma: either actions depend on motives or they do not. If they depend on motives, then the servant's action is determined by the motive, leading to determinism (jabr) and making God the creator of both good and evil—a consequence the opponent likely rejects. If actions do not depend on motives, then a miracle could be created without any purpose, so it would not serve as evidence of the prophet's truthfulness, thereby undermining all prophethood. Thus, whichever horn the opponent chooses, their position is refuted.

Key Terms
الداعي (al-dāʿī)
الجبر (al-jabr)
المعجزة (al-muʿjiza)
الصدق (al-ṣidq)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The opponent's objection only applies to the other side, not to themselves.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that the same reasoning boomerangs: the opponent's own criteria lead to either determinism or the collapse of prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the first objection, what follows if actions depend on motives?
A) Miracles become impossible.
B) Determinism (jabr) is necessitated.
C) The servant gains free will.
D) Revelation is invalidated.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi's first reciprocal objection forces the opponent into an impossible choice: either accept determinism or admit that miracles cannot prove prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Razi's dilemma forces you to choose: either your actions depend on motives (making you responsible) or they don't (making them arbitrary). In your own life, have you ever excused a sin by thinking you had no choice? Which horn of the dilemma do you actually live by, and are you ready to face the theological consequences of that choice before Allah?
?
The second horn shows that without a purpose, a miracle cannot prove truth. When you witness blessings or trials in your life, do you see them as purposeful signs from God, or do you treat them as random events? This lesson demands that you align your heart with the belief that Allah acts with wisdom. Can you point to a recent event in your life where you doubted Allah's purpose? How does that doubt affect your faith in His signs?

The Second Objection: Creator of Actions and Miracles
Al-Razi presents a dilemma on the creator of actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Razi uses a dilemma about the creator of human actions to challenge the evidential value of miracles in proving prophethood.

Core Idea
Whichever side you pick, the objection backfires.

Al-Razi continues his reciprocal objections by focusing on who creates human actions. He presents a dilemma: if God creates all actions, then both good and evil are from God, turning the opponent's accusation against themselves. If the servant creates his own actions, then the servant's power is genuinely productive, which would make it possible for miracles to be created by other than God, undermining their role as proof of prophethood. This passage sets up the need to refute Mu'tazilite arguments about exclusive divine creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Try to identify the two horns of the dilemma and their immediate consequences.
Focus on the two 'if' clauses: 'إذا كان هو الله تعالى' and 'وإن كان هو العبد'.
2
Examine the consequences of each horn
For the first horn (God creates all actions): good and evil both come from God, so the opponent's objection that evil must be traced back to God applies to them as well. For the second horn (servant creates): the servant's power is genuinely productive, so miracles could be created by other than God, making them invalid as proofs of prophethood.
Notice how Al-Razi leverages the opponent's own premises against them.
3
Understand the cross-reference
Al-Razi mentions that a full refutation requires showing the Mu'tazila's arguments that only God can create bodies and life are weak. He promises to address this later in the chapter on determinism (al-jabr wa al-qadar).
This indicates that the present objection anticipates a deeper theological discussion.
4
Reflect on the dialectical force
Consider how this dilemma forces the opponent into an equally uncomfortable position as the one they tried to impose on Al-Razi. The objection is reciprocal.
5
Summarize the lesson in your own words
Write a brief summary of the dilemma and its consequences. Ensure you can explain why the first horn applies to the opponent and why the second horn undermines miracles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والوجه الثاني: أن نقول: خالق أفعال العباد إما أن يكون هو الله تعالى، أو العبد. فإن كان هو الله تعالى فحينئذ يكون الخير والشر كله من الله تعالى، وحينئذ يلزمهم ما التزموه علينا. وإن كان هو العبد فحينئذ تكون قدرة العبد صالحة للإيجاد، وإذا ثبت هذا فحينئذ يحتمل في جميع المعجزات أن يكون خالقها غير الله، وعلى هذا التقدير فلا تكون المعجزة دليلا على أن الله خصه بالتصديق. وتمام هذا الكلام، إنما يتم ببيان أن دلائل المعتزلة في أن غير الله لا يقدر على خلق الجسم والحياة ضعيفة، وسيأتي بيان ذلك في باب الجبر والقدر، فيثبت أن الإلزام الذي وردوه علينا، هو بعينه لازم عليهم.
The second aspect: We say: The creator of the actions of servants is either God or the servant. If it is God, then all good and evil are from God, and then what they imposed on us becomes incumbent upon them. If it is the servant, then the servant's power is capable of bringing into existence. And if this is established, then it is possible for all miracles that their creator is other than God. On this supposition, the miracle does not serve as evidence that God singled him out for verification. The complete discussion on this is only completed by showing that the proofs of the Mu'tazila that other than God is not capable of creating bodies and life are weak. This will come later in the chapter on determinism and predestination (al-jabr wa al-qadar). Thus it is established that the objection they directed at us is exactly the same as what is incumbent upon them.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, again a dilemma about who creates actions. If God creates actions, then the opponent's accusation about evil being from God applies to them. If the servant creates, then the servant's power is truly productive, which would mean that miracles could be created by someone other than God—undermining their evidential value for prophethood. Al-Razi notes that a full refutation requires showing the weakness of Mu'tazilite arguments that only God can create bodies and life, which he promises to address later in the chapter on predestination. Thus, the objection supposedly directed at him is equally binding on the opponent.

Key Terms
خالق أفعال العباد (khāliq afʿāl al-ʿibād)
قدرة العبد (qudrat al-ʿabd)
الإيجاد (al-ījād)
المعجزات (al-muʿjizāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That if God creates all actions, then only the other side (the Ash'arites) has the problem of attributing evil to God.
Correction: Al-Razi shows that if God creates all actions, the same problem applies to the Mu'tazila as well, because they also believe God is the creator of all things — the dilemma is reciprocal.

Checkpoint
According to the second objection, what is a consequence if the servant is the creator of his own actions?
A) The servant becomes a prophet.
B) Miracles could be created by someone other than God.
C) Evil cannot exist in the world.
D) God must be unjust.

Key Takeaway
The creator-of-actions dilemma forces the Mu'tazila into the same difficulty they accuse Muslims of: either God creates evil or miracles lose their evidential force.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you believe it was created by God or by you? How does the dilemma in this passage challenge you to take full responsibility for your actions?
?
Suppose you discovered that a miracle you believed in could have been performed by a human being. Would your trust in the prophet who performed it waver? Why or why not?
?
Which horn of the dilemma do you find yourself leaning toward? What does that choice reveal about your understanding of God's justice and your own accountability? How will you live differently today because of this tension?
